1 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mitchell v. Superior Court


Crim. No. 24790


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 1230; 783 P.2d 731; 265 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 2106



December 28, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of real party in interest for a rehearing was denied February 1, 1990. Panelli, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 772328, Frank W. Shaw, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of contempt as it applies to petitioner Stanich is annulled, and the superior court is directed to dismiss the proceeding as to him.

The judgment of contempt is annulled as to petitioners James Mitchell and Artie Mitchell, and the matter remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, owners and manager of an adult entertainment business, appealed from the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which found petitioners to be in violation of an injunction and imposed substantial fines and six-month jail terms upon them. Petitioners were not afforded a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were the owners and manager of a business offering filmed and live adult entertainment. An injunction had earlier been issued under the Red Light Abatement Law (RLAL), Cal. Penal Code §11225 et seq, enjoining petitioner owners and their agents, servants, employees and representatives from allowing the occurrence, continuance, or reoccurrence of acts of lewdness or prostitution upon the premises. Later, after a seven-day evidentiary hearing, the trial court found petitioners guilty of 62 acts in contempt of the preliminary injunction, fined petitioner owners $ 62,000, fined petitioner manager $ 6,200, and sentenced all three to six months in jail. The court annulled the judgment, holding that, because a proceeding under Cal. Penal Code § 11229 carried a maximum penalty comparable to a misdemeanor, the right to a jury trial afforded by Cal. Const. art. I, § 16 applied to such a proceeding. The court held that, insofar as an injunction authorized under the RLAL was an injunction to abate a nuisance, persons who violate an injunction under the RLAL could only be charged with only one count of contempt for each separate day they permitted the enjoined nuisance to continue.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the judgment of contempt, holding that, because petitioners, owners and manager of an adult entertainment business, faced a potential sentence of six months' imprisonment and a $ 1,000 fine per violation, a sentence equivalent to that of many misdemeanors, petitioners were guaranteed a trial by jury. The court held that petitioners could only be charged one count per each day that they were in contempt of court.

 

2 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Jackson


No. S004615


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 1170; 783 P.2d 211; 264 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 2100



December 18, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied February 15, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-530714, Loren Miller, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, California, which entered judgment upon a jury verdict of death imposed for first-degree murder. The jury found true the special circumstance allegation that the victim was a peace officer who was intentionally killed while engaged in the performance of his duties and that defendant knew or should have known that the victim was a peace officer in the performance of his duties.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death for the first-degree murder of a peace officer killed while engaged in the performance of his duties. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed. The court found no error in permitting the introduction of statements defendant made to a police detective on the evening of the killing and at noon on the following day. The evidence was relevant to show defendant's state of mind following the homicide because the prosecution correctly anticipated the defense claims of unconsciousness due to drugs and of amnesia. Defense counsel performed with reasonable competence. The court rejected defendant's claims of prosecutorial misconduct and faulty jury instructions. The court concluded that the evidence, viewed in the light most favorable to the prosecution, supported a finding of premeditated and deliberate first-degree murder. Defendant's contention that it was prejudicial error to try a black defendant with an all white jury was patently unmeritorious. Similarly, the court rejected defendant's constitutional challenge to the death penalty as cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: Judgment of death imposed for first-degree murder was affirmed. Defendant had not established that there was an earlier interrogation necessitating a waiver of Miranda rights, that there was prosecutorial misconduct, that the jury instructions were inadequate, or that the evidence was insufficient. Additionally, there was no constitutional violation in the removal of the only black juror from the jury panel.

 

3 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hippard v. State Bar


No. S008378


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 1084; 782 P.2d 1140; 264 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 2095



December 11, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied March 1, 1990. Broussard, J., and Panelli, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for reinstatement is denied. This is effective upon finality of this decision in this court. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rule 24(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review from respondent State Bar of California's denial of petitioner's request for reinstatement to the practice of law. The court considered whether petitioner's discharge in bankruptcy of indebtedness to clients arising from misconduct precluded respondent from considering petitioner's efforts to make restitution as an indicator of rehabilitation.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney sought review from respondent State Bar of California's denial of petitioner's request for reinstatement to the practice of law. The court denied petitioner's request for reinstatement. Petitioner's discharge in bankruptcy of indebtedness to clients arising from misconduct did not preclude respondent from considering petitioner's efforts to make restitution as an indicator of rehabilitation. The court concluded that petitioner did not meet his burden of demonstrating rehabilitation. Petitioner failed to prove either an inability to make restitution or an objectively verifiable effort to make restitution reasonably related to his ability to pay. Because petitioner had not shown an inability to pay, his assertion that respondent violated due process by penalizing him for his impecunious position was not supported by the record. Petitioner's noncompliance with Cal. R. Ct. 955 was a significant consideration and that petitioner's behavior did not amount to merely a "technical" noncompliance with the rule. The court held that it would be improper to grant reinstatement subject to petitioner thereafter making the requisite showing of restitution.


OUTCOME: Petitioner attorney's request for reinstatement to the practice of law was denied. Petitioner did not meet his burden of demonstrating rehabilitation. The court indicated that petitioner could reapply for reinstatement after the formulation of a plan of restitution and adherence to the plan for a meaningful period of time, or upon a specific showing of inability to make restitution.

 

4 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Kloepfer v. Commission on Judicial Performance


No. S004893


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 826; 782 P.2d 239; 264 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 2085; 89 A.L.R.4th 235



November 27, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied January 25, 1990. Kaufman, J., did not participate therein.



DISPOSITION: We order that Judge Kenneth Lynn Kloepfer, judge of the San Bernardino Municipal Court District, San Bernardino County, be removed from office. He shall, however, if otherwise qualified, be permitted to resume the practice of law (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 18, subd. (d)) on condition that he pass the Professional Responsibility Examination. This order is effective upon the finality of this decision in this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Cal. Const., art. VI, § 18(c), and Cal. R. Ct. 919(b), petitioner judge challenged the recommendation of respondent Commission on Judicial Performance (California) that he be removed from office. Petitioner alleged that respondent's bias toward him violated his due process rights, and undue delay in the commencement of the disciplinary hearing prejudiced his case and violated his right to a speedy resolution of the hearings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner judge was brought before respondent Commission on Judicial Performance to face disciplinary action. Respondent found petitioner to have committed five acts of wilful misconduct, and 20 acts of conduct prejudicial to the administration of justice that brings the judicial office into disrepute and recommended his removal from office. Petitioner challenged respondent's findings and alleged both prejudicial delay and denial of due process. Examining petitioner's claims, the court found no evidence of prejudicial bias and held that existing administrative procedures and mandatory judicial review over respondent's proceedings provided adequate protection to petitioner's due process rights. On petitioner's allegations of prejudicial delay, the court held that Cal. R. Ct. 905(a) did not create a right to notice similar to a statutory right to speedy trial and that there was no evidence to support petitioner's claims of prejudice due to delay. Examining the administrative record, the court held that respondent's recommendation of dismissal was reasonable and supported by sufficient evidence and ordered petitioner's removal from office.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that existing administrative procedures and judicial review adequately protected petitioner judge from respondent Commission on Judicial Performance's alleged bias against him, and there was no prejudice to petitioner based on delays in the disciplinary proceedings against him. The recommendation of respondent, to remove petitioner from judicial office, was adopted by the court in all respects.

 

5 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Williams v. Superior Court


No. S002131, L.A. 32206


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 736; 781 P.2d 537; 263 Cal. Rptr. 503; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 2103



October 31, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Time for Granting or Denying Review Extended Williams (Charles E.) on H.C., 2006 Cal. LEXIS 3067 (Cal., Jan. 24, 2006)
Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by In re Williams, 2014 Cal. App. Unpub. LEXIS 2528 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., Apr. 9, 2014)



DISPOSITION:  The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed. The Court of Appeal is directed to remand the cause to the West Superior Court District for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the California Court of Appeal denying his petition for a writ of mandate after respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denied defendant's motion to quash the venire of his criminal trial on the ground that black persons on jury panels in the West District of Los Angeles County were unconstitutionally underrepresentative of the black population of Los Angeles County.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion in his criminal trial to quash venire on grounds that black persons on jury panels in the West District of Los Angeles County were unconstitutionally underrepresentative of the black population of Los Angeles County in violation of U.S. Const. amend. VI and Cal. Const. art. I, § 16. The people, real party in interest, opposed defendant's motion on the basis that the judicial district, not the entire county, was the proper geographic area for defining community for group representation purposes. Respondent superior court denied defendant's motion. Defendant filed a petition for writ of mandate in the appellate court, which was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the judicial district best served the constitutional considerations in the determination of the appropriate community for cross-section analysis. The court found that creation of judicial districts under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 69640- 69650 manifested a legislative intent that the courts of the district serve the population within its boundaries and that use of the judicial district as the appropriate community effected this purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant's petition on the grounds that the judicial district best served the constitutional considerations in the determination of the appropriate community for cross-section analysis and defendant had not made the required prima facie showing of systematic underrepresentation of black people in jury panels within that district. Defendant's case was remanded for trial.

 

6 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Cumero v. Public Employment Relations Bd.


S.F. No. 24905


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 575; 778 P.2d 174; 262 Cal. Rptr. 46; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1603; 132 L.R.R.M. 2575



September 7, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied July 10, 1990.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the cause is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which ruled in favor of respondent employment board in a review of a law and practice of deducting employment organization fees from payrolls of nonmembers.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a public school principal, filed a claim to dispute the involuntary withholding of an employment organization fee from his payroll for an organization of which he was not a member. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the Educational Employment Relations Act (EERA), Cal. Govt. Code, tit. 1, div. 4, ch. 10.7 § 3540, et seq., forbade the use of nonmember fees for any activities other that for representational obligations. The court held that affiliates of the organization could have spent the fees for such, and that the employment organization had the burden to prove what the expenditures were attributable to. The court further held that the organization's right under EERA to withhold involuntary payroll deductions was lawful pursuant to the organization's agreement with the employer. The court also ordered the lower appellate court reconsider appellant's application for attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower appellate court on the grounds that fees may only be withheld under the relevant statutory law for activities relating to representational activities.

 

7 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bell


No. S004260, Crim. No. 20879


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 502; 778 P.2d 129; 262 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1602



September 5, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied November 30, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.
Rehearing denied by, Stay granted by People v. Bell, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 1400 (Cal., Mar. 14, 1990)
Writ of certiorari denied Bell v. California, 493 U.S. 963, 495 U.S. 963, 110 S. Ct. 2576, 109 L. Ed. 2d 757, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 2694, 58 U.S.L.W. 3754 (1990)
Stay granted by In re Bell, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 2379 (Cal., May 29, 1990)
Later proceeding at People v. Bell (Ronald Lee), 2004 Cal. LEXIS 12589 (Cal., Dec. 15, 2004)
Costs and fees proceeding at People v. Ronald Lee Bell, 2007 Cal. LEXIS 5637 (Cal., May 24, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 21631, Richard E. Arnason, Judge.
People v. Bell, 44 Cal. 3d 137, 241 Cal. Rptr. 890, 745 P.2d 573, 1987 Cal. LEXIS 458 (1987)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted defendant of the first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664, 187, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and possession of a concealable firearm by an ex-felon under Cal. Penal Code § 12021. Defendant was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment sentencing defendant to death. The court concluded that defendant failed to carry his burden of establishing a prima facie case that the underrepresentation of Blacks was due to systematic exclusion in the jury selection process. The court found that a timely admonition would have cured the impact of prosecutorial misconduct in the guilt phase of the trial to which defendant failed to object and concluded that the prosecutorial misconduct to which objection was made did not render defendant's trial fundamentally unfair so as to deny him due process of law. The court concluded that defendant's conviction for violating Cal. Penal Code § 12021 was valid because it was not one of the penalties or disabilities released pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1772. The court found that defendant did not carry his burden of proving that trial counsel's failure to object to the prosecutor's misconduct amounted to deficient performance. The court determined that there was no error in the reading of the entire list of mitigating factors to the jury or in the prosecutor's comments on the factors included in that instruction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant. Defendant failed to establish that the underrepresentation of Blacks was due to systematic exclusion in the jury selection process, prosecutorial misconduct did not render defendant's trial fundamentally unfair and there was no error in the reading of the entire list of mitigating factors to the jury in an instruction.

 

8 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lentz v. McMahon


No. S.F. 25123


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 393; 777 P.2d 83; 261 Cal. Rptr. 310; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1594



August 17, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 813146, Stuart R. Pollak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that, under appropriate circumstances, a recipient of welfare benefits may raise the defense of equitable estoppel; the relevant statutes contemplate application of the defense at DSS hearings; and our Constitution does not preclude application of the equitable estoppel doctrine at DSS administrative hearings. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, welfare recipients and watchdog agency, appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (California), which barred the application of the doctrine of equitable estoppel at hearings by defendants, department of social services and director, in an action concerning the recoupment of over-payments of welfare benefits.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, welfare recipients and watchdog agency, filed suit against defendants, department of social services and director, after defendant director failed to adopt a proposed decision by the administrative hearing judge that defendants were equitably estopped from collecting overpayment of welfare benefits to plaintiff recipients. The trial court issued a permanent injunction and declaratory judgment that held illegal defendants' policy of refusing to consider claims of equitable estoppel in administrative hearings. The court of appeals reversed, and the court reversed on plaintiffs' appeal, reinstating the decision of the trial court. The court held that under appropriate circumstances, a recipient of welfare benefits may raise the defense of equitable estoppel. The court held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10950 et seq. contemplated application of the defense at hearings held by defendants, and that Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1 and art. III, § 3 did not preclude application of the equitable estoppel doctrine at administrative hearings by defendants.


OUTCOME: Decision barring the application of equitable estoppel at hearings by defendants, department of social services and director, was reversed because plaintiffs, welfare recipients, could raise the defense of equitable estoppel. The relevant statutes contemplated application of the defense at defendants' hearings, and the California constitution did not preclude application of the equitable estoppel doctrine at such hearings.

 

9 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McHugh v. Santa Monica Rent Control Bd.


L.A. No. 32062


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 348; 777 P.2d 91; 261 Cal. Rptr. 318; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1597



August 17, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondent McHugh for a rehearing was denied November 1, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC83569, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons discussed above, we conclude former section 1809 is not constitutionally infirm except insofar as it authorizes the Board to award treble damages and permits orders awarding restitution to become effective before there is an opportunity for the court to pass on whether to stay the challenged order pending review of the administrative decision by writ of mandate. The judgment is reversed with directions to recall and/or set aside the peremptory writ of mandate issued by the court on November 15, 1983, to issue a new and different writ commanding the Board to reduce its awards in conformity with this decision, to stay enforcement of any future order in accordance with this decision (see ante, fn. 44), to deny the petition for writ of mandate in all other respects, and to deny the motions of plaintiff and interveners for summary judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant rent control board appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment for plaintiff landlord and intervenor landlords on their petition for writ of mandate, directed defendant to vacate the orders awarding restitution and treble damages to the tenants, and declared that Santa Monica City Charter art. XVIII, § 1809(b) violated Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1's judicial powers clause.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court determined that administrative adjudication of excess rent claims under the Santa Monica Rent Control Charter Amendment, Santa Monica City Charter art. XVIII, did not, in and of itself, violate Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1. The court found that defendant rent control board, an administrative agency, was authorized to make restitutive money awards where it was reasonably necessary to effectuate defendant's primary, legitimate regulatory purpose and ultimate judicial review remained in the courts. Nevertheless, the court then held that the imposition of treble damages was a power beyond defendant's authority and that the rent withholding order was invalid because it did not provide for an opportunity for judicial review before the order took effect. The court reversed with directions to set aside the peremptory writ of mandate, to deny the mandate petition and the summary judgment motions, and to issue a writ commanding defendant to reduce its award, noting that the constitutional right to jury trial did not preclude administrative adjudication of excess rent claims that met the substantive-limitations requirement imposed by the judicial powers clause.


OUTCOME: Although the court held that the Santa Monica Rent Control Charter Amendment was constitutionally infirm to the extent that defendant rent control board was not authorized to award treble damages or to order the immediate withholding of rent before an opportunity for judicial review, the court reversed the summary judgments and mandate relief granted in the case because defendant was authorized to adjudicate excess rent claims administratively.

 

10 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Andrews


No. S004617, Crim. No. 23785


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 200; 776 P.2d 285; 260 Cal. Rptr. 583; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1537



August 3, 1989  
August 31, 1989


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 29, 1989, and the opinion was modified on September 28, 1989, to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-364296, Aurelio Munoz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The four duplicative multiple-murder and prior-murder special circumstances are ordered stricken. In all other respects, the judgment, including the sentence of death, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was convicted of three first-degree murder counts, robbery, rape, and sodomy by foreign object, challenged the imposition of the death penalty by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California).


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the death sentence of defendant, who was convicted of three counts of first-degree murder as well as robbery, rape, and sodomy by foreign object. Considering his numerous arguments, it found that a tape-recorded statement by his accomplice was admissible as a prior consistent statement under Cal. Evid. Code § 791(b) and that the jury was properly instructed about the use of his testimony under Cal. Penal Code § 1111. It held that the trial court did not err in denying his motion to serve as his own co-counsel, finding that defendant did not show how this would have served justice and judicial efficiency. It found that his conviction as an adult for an Alabama murder he committed when he was 16 could be used as a special circumstance under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(2) because he could have been convicted as an adult too had he committed the crime in California. Finally, it held that although the trial court was not required to instruct the jury that it could exercise mercy, the prosecutor had in effect acknowledged that such a consideration was proper, and that the jury had been clearly instructed on how it was to treat the aggravating and mitigating factors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the imposition of the death penalty on defendant for his convictions of murder, robbery, rape, and sodomy by foreign object, finding that the jury instructions were proper, that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion to serve as his own co-counsel, and that a tape-recorded statement by his accomplice was admissible.

 

11 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (lucero)


No. S002438


Supreme Court of California


49 Cal. 3d 14; 774 P.2d 769; 259 Cal. Rptr. 740; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1524; 10 A.L.R.5th 1037



June 29, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of real parties in interest for a rehearing was denied August 24, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The People, having alleged multiple violations of Chapter 21.51, section 21.51.030 of the Long Beach Municipal Code, each based on the unconstitutional "single use" standard, are not entitled to proceed with the action as pleaded. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from the decision of the Court of Appeal (California) that refused to issue a writ of mandate to compel respondent superior court to vacate its judgment that granted, with leave to amend, the petition of real parties in interest, owners, for a peremptory writ of mandate directing the municipal court to sustain demurrers to the complaints which charged numerous violations of Long Beach, Cal. Mun. Code ch. 21.51.


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest, owners, were charged with unlawfully establishing an adult entertainment business. Long Beach, Cal. Mun. Code ch. 21.51. They filed demurrers, which were overruled. They petitioned respondent superior court for a peremptory writ of mandate directing the municipal court to sustain the demurrers. Respondent granted the writ, with leave to amend. The people requested a writ of mandate to compel respondent to vacate its judgment. The court of appeal denied the writ and rejected the contention that a single showing of an adult film made a theater an adult motion picture theater within the meaning of the zoning ordinance. The people appealed. The court affirmed. Adult entertainment ordinances were intended to regulate the clustered establishment of adult entertainment businesses and not to prohibit theaters from occasionally exhibiting an "adult" film. A "regular and substantial course of conduct" standard defined the constitutional level of "use" for purposes of such ordinances. Section 21.51.020(A)(2) applied to theaters offering adult fare as a substantial part of their business, but did not apply to theaters showing only occasional adult movies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the refusal to issue a writ of mandate compelling respondent superior court to vacate its judgment. The court held that a "regular and substantial course of conduct" standard defined the constitutional level of "use" for purposes of zoning ordinances aimed at regulating the clustered establishment of adult entertainment business. The ordinance did not apply to a theater that only showed adult movies occasionally.

 

12 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Murtishaw


No. S004521, Crim. No. 23039


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 1001; 773 P.2d 172; 258 Cal. Rptr. 821; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1359



June 8, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied August 31, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 19333, William A. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We find no prejudicial error warranting reversal of the verdicts of guilt and death. We therefore affirm the judgment in full.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was automatically entitled to a appeal pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b) after the Superior Court of Kern County (California) convicted him of three counts of first degree murder, one count of assault with intent to commit murder, one firearm-use allegation, and one multiple-murder special-circumstance allegation and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder, assault with intent to commit murder, a firearm-use allegation, and a multiple-murder special-circumstance. He was sentenced to death, and he was entitled to an automatic appeal. Defendant alleged that it was improper to deny his request to change venue, to fail to give a jury instruction regarding an unreasonable belief that self-defense was necessary, and to utilize penalty instructions that had not yet become effective, among other claims. The death sentence was affirmed because the change of venue was properly denied as defendant did not show a reasonable likelihood that he would be unable to get a fair trial in the original county. The court found that a specific instruction explaining that an unreasonable belief that self-defense was warranted as a reasonable defense was not required because the case law establishing the instruction did not have to be applied retroactively and that other jury instructions sufficiently explained such a defense. The court found that while it was error to give instructions not yet effective, the difference between the current penalty instructions and the ones given did not substantially prejudice defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and the death sentence imposed upon him because the trial court did not err in refusing to change the venue of the trial, in inadvertently using penalty instructions that had not yet become effective, or in permitting and denying various other jury instructions requested because such decisions did not result in prejudicial error to defendant.

 

13 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sheldon


No. S004704, Crim. No. 25109


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 935; 771 P.2d 1330; 258 Cal. Rptr. 242; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1297



May 15, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied August 10, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-22919, J. William Mortland and John H. Hews, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment convicting defendant of assault with a deadly weapon is reversed. The judgment of conviction is affirmed in all other respects but the judgment of death is vacated and the cause remanded to the trial court for the limited purpose of redetermining defendant's application for modification of the verdict in accordance with this opinion. If the trial court, upon application of the appropriate standards, denies the application for modification, it shall reinstate the judgment of death. If it grants the application, it shall enter a judgment of life without possibility of parole. Any subsequent appeal shall be limited to issues related to the modification application. (See People v. Rodriguez, supra, 42 Cal.3d 730, 794-795.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which denied defendant's motion to modify the death sentence he received following his convictions for first-degree murder, accompanied by three felony-murder special-circumstances findings, and for robbery, vehicle theft, kidnapping for ransom and extortion, residential burglary, burglary, and assault with a firearm.


OVERVIEW: On review, the appellate court determined that no prejudicial error had occurred with respect to all of the challenges defendant raised to his convictions except the weapon-assault charge, and accordingly affirmed those judgments of conviction. The appellate court reversed the conviction on the assault with a deadly weapon charge because the trial court failed to instruct the jury, sua sponte, on the definition of an assault. The appellate court noted that it could not have been assumed, from the jury's firearm-use finding alone, that the jury further found defendant had the present ability to, and in fact attempted to, apply physical force to the victim. The appellate court then vacated the judgment of death and remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings on defendant's automatic application for modification of sentence pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.4(e) because of the trial court's failure to state the reasons supporting the denial of that application.


OUTCOME: Although the appellate court reversed the judgment convicting defendant of assault with a deadly weapon, it affirmed the convictions on the remaining offenses against defendant. Defendant's death sentence was also vacated and the case remanded for the limited purpose of redetermining defendant's application for modification of the verdict because the trial court failed to state its reasons for denying the application.

 

14 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Allison


No. S004649, Crim. No. 24058


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 879; 771 P.2d 1294; 258 Cal. Rptr. 208; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1295



May 11, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 29, 1989, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Modification of Opinion June 29, 1989; Rehearing denied June 29, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A026128, Pat Mullendore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We have found no prejudicial error at either the guilt or penalty phases of defendant's trial. The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction and death penalty sentence by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211, first degree murder Cal. Penal Code § 187, use of a firearm in the commission of both offenses, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, and the special circumstance of committing the murder while engaged in the commission or attempted commission of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to death for his convictions of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211, first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, use of a firearm in the commission of both offenses, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, and the special circumstance of committing the murder while engaged in the commission or attempted commission of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i). Appellant sought review and the court affirmed and held that appellant did not object to testimony of unrelated crimes or statements characterizing the victim's death, and therefore waived them on appeal, that the state did not commit misconduct by implying that appellant's testimony was fabricated, that there was no error in instructing the jury on intent to kill or in the penalty and sentencing phases, that there were no grounds for reversal for the trial court's unresponsive answer to a jury question, that appellant was not prejudiced by the trial court's failure to adequately state its reasons for denying the automatic motion for modification of the penalty verdict, and that there was no prejudicial error at either the guilt or penalty phases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and sentence and held that the issue of errors in admitting certain testimony were waived because appellant failed to object at trial, that there was no misconduct in the state's implication that testimony was fabricated, that there was no error in the jury's instructions, and that there was no prejudicial error in the trial court's failure to state its reasons for denying the penalty verdict modification motion.

People v. Burton


No. S004691, Crim. No. 24589


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 843; 771 P.2d 1270; 258 Cal. Rptr. 184; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1293



May 8, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied July 27, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A026664, D. Sterry Fagan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of murder with personal use of a firearm, three counts of robbery with personal use of a firearm, and intentional infliction of great bodily injury, and sentencing him to death. The jury found true the special circumstance allegation that the murder was committed in the course of a robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought an automatic appeal from a judgment of death after his conviction for murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, with personal use of a firearm Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5, three counts of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211, with personal use of a firearm, § 12022.5, and, in one instance, with intentional infliction of great bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7. The jury also found true the special circumstance allegation that the murder was committed in the course of a robbery. Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i). The court found that there was no abuse of discretion by the trial court as to evidentiary matters and the court found that no reasonable jury would have been confused or misled as to whether to impose the sentence of death or life in prison without possibility of parole. The robbery victim had a good opportunity to observe defendant, and she positively identified him. The assault victim, who was shot in the eye, watched defendant shoot and kill the victim's mother. He retained consciousness and was also able to identify defendant. The conviction and sentence were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding defendant guilty of murder with personal use of a firearm, three counts of robbery with personal use of a firearm, and, in one instance, with intentional infliction of great bodily injury, and the finding of the special circumstance that the murder was committed in the course of a robbery, and the sentence of death, were affirmed because there was no abuse of discretion and the jury was not misled or confused.

 

16 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Calfarm Ins. Co. v. Deukmejian


No. S007838


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 805; 771 P.2d 1247; 258 Cal. Rptr. 161; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1292; 1990-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P68,951



May 4, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is granted to the extent that it seeks to compel respondents to refrain from enforcing sections 1861.01, subdivision (b) and 1861.10, subdivision (c). In all other respects the petition is denied. The parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners applied for a writ of mandate challenging the validity of an initiative that made changes in the regulation of automobile insurance.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed an application for a writ of mandate challenging an initiative that made changes in the regulation of automobile insurance, arguing that the initiative was unconstitutional on its face. The court held that two provisions of the initiative were invalid but severable from remainder of initiative. A provision related to the reduction and adjustment of insurance rates was declared invalid because the court found that it precluded adjustments necessary to achieve a constitutional standard of fair and reasonable rates and that it could not be sustained as a temporary or emergency measure. A provision related to consumer-advocacy was declared invalid as a violation of Cal. Const. art. II, § 12. However, since the court found that each invalid provision was severable from remainder of initiative, it held that the remainder of initiative was facially constitutional. Accordingly, the court granted the petition as it related to the invalid provisions but denied it for the remainder of initiative.


OUTCOME: The court held that two provisions of the initiative were unconstitutional and therefore granted the petition for a writ of mandate as to the unconstitutional provisions, and denied it as to the remainder of the initiative, finding that since unconstitutional provisions were severable, the remainder was constitutional.

 

17 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bonillas


No. S004525, Crim. No. 23117


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 757; 771 P.2d 844; 257 Cal. Rptr. 895; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1158



May 1, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. OCR7904, Fenton E. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in all respects except that the judgment of death is vacated and the cause is remanded to the trial court for prompt reconsideration of the automatic application for modification of the death verdict (§ 190.4, subd. (e)), and for pronouncement of judgment on the conviction of burglary. If the trial court, upon application of appropriate standards, denies the application for modification of the verdict, it shall reinstate the judgment of death. If it grants the application, it shall enter a judgment of life without possibility of parole. Any subsequent appeal shall be limited to issues related to the modification application or the sentence on the burglary count. (See People v. Rodriguez, supra, 42 Cal.3d 730, 794-795.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which entered convictions against him for murder and burglary with a finding of a burglary felony-murder special circumstance and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial for murder and burglary with a finding of a burglary felony-murder special circumstance, the evidence during the guilt phase was largely uncontroverted. Defendant entered his neighbor's home with the intent to steal something. When the victim came home unexpectedly, defendant hid in a closet. When defendant attempted to escape, the victim struggled with him. The struggle ended with defendant killing the victim. The jury verdict found defendant guilty of murder and burglary and found true the burglary-murder special circumstance. However, the guilty verdict did not specify the degree of murder. Before beginning the penalty phase, which resulted in imposition of the death penalty, the trial court reconvened the jury and the jury returned a verdict of murder in the first degree. On appeal, the court found that the trial court was authorized to reconvene the jury to complete the verdict by specifying the degree of murder because the jury remained within the trial court's control. The court vacated the judgment of death because the trial court did not exercise its independent judgment in reweighing the evidence of mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the murder and burglary convictions but remanded the case for pronouncement of judgment on the burglary conviction. The death sentence was vacated because the trial court rejected defendant's motion for modification of the verdict without exercising its independent judgment in reweighing the evidence.

 

18 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Brown v. Kelly Broadcasting Co.


No. S005126


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 711; 771 P.2d 406; 257 Cal. Rptr. 708; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1527; 16 Media L. Rep. 1625



April 27, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 323542, Roger K. Warren, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Because we find there is no such privilege in this case, we agree with the Court of Appeal that summary judgment was improper. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed with directions to remand this action to the trial court for further proceedings in accordance with the views set forth in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: News media defendant challenged the decision of the court of appeals (California), which reversed the summary judgment in defendant's favor in private-figure plaintiff's defamation action. The court of appeals held that Cal. Civ. Code § 47(3) provided a conditional privilege to defendant's television broadcasts but found that whether defendant acted with malice was a triable issue.


OVERVIEW: News media defendant, which operated a television station, broadcast a story that private-figure plaintiff, a contractor, made mistakes in remodeling a home. After defendant refused plaintiff's demand for a retraction, plaintiff filed a defamation action. Defendant filed a motion for summary judgment, which was granted by the trial court. The trial court held that the broadcast was conditionally privileged under Cal. Civ. Code § 47(3) and that plaintiff failed to raise a triable issue regarding whether the privilege was overcome by defendant's malice. The court of appeals agreed that § 47(3) provided a conditional privilege but found a triable issue regarding whether defendant had acted with malice. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals because the broadcast was not subject to a privilege under § 47(3). In refusing to create a public-interest privilege for the news media, the court stated that the implicit result of recognition of such a privilege would be the following rule: If it is published, it is privileged. The court presumed that the media would always argue that its communication was a matter of public interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of the grant of summary judgment in favor of news media defendant. The court concluded that the television broadcasts regarding private-figure plaintiff were not protected by a conditional privilege and that plaintiff was not required to prove malice to recover compensatory damages.

People v. Morales


No. S004552, Crim. No. 23153


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 527; 770 P.2d 244; 257 Cal. Rptr. 64; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1107



April 6, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of appellant Morales for a rehearing was denied June 1, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Modification of Opinion June 1, 1989.
Rehearing denied by People v. Morales, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1672 (Cal., June 1, 1989)
Writ of certiorari denied Morales v. California, 493 U.S. 984, 110 S. Ct. 520, 107 L. Ed. 2d 520, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 5529 (1989)
Later proceeding at People v. Morales (Michael Angelo), 2006 Cal. LEXIS 2164 (Cal., Jan. 9, 2006)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Morales v. Ornoski, 439 F.3d 529, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 3997 (9th Cir. Cal., 2006)
Post-conviction proceeding at Morales v. Cal. Dep't of Corr. & Rehab., 2008 Cal. App. LEXIS 2283 (Cal. App. 1st Dist., Nov. 21, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 17960, Charles R. McGrath, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported judgment n.o.v. as to the implied torture-murder finding is set aside, and in all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which entered a conviction against him for murder in the first degree and sentenced him to death. The state cross-appealed the trial court's ruling that granted judgment notwithstanding the verdict as to the jury's implied finding of first degree torture murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, codefendant, and the victim went to the shopping mall together. Defendant sat in the backseat directly behind the victim. After codefendant drove to a remote location, defendant strangled the victim with his belt. After the belt broke, defendant hit the victim's head repeatedly with a hammer. The jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder. In addition to finding that the murder was premeditated, the jury made an implied finding of torture murder. The jury also found true the lying-in-wait and torture-murder special circumstances. Defendant was sentenced to death. The trial court granted judgment notwithstanding the verdict as to the jury's implied finding of torture murder. On the state's cross-appeal, the court concluded that the jury's implied finding of torture murder should not have been disturbed by the trial court. The court rejected defendant's argument that physical concealment was a required element of lying-in-wait murder. The court sustained the finding regarding the torture-murder special circumstance because the jury necessarily found an intent to cause extreme pain when it found defendant guilty of torture murder.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's first degree murder conviction and death sentence. On the state's cross-appeal, the court reversed the trial court's ruling that granted judgment notwithstanding the verdict as to the jury's implied finding of first degree torture murder.

In re Utz


No. S005651


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 468; 769 P.2d 417; 256 Cal. Rptr. 561; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1102



March 30, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied May 3, 1989.



DISPOSITION: It is ordered that petitioner be disbarred from the practice of law in this state and that his name be stricken from the roll of attorneys. This order is effective upon the finality of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court reviewed the unanimous recommendation of the Review Department of the State Bar Court, (California) that petitioner attorney be disbarred from the practice of law. The recommendation was based on petitioner's conviction of seven counts of mail fraud, 18 U.S.C.S. § 1341, and two counts of using interstate transportation to defraud individuals, 18 U.S.C.S. § 2314.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was placed on interim suspension pending the finality of his convictions for mail fraud and use of interstate transportation to defraud as to discipline. After petitioner's convictions were final, his case was referred to the state bar for hearing, report and recommendation. The hearing panel, composed of a single referee, recommended, pursuant to state bar procedural rules, that petitioner be summarily disbarred under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6102 (c). The state bar court's review department unanimously adopted these findings and recommendations. The state bar, heard the case, upon referral. Petitioner contended that he was deprived of an impartial hearing and that state bar procedures were unconstitutional. The state bar conducted its own independent review and found petitioner's arguments without merit and considered that many people had lost money in the fraudulent land scheme. Finding that petitioner's conviction dealt with moral turpitude, the court concluded that under § 6102 it had to protect the public, the courts, and the legal profession. Therefore, petitioner's disbarment was warranted. Petitioner was disbarred from the practice of law.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that petitioner attorney be disbarred from the practice of law in this state and that his name be stricken from the roll of attorneys. The court concluded that lesser discipline would not have been effective in protecting the public and that the record supported disbarment of petition. The court's order was to be made effective upon the finality its opinion.

 

21 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Schmidt v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 32110


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 370; 769 P.2d 932; 256 Cal. Rptr. 750; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1100



March 27, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the case is remanded with directions to deny the peremptory writ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In order to resolve a conflict among the appellate court decisions, the court took review of a decision from the Court of Appeal (California) holding that Cal. Civ. Code § 798.76 barred a private mobile home park owner from adopting or enforcing a rule limiting residence in the part to persons 25 years or older.


OVERVIEW: This case involved the issue of whether, under California law, a private mobile home park rule limiting residence in the park to persons 25 years or older was valid. Petitioners were denied residency in a mobile home park because some of them were younger than 25. The trial court found the rule valid, but the Court of Appeal disagreed, concluding that Cal. Civ. Code § 798.76 barred a private mobile home park owner from adopting or enforcing such a rule. The California Supreme Court granted review in order to resolve a conflict among appellate court decisions. With respect to that state law issue, the court concluded that applicable California statutes permitted the establishment and enforcement of such a rule in private mobile home parks, and that neither the rule nor the related statutes were unconstitutional. While recently enacted federal law would alter the validity of such a rule in the future, the mobile home park owner did not violate petitioners' statutory or constitutional rights in applying the rule during the time period at issue in this case. Accordingly, the judgment below was reversed.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court, which found a private mobile home park rule barring residents under age 25 to be invalid, was reversed based on the court's conclusion that applicable California statutes permitted the rule, and neither the rule nor the related statutes were unconstitutional.

S. G. Borello & Sons, Inc. v. Department of Industrial Relations


No. S003956


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


48 Cal. 3d 341; 769 P.2d 399; 256 Cal. Rptr. 543; 54 Cal. Comp. Cases 80; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 975



March 23, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 587811, Jack Komar, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  We therefore hold as a matter of law that Borello has failed to demonstrate the cucumber sharefarmers are independent contractors excluded from coverage under the Act. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal, directing the superior court to grant Borello's petition for writ of mandate, is reversed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On its own motion, the court reviewed the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California) that laborers engaged to harvest cucumbers under a written "sharefarmer" agreement were "independent contractors" exempt from workers' compensation coverage and not "employees."


OVERVIEW: Defendant, the California Division of Labor Standards Enforcement of the Department of Industrial Relations rejected plaintiff employer's contentions that agricultural laborers engaged to harvest cucumbers under a written "sharefarmer" agreement were "independent contractors" exempt from workers' compensation coverage. Plaintiff appealed. The superior court found that the agency's decision was supported by the evidence. Plaintiff appealed to the court of appeal, which reversed the superior court decision. On its own motion, the court reviewed and reversed the judgment of the court of appeal because plaintiff had failed to meet his burden of proof under the statutory "control-of-work" test. The court held that the laborers were "employees" because plaintiff retained control over their work.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeal that laborers engaged to harvest cucumbers under a written "sharefarmer" agreement were "independent contractors" exempt from workers' compensation coverage and not "employees" because plaintiff employer controlled their work.

The gravity of petitioner's conduct could not be overstated -- he misappropriated client funds and used those funds to purchase illegal drugs.


OUTCOME: The recommendation of the Review Department of the California State Bar Court was adopted and petitioner was ordered disbarred based on his conviction for misdemeanor grand theft stemming from his misappropriation of funds from a client trust account.

In re Prantil


No. S006817


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 227; 768 P.2d 109; 255 Cal. Rptr. 890; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 379



March 6, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  It is ordered that petitioner Frank George Prantil be disbarred and that his name be stricken from the roll of attorneys in this state. It is further ordered that petitioner comply with the requirements of rule 955 of the California Rules of Court, and perform the acts specified in subdivisions (a) and (c) of that rule within 30 and 40 days, respectively, of the effective date of this order. This order is effective upon the finality of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought review of the decision of the review department of the California State Bar Court, which recommended disbarment following petitioner's conviction for forgery by uttering pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 470.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was on probation from a prior suspension when he committed the acts that led to his conviction for forgery. He was charged with forgery by uttering pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 470. At trial, petitioner argued that he knew nothing about forgery and that he had helped the woman deposit the check merely for collection purposes; the check had been stolen and the signature on check had been forged. The court disbelieved petitioner and upheld his conviction. Following a hearing, the State Bar referee recommended disbarment based on petitioner's crime of moral turpitude, and the review department unanimously adopted the recommendation. The court adopted the review department's recommendation and held that petitioner could not reassert his innocence of the crime of forgery in this proceeding because, pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6101, the record of conviction was conclusive evidence of guilt of petitioner's crime of forgery; collateral attacks were prohibited by § 6101. Petitioner was afforded full opportunity to contest the validity of his underlying conviction by means of direct appeal and habeas corpus; therefore, petitioner was not denied due process.


OUTCOME: The court adopted the review department's recommendation and disbarred petitioner attorney because petitioner's crime of forgery was one of moral turpitude. Petitioner was not able to collaterally attack his forgery conviction; the record of conviction was conclusive evidence of guilt of the crime.

People v. Coleman

Supreme Court of California

Rehearing denied by, Writ of certiorari denied Coleman v. California, 494 U.S. 1038, 110 S. Ct. 1501, 108 L. Ed. 2d 635, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 1369, 58 U.S.L.W. 3596 (1990)

DISPOSITION: The finding of the special circumstance that defendant committed the murder in order to avoid or prevent lawful arrest (§ 190.2, subd. (a)(5)) is set aside as unsupported by substantial evidence. Otherwise the judgment convicting defendant of murder and sentencing him to death is affirmed. The prison sentences on counts two through eight are vacated, and the cause is remanded for resentencing on those counts in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction from the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California) of murder for the purpose of avoiding or preventing lawful arrest, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(5), in the commission of a robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i), and in the commission of a burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(vii), as well as seven other counts. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(5), had to be set aside as unsupported by substantial evidence, but that otherwise the judgment convicting defendant of murder and sentencing him to death was proper. The case was remanded for resentencing on several counts because the trial court failed to explain its decision to impose a full consecutive term on a rape by force and threats count, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667.6(c), and because the court relied on duplicative factors to justify consecutive terms, upper terms, and enhancements. Because substantial evidence supported the jury's verdict on the other counts, there was no reversible error. The court found that pre-trial publicity, though initially graphic, was not persistent and pervasive, and denial of defendant's motion for change of venue was not prejudicial error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the death penalty and the convictions of murder, robbery, burglary, and several other counts. The finding of the special circumstance that defendant committed the murder in order to avoid or prevent lawful arrest was set aside as unsupported by substantial evidence. The prison sentences on six counts were vacated, and remanded for resentencing because the trial court erroneously applied duplicative factors.

Title Ins. & Trust Co. v. County of Riverside


No. S001002


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 84; 767 P.2d 1148; 255 Cal. Rptr. 670; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 20



February 27, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of plaintiff and appellant for a rehearing was denied April 5, 1989. Kaufman, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 144292, Gerald F. Schulte, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed with directions to order the trial court to enter judgment consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county sought review the judgment of the Court of Appeals (California), which affirmed the ruling of the trial court, holding Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c) did not apply, so as to require reassessment of property owned by plaintiff corporation. The appellate court had determined that as a result of a stock merger, there was not a change of ownership.


OVERVIEW: As a result of a stock merger, plaintiff corporation was acquired as a subsidiary. The county assessor reassessed two parcels of real property owned by plaintiff, pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 64(c). The assessments appeal board denied plaintiff's application for reconsideration, and plaintiff paid additional taxes under protest. The trial court ruled in favor of plaintiff, holding § 64(c) did not apply, and that plaintiff was entitled to a refund. The appellate court affirmed the ruling. The court reversed the judgments of the lower courts and held that the stock merger resulted in indirect control over plaintiff, and was a change of ownership within the meaning of § 64(c). The court held that plaintiff's real property was subject to reassessment under Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 2(a) because where one corporation either directly or indirectly obtained control over another by the transfer or purchase of stock, a change of ownership occurred as to the real property owned by the corporation over which it had obtained direct or indirect control.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment holding reassessment was not required, because the stock merger resulted in indirect control over plaintiff corporation. The court held that the stock merger was a change in ownership, and plaintiff's property was subject to reassessment.

 

27 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Johnson


No. S004425, Crim. No. 22503


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 1194; 767 P.2d 1047; 255 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 18



February 23, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied May 4, 1989. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 77796, George W. Bonney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his felony murder conviction and death penalty, which was rendered by the Superior Court No. 77796 of Santa Clara County (California).


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of felony murder and sentenced him to death. Defendant was granted an automatic appeal. Defendant challenged the state's use of peremptory challenges in violation of Batson and Wheeler. Batson only allowed a defendant to challenge the exclusion of the group of which he was a member, whereas Wheeler did not. The state had used its peremptory challenges to strike three Black, four Jewish, and two Asian jurors. When asked to state his reasons for doing so, the state answered with what amounted to trivial reasons concerning body language, dress, and appearance. The court upheld the state's use of peremptory challenges on this group, holding that nowhere in Batson or Wheeler were trivial reasons held invalid. The court recognized that the dissent's hunches concerning the majority's rejection of hunches, gut feelings, and body language was a fear that the state would insincerely attempt to justify group bias with trivial reasons and that trial judges would rubber stamp the state's explanations. The court reasoned that if trial courts could not be trusted to do their jobs fairly, the courts should not operate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's felony murder conviction and death penalty after entry of the jury verdict, holding that the trial judge, although not being as detailed in his statement as the court might desire, was clear about it's obligation to independently review all the evidence, and did give sufficient reasons to support its ruling.

People v. Robertson


No. S004608, Crim. No. 23538


Supreme Court of California


48 Cal. 3d 18; 767 P.2d 1109; 255 Cal. Rptr. 631; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 16



February 23, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied April 5, 1989, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. CR 34455, Roy E. Chapman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The penalty judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of two first degree murders and found true nine charged special circumstances. The jury fixed the penalty at death; which judgment was entered by the Superior Court No. CR 34455 of San Bernardino County (California). The court reversed the judgment as to penalty. After retrial, the trial court fixed the penalty at death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who had the mentality of a 12 year old, was convicted of two first degree murders and sentenced to death by a jury. The court reversed the penalty of death on review, but the trial court fixed the penalty at death on remand. Defendant received an automatic appeal. Defendant challenged the death penalty, contending that appellee state's use of two prior uncharged acts of violence, which were time barred by the statute of limitations, could not be used by the trial court as an aggravating factor because they did not constitute a "crime" within the definition of that term. The court affirmed the judgment of death, holding that the two prior time barred attacks fell within the meaning of one or more penal statutes and were properly considered as other "criminal activity." The court reasoned that there was no bar to consideration of time barred felonious conduct involving force or the threat of force as an aggravating factor. The fact that the felonious conduct was established by one witness was a matter to be explored on cross examination and argued to the trier of fact.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence of death, holding that the trial court properly considered two uncharged time barred felonious conduct involving force or the threat of force, which was established by the testimony of one witness each, as an aggravating factor in determining the sentence of death.

 

29 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Keller v. State Bar


S.F. No. 25050


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 1152; 767 P.2d 1020; 255 Cal. Rptr. 542; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 17



February 23, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 307168, Horace Cecchettini, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the case remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state bar and its board of governors, challenged an order of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed the summary judgment granted to defendants, that had authorized bar activities under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6031(a).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, 21 members of state bar, sued defendants, state bar and its board of governors, alleging that defendants had improperly expended their dues and violated their rights of free speech. The court of appeals reversed the summary judgment granted to defendants; and the supreme court then granted review and reversed in favor of defendants, holding that defendant state bar was a governmental agency authorized to expend dues it collected from members for authorized purposes under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6031(a), such as lobbying, education, filing amicus curiae briefs, and holding delegate conferences. However, the court remanded in part, finding that election campaigning was an impermissible function for defendant state bar and impermissible use of member dues.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court in favor of defendants, state bar and its board of governors, because defendant state bar as a government agency, properly used members' dues to lobby and advise regarding legislation, to file amicus curiae briefs, and for the annual conference of delegates. The court remanded as to election campaigning, which was impermissible.

Conway v. State Bar


No. S004556


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 1107; 767 P.2d 657; 255 Cal. Rptr. 390; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 19; 80 A.L.R.4th 101



February 21, 1989 



DISPOSITION:  We conclude that the procedures for the involuntary inactive enrollment of attorneys under section 6007, subdivision (c) satisfy the requirements of due process and that petitioner has not met his burden of proving he should not have been placed on inactive status. We therefore sustain the State Bar's order enrolling petitioner Daniel James Conway as an inactive member of the State Bar of California.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged the action of the State Bar of California, which involuntarily enrolled petitioner as an inactive member of the bar on the ground that his conduct posed a substantial threat of harm to his clients and the public within the meaning of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6007(c).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was involuntarily enrolled as an inactive member of the bar under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6007(c) as a result of misconduct with clients' cases that was attributable to a severe cocaine addiction. The supreme court issued a writ of review under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6083(b), Cal. R. Ct. 952(c) to consider alleged violations of petitioner's due process rights. The court sustained the bar's order, holding that there had been no denial of petitioner's due process rights because there were reasonable investigatory, adjudicative, and appellate procedures applied to the bar's process, that clear and convincing evidence established guilt of numerous instances of misconduct, and that petitioner had not met his burden of proving he should not have been placed on inactive status.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the state bar's order because the procedures for the involuntary inactive enrollment of attorneys satisfied the requirements of due process and petitioner attorney did not met his burden of proving he should not have been placed on inactive status.

 

31 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Edelbacher


No. S004527, Crim. No. 23126


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 983; 766 P.2d 1; 254 Cal. Rptr. 586; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 7



January 23, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 281252-7, Hollis G. Best, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Insofar as it imposes the death penalty, the judgment is reversed. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of death following conviction in the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), finding defendant guilty of first degree murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187-189, with special circumstance findings of murder while lying in wait, murder for financial gain and murder using a firearm. On a plea of guilty, defendant was also convicted of solicitation to murder another victim under Cal. Penal Code § 653f(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pursued an automatic appeal pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239, of a death penalty judgment that followed his conviction of first degree murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187-189, with special circumstance findings that the murder was intentional and carried out for financial gain, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(1); that defendant intentionally killed the victim while lying in wait, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(a5); and with a finding that the murder was committed using a firearm, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5. The court held that the judgment must be reversed as to the death penalty because the jury was led to believe that the absence of evidence of statutory mitigating factors provided additional aggravating factors that supported the imposition of the death penalty. Moreover, the court also found the death penalty should be reversed because the prosecutor impermissibly argued that evidence of a defendant's character and background could be considered in aggravation, and, most significantly, because the jury may have been misled concerning the nature of the process for determining penalty under the death penalty law, thereby resulting in prejudice to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as to the findings of guilt and special circumstance, but reversed the penalty of death and remanded the matter for a new penalty trial. The court found reversal was required because of the possibility defendant was prejudiced by misleading statements during the penalty phase that might have misled the jury about the nature of the penalty determination process and the scope of a jury's sentencing discretion.

 

32 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Farmer


No. S004489, Crim. No. 22960


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 888; 765 P.2d 940; 254 Cal. Rptr. 508; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 4



January 12, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:
The petitions of both parties for a rehearing were denied February 23, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR 18777, Gerald F. Schulte, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to penalty, and is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant convicted of murder and burglary appealed his conviction and the order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) imposing the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of murder and first-degree burglary, finding that the statements the victim made in telephone conversations with the police dispatcher and a police officer were admissible as spontaneous utterances because although the victim was responding to questions rather than volunteering information unprompted, he was distraught and in severe pain and therefore was unlikely to be in a position to fabricate answers. It found that the probative value of playing a tape recording of the conversation with the dispatcher outweighed the prejudice to defendant because the tape allowed the jury to hear that the victim was in command of his mental faculties when he described his assailant. The court reversed defendant's death sentence, however, finding that the prosecution in its closing argument had suggested that all the jury had to do was weigh aggravating and mitigating factors, and that the law, not the jury, would decide whether defendant lived or died. Because the jury was misled as to its discretion and responsibility in sentencing defendant, it concluded, imposing the death sentence would violate U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of murder and burglary, finding that the trial court did not err in admitting the victim's statements as spontaneous utterances and that a tape recordings of the statements was not unduly prejudicial. It reversed his death sentence as unconstitutional, finding that the prosecution misled the jury about its responsibility for making the decision as to whether defendant lived or died.

People v. Garrison
