Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 746; 765 P.2d 419; 254 Cal. Rptr. 257; 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1



January 5, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitions of both parties for a rehearing were denied March 30, 1989. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that appellant's petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. VCR1144, Bob N. Krug, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt, the finding of one multiple-murder special circumstance, and the findings of felony-murder (robbery) special circumstances are affirmed. The second multiple-murder special circumstance, the witness-killing special circumstances, and the felony-murder (burglary) special circumstances are set aside. The judgment of death is reversed. We discharge the order to show cause and deny the petition for habeas corpus.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which convicted him of two counts of felony murder and sentenced him to death. Defendant also filed a petition for habeas corpus based upon ineffective assistance of his court appointed counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of murder and sentenced to death. The court affirmed the judgment of guilt, the finding of multiple murder special circumstance, and the findings of felony murder, based on a robbery offense. The court set aside the second multiple murder, murder special circumstances, the witness killing special circumstances, and the felony murder, based on a burglary offense, and reversed the judgment of death from the penalty phase, holding that where the trial court gave the Briggs Instruction, which told the jury that the governor had the power to commute the sentence, issue a reprieve, or issue a pardon, the jury instruction was incompatible with the guarantee of fundamental fairness established in the due process clause of Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7, 15, because it was seriously and prejudicially misleading, and it invited the jury to be influenced by speculative and improper considerations. The court denied the a writ of habeas corpus because although defendant's court appointed trial counsel was an alcoholic, defendant failed to show that counsel performance was deficient or that defendant was denied his right to effective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, based on the finding of one multiple murder special circumstance, and the findings of felony murder, robbery, special circumstance on the first count, but reversed on the second count and reversed the judgment of death as to the penalty phase of the trial, because of the Briggs instruction. The court denied defendant's petition for habeas corpus.

Nally v. Grace Community Church


No. S002882


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 278; 763 P.2d 948; 253 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 256



November 23, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NCC18668B, Joseph R. Kalin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the Court of Appeal is directed to enter judgment affirming the judgment of nonsuit and dismissing the action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, parents of decedent, sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (California) that reversed the order of the trial court that granted a motion for nonsuit in favor of defendants, church and clergymen, in plaintiff's suit that alleged clergyman malpractice resulted in the wrongful death of their son where their son committed suicide.


OVERVIEW: Decedent, despondent for months over a personal relationship, was encouraged to seek counseling from defendants, clergymen and church. Such counseling was administered over a short period of time followed by a visit to a medical practitioner who proscribed an antidepressant. Decedent shared his past suicidal thoughts with members of the clergy, none of which referred him to a psychiatrist. After an overdose, decedent expressed his wish to commit suicide to defendants. A church deacon suggested decedent go to a psychiatric facility. The idea was rejected by decedent and his mother. After their son's suicide, plaintiffs, parents of decedent, alleged the clergymen committed malpractice by not referring their son to a psychiatrist once suicide became a foreseeable risk. The trial court rejected the contention and granted a motion for nonsuit. The court of appeals reversed. Upon review, the supreme court held that to impose liability, a duty of care had to be established. Further, the mere foreseeability of harm was insufficient to create a special relationship giving rise to such a duty. The supreme court reinstated the trial court's judgment of nonsuit in favor of defendants.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and directed it to affirm the trial court's judgment of nonsuit dismissing the wrongful death action filed by plaintiffs, parents of decedent. Plaintiffs failed to meet their burden showing that defendant clergymen had a duty as non-therapist counselors to refer their son to licensed mental health professionals once suicide became a foreseeable risk.

Williams v. City of L.A.


No. S005008


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 195; 763 P.2d 480; 252 Cal. Rptr. 817; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 253



November 17, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 365660, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal affirming the trial court's issuance of a writ of mandate is reversed, and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and others, sought review of a judgment of an appellate court (California) which affirmed the trial court's judgment granting respondent discharged police officer's petition for writ of mandate ordering reinstatement, arguing internal affairs investigators failed to advise him of his rights under the Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3300 et seq., among other things.


OVERVIEW: After cooperating with internal affairs investigators, respondent police officer was dismissed from the force for misconduct and being involved in an improper manner of making arrests for bookmaking. Respondent was granted reinstatement up on his petition for writ of mandate that argued that investigators failed to advise him of his rights under the Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act, among other things. Further disciplinary actions were permitted, but the statements made to the investigators had to be excluded as a remedy for the violation of the act. The appellate court affirmed the judgment, and the state supreme court granted review to determine the propriety of remedies for the deprivation of statutory rights conferred on respondent by the act. The state supreme court reversed the affirmance and remanded. The supreme court acknowledged the importance of the rights conferred by the act, and the need to secure compliance with those provisions, but found the trial court abused its discretion in compelling reinstatement where the officer was not prejudiced by the failure to advise him properly and reinstatement was not an appropriate remedy for the wrong.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appellate court's affirmance of the trial court's issuance of a writ of mandate reinstating respondent police officer after arguing that investigators failed to advise him of his rights under an act; therefore, statements to investigators were to be excluded. An abuse of discretion existed where the officer was not prejudiced and reinstatement was not an appropriate remedy, favoring appellants, city and others.

The judgment of the trial court was affirmed, except that the financial-gain special circumstance finding was set aside.

Sam Andrews' Sons v. Agric. Labor Relations Bd.


L.A. No. 32129


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 157; 763 P.2d 881; 253 Cal. Rptr. 30; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 255; 116 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P56,348



November 17, 1988 



DISPOSITION:  The portions of the Court of Appeal's judgment vacating the special compensated one-hour field access provision of the Board's order and directing the Board to establish specific limitations on access are reversed. Otherwise, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed and each party is ordered to bear its own costs on review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent labor relations board and union, party in interest, requested a review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (California) that vacated a previous order of respondent board that required petitioner grower to allow the union field access to workers in its labor camps.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner grower was an agricultural employer subject to the Agricultural Labor Relations Act (ALRA) and its governing board, the ALRB, respondent. There had been repeated disputes between the grower and the United Farm Workers of America (UFW) after the grower continually denied the union access to labor camps. The grower had promulgated rules that required all visitors including family, friends, union representatives and labor board agents to identify the camp resident they wished to see. The resident was then permitted to meet the visitor in the parking lot or in the company park, but outside the camp compound. Unfair labor practice allegations arose leading to a cease and desist order from the ALRB which required the grower to provide access by the union to its employees for one hour to speak about collective bargaining issues. The Court of Appeals reversed the order of the board finding it to be overbroad. Upon review, the state supreme court vacated the court of appeal's judgment that vacated the one-hour field access provision holding the access order was reasonable and allowed only one hour of field access on one agreed-upon day, not one hour each day.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court vacated the decision of the court of appeals that found the special compensated one-hour field access provision of respondent labor relations board order to be overbroad because petitioner grower had previously denied the union field access and because the order was reasonably interpreted to allow only one hour of expanded field access on a specified day, not one hour each day as perceived.

 

40 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Walker v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24996


Supreme Court of California


47 Cal. 3d 112; 763 P.2d 852; 253 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 252



November 10, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied January 9, 1989.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the prosecution of defendant for involuntary manslaughter and felony child endangerment violates neither statutory law nor the California or federal Constitution. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (California) that refused to grant petitioner's application for a writ of prohibition and a stay of the order of respondent, the Superior Court of Sacramento County, which denied her motion to dismiss charges of involuntary manslaughter and felony child endangerment after her child died from meningitis wherein prayer was administered in lieu of medical attention.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner mother had a child who died of meningitis. In keeping with her chosen faith, petitioner treated her ill child through prayer rather than through medical treatment. After several days without conventional medical intervention, the child died. State charged petitioner with involuntary manslaughter and felony child endangerment. Counsel's motion to dismiss, alleging petitioner's conduct was protected by law and asserting that she was not given fair notice that her conduct was criminal, was denied. The court of appeals denied her motion for w writ of prohibition and a stay. The state supreme court granted petitioner's application for review after the motion to dismiss was denied. Upon review, the court found that under Cal. Penal Code § 270, parents had to furnish medical attendance or other remedial care to their children. Further, the section exempted parents who utilized prayer treatment from the requirement to furnish medical care. However, felony liability for failure to seek medical care for a seriously ill child was justified by a compelling state interest. As such, the prosecution for the charges against petitioner could stand.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals that denied petitioner mother's application for a writ of prohibition and a stay because prosecution of petitioner mother for involuntary manslaughter and felony child endangerment did not violate statutory law nor the California or federal Constitution because there was a compelling state interest in requiring parents to provide medical care for their seriously ill child.

County of San Mateo v. Dell J.


No. S002243


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 1236; 762 P.2d 1202; 252 Cal. Rptr. 478; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 249



October 31, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 269050, James L. Browning, Jr., Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff County of San Mateo sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California) reversing the order for defendants parents to pay child support and reimbursement, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff County of San Mateo commenced an action against defendants parents for child support and reimbursement, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350, which mandated compulsory reimbursement from the minor's family for Aid to Families With Dependent Children-Foster Care (AFDC-FC) funds paid to a foster care facility on the minor's behalf. The superior court entered a judgment for plaintiff. In reversing, the court of appeal held it a violation of equal protection to require reimbursement from the parents for AFDC-FC funds used to support their minor child who was declared a ward of the court. On appeal, the court held that the county's statutory entitlement, under both state and federal law, to reimbursement from the family of such a minor for AFDC-FC funds expended for his support and maintenance in a foster care facility did not violate equal protection. The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' judgment reversing order for defendants parents to pay child support and reimbursement. The county's statutory entitlement, under both state and federal law, to reimbursement from the family of such a minor for Aid to Families With Dependent Children-Foster Care expended for his support and maintenance in a foster care facility did not violate equal protection.

 

43 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Henning v. Industrial Welfare Com.


No. S005119


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 1262; 762 P.2d 442; 252 Cal. Rptr. 278; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 247; 28 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 1619; 110 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,965



October 31, 1988 



DISPOSITION:  The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Industrial Welfare Commission and intervenors, a hotel and a restaurant association, challenged the writ of administrative mandamus issued by a California court of appeal at the request of appellant employees, which writ compelled respondent to vacate and treat as void an administrative order fixing a different minimum wage for employees receiving tips.


OVERVIEW: After respondent Industrial Welfare Commission issued a two-tiered minimum wage order authorizing a lower minimum wage for employees who received tips, appellant employees requested and obtained a writ of administrative mandamus from the court of appeal compelling respondent to vacate its order to the extent it created a lower minimum wage for tip employees. Petitions for review were filed by respondent and by intervenors, a hotel and a restaurant association. The court affirmed the grant of administrative mandamus. While acknowledging that respondent was not required to fix a single minimum wage for all employees, the court interpreted Cal. Lab. Code § 351, based primarily on its legislative history, to prohibit respondent from permitting an employer to obtain the benefit of his employee's tips by paying the employee a wage lower than he would be obligated to pay if the employee did not receive tips.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the writ of administrative mandamus granted at the request of appellant employees and directing the respondent Industrial Welfare Commission to vacate its order authorizing a lower minimum wage for employees who received tips. Such a minimum wage system violated a statute intended to protect employees' tips from usurpation by their employers.

45 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Caro


No. S004418, Crim. No. 22461


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 1035; 761 P.2d 680; 251 Cal. Rptr. 757; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 237



October 6, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 1, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 79626, John Schatz, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: One of the multiple-murder special-circumstance findings is vacated, and the judgment is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence in the verdict from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), that convicted defendant of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §187, found two multiple-murder special circumstances were true under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3) and sentenced defendant to death, and admitted evidence of uncharged crimes at the penalty phase of defendant's trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187. The jury found two multiple-murder special circumstances were true under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3) and returned a verdict of death on each murder count. Upon automatic appeal, defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence for the jury to find premeditation and deliberation, the trial court erred in charging him with two multiple-murder special circumstances, and during the penalty phase, evidence of uncharged crimes was admitted in violation of U.S. Const. amend. V. The supreme court vacated one of the multiple-murder special circumstance findings because it was error to charge more than one special circumstance alleging multiple murder. The supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment in all other respects because there was sufficient evidence for a reasonable trier of fact to have found that defendant had planned the murder according to a preconceived design, and because the trial court instructed the jury during the penalty phase to disregard all evidence of the uncharged killings, defendant's failure to testify about them was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated one of the multiple-murder special circumstance findings but otherwise affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence because there was sufficient evidence for the jury to have found that defendant had planned the murder according to a preconceived design, and because the jury was instructed to disregard all evidence of the uncharged killings, defendant's right against self-incrimination was not violated.

 

46 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Reese v. Kizer


No. S002757


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 996; 760 P.2d 495; 251 Cal. Rptr. 299; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 195



September 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H104037-3, Michael E. Ballachey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, director of California Department of Health Services, appealed a decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the trial court's preliminary injunction and judgment granting retroactive benefits, in a class action brought by plaintiffs, spouses of long-term care patients, seeking to implement Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14005.16, which defendant had determined conflicted with the Medicaid Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Under the authority of 1983 Cal. Stat., ch. 1031, § 2, p. 3616, defendant, director of the California Department of Health Services, chose not to implement Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14005.16, because the U.S. Department of Health and Human Services advised defendant that it conflicted with the Medicaid Act, 42. U.S.C.S. § 1396 et seq. Plaintiffs, spouses of long-term care patients, brought a class action suit seeking implementation of § 14005.16 anyway. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction and summary judgment for retroactive benefits. The court of appeal affirmed. The supreme court reversed, holding that Cal. Const. art. III, § 3.5, did not place a restriction on the authority of the legislature to limit the scope of its own enactments, but was intended to prevent administrative agencies from thwarting legislative intent by their own interpretation of the constitution. The court held that by limiting the statute as directed by the legislature, defendant fulfilled its legislative mandate and did not violate the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeal, because the actions taken by defendant, director of the California Department of Health Services, in response to a legislative directive, did not violate the California Constitution, as the legislature's limiting provision permitted defendant to effectuate the state statute to the extent it did not conflict with federal law.

People v. Coleman


No. S004410, Crim. No. 22376


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 749; 759 P.2d 1260; 251 Cal. Rptr. 83; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 209



September 8, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 3, 1988, and on November 2, 1988, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.

DISPOSITION:  The judgment of guilt, the findings of two special circumstances and the judgment of death are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of a decision from Superior Court of City and County of San Francisco (California) convicting him of murder and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was convicted of murder. He contended that the trial court erred in failing to exclude for cause four members of the venire who made it clear that they would automatically vote for the death penalty. The court found that the trial court here improperly denied one challenge for cause in this case. However, the erroneous denial of a challenge for cause to a death-biased juror was harmless error. Defendant also argued that errors at the guilt phase of his trial require reversal of the verdict. The court held that defendant had not met the burden of showing prejudice as a result of his attorney's pretrial performance. Despite the erroneous admission of scientific and expert testimony, the error was harmless on the facts of this case. The wrongly admitted evidence did not point to defendant as the perpetrator of the murder, nor was it the most incriminating evidence in the case. The photos were clearly relevant to the issues raised by the defense. Defendant contended that a number of errors were committed during the penalty phase of his trial. The court affirming the judgment found that none of the errors required reversal on this record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed criminal defendant's murder conviction. The court held that the wrongly admitted evidence was harmless error.

People v. Bonin


No. S004565, Crim. No. 23286


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


46 Cal. 3d 659; 758 P.2d 1217; 250 Cal. Rptr. 687; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 189



August 29, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 19, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C 47500, Kenneth E. Lae, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) convicting him of murder and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. On appeal, he contended that the court erred by denying his request for a change of venue. The court held that defendant failed to carry his burden of proving that the jurors selected for his trial had formed, or would at trial form, such fixed opinions as a result of pretrial publicity that they could not make the determinations required of them with impartiality. The court also held that to establish the constitutional entitlement to additional peremptory challenges argued for here, defendant had to have shown at the very least that in the absence of such additional challenges he was reasonably likely to receive an unfair trial before a partial jury. Defendant's argument of prosecutorial misconduct was also rejected. His claim that it was error for the prosecution to allege four multiple-murder special circumstances instead of one was correct. Three of the four multiple-murder special-circumstance findings had to be vacated. Defendant's claims bearing on the issue of penalty established no reversible error. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed criminal defendant's murder conviction. The court concluded the evidence amply supported the finding. Accordingly, the imposition of the penalty of death on defendant did not violate the Eighth Amendment.

 

49 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Keenan


Crim. No. 22956


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 478; 758 P.2d 1081; 250 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 174



August 25, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 31, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified on September 22 and October 31, 1988, to Read as Printed Above. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.
Writ of certiorari denied Keenan v. California, 490 U.S. 1012, 109 S. Ct. 1656, 104 L. Ed. 2d 169, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 1739 (1989)
Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Keenan, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 3734 (Cal., June 21, 1989)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 100403, Thomas J. Dandurand, Judge.
Keenan v. Superior Court, 31 Cal. 3d 424, 180 Cal. Rptr. 489, 640 P.2d 108, 1982 Cal. LEXIS 183 (1982)



DISPOSITION: Whether viewed singly or in combination, we find no errors warranting reversal of the guilt or penalty verdicts. We therefore affirm the judgment in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that convicted him of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187 and 189, burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459, robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, and attempted robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 664, and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, burglary, robbery, and attempted robbery and was sentenced to death after he and a co-defendant shot and killed an art gallery owner. Defendant appealed from his conviction and sentence, and the court affirmed. One error alleged by defendant was the failure of the trial court to sever his trial from that of his co-defendant. California law preferred a joint trial for jointly charged defendants. Cal. Penal Code § 1098. Severance was largely within the discretion of the trial court. Severance was only to be granted where there was an incriminating confession by a codefendant, prejudicial association with codefendants, likely confusion resulting from evidence on multiple counts, conflicting defenses, or the possibility that at a separate trial a codefendant would give exonerating testimony. Even employing heightened scrutiny given the capital nature of the case, the court failed to find any prejudice mandating severance. The court also found no error in the failure of the trial court to instruct the jury to find intent to kill; this element was not required for felony murder special circumstances where defendant was the actual killer.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder, burglary, robbery, and attempted robbery and sentence to death were affirmed.

People v. McDowell

People v. Freeman


No. S000070


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 419; 758 P.2d 1128; 250 Cal. Rptr. 598; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 171; 15 Media L. Rep. 2072



August 25, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by Freeman v. Superior Court of County of Orange, 1988 Cal. LEXIS 1474 (Cal., Oct. 20, 1988)
Application denied by California v. Freeman, 488 U.S. 1311, 102 L. Ed. 2d 957, 109 S. Ct. 854, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 587 (1989)
Writ of certiorari denied Applegate v. California, 489 U.S. 1017, 103 L. Ed. 2d 194, 109 S. Ct. 1133, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 1031, 57 U.S.L.W. 3551 (1989)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 805936, James A. Albracht, Judge.
 People v. Freeman, 198 Cal. App. 3d 292, 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1267, 233 Cal. Rptr. 510 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., 1987)



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment of the Court of Appeal affirming defendant's pandering conviction based solely on the payment of wages to the actresses in his film, which was not determined to be obscene, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant adult video producer appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which convicted him of five counts of pandering pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §266I, based on the hiring of five actresses who performed sex acts in a film. Defendant was not charged with any violation of the obscenity laws, Cal. Penal Code §311 et seq., in connection with production or distribution of the film.


OVERVIEW: Defendant adult video producer was convicted of five counts of pandering under Cal. Penal Code §266i, after he hired and paid actors to perform in an adult film, which portrayed sexually explicit acts. The court of appeal affirmed his conviction and defendant sought review. The court held that there was no evidence that defendant paid the acting fees for the purpose of sexual arousal or gratification, his own or the actors, and that the payment of acting fees in a non-obscene film was the only payment involved. The court held that defendant did not participate in any of the sexual conduct, thus did not engage in either the requisite conduct nor did he have the requisite mens rea or purpose to establish procurement for purposes of prostitution. The court held that even if defendant's conduct was somehow found to come within the definition of "prostitution" literally, the application of § 266i to the hiring of actors to perform in the production of a non-obscene motion picture would impinge unconstitutionally upon First Amendment values, that the governmental interest be unrelated to the suppression of free expression. The court reversed the court of appeal judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeal judgment convicting appellant of pandering because it was based solely on the payment of wages to the actresses in his film, which was not determined to be obscene. The court found that defendant did not engage in the requisite conduct to be found guilty of pandering and that he did not possess the mens rea for the crime.

Supreme Court of California

53 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Caswell)


S.F. No. 25040


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 381; 758 P.2d 1046; 250 Cal. Rptr. 515; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 173



August 22, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Real Parties in Interest Caswell et al., and Petitioner Grassi for a Rehearing was Denied October 13, 1988. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed. The stays of defendants' respective trials, issued by the lower court and continued by this court, are dissolved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California) that denied his petition for writ of mandate directing the superior court to vacate its order that declared Cal. Penal Code § 647(d) constitutional. Defendant was one 14 persons charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 647(d), which criminalized loitering in or about any toilet open to the public for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting any lewd or lascivious acts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the court of appeal judgment that denied his petition for writ of mandate to direct respondent superior court to vacate its order declaring Cal. Penal Code § 647(d) constitutional. Defendant was charged with violation of § 647(d) for loitering in or about any public toilet for the purpose of engaging in or soliciting any lewd or lascivious acts, and claimed § 647(d) was unconstitutionally vague on its face. The court affirmed the court of appeal judgment holding that § 647(d) was not unconstitutionally vague because although it failed to list actions which, if observed, gave rise to a legitimate inference of the prohibited intent, it provided sufficient guidance to police by requiring the loitering be done with a specific intent and within a designated area. The court held that it clearly proscribed conduct that would subject an individual to prosecution and that by limiting its scope to include only those loitering in a certain place, the likelihood that § 647(d) would permit arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement was thus reduced. The court noted that a potential suspect's sexual orientation was not, in itself, a sufficient to give rise to probable cause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeal judgment that denied defendant's petition for writ of mandate, holding that the loitering statute was not unconstitutionally vague as it clearly prescribed the conduct that would subject an individual to prosecution and was limited in scope to loitering in a certain place with a specific intent.

People v. Boyde


No. S004447, Crim. No. 22584


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 212; 758 P.2d 25; 250 Cal. Rptr. 83; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 164



August 11, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 9, 1988. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Arguelles, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety. The abstract of judgment is ordered corrected to conform to the oral judgment pronounced as to count II (kidnapping for robbery) of life imprisonment, with execution of sentence on that count stayed pending execution of the sentence of death.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the verdict from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), that convicted him of robbery, kidnapping for robbery, and first degree murder, found two special circumstances true and fixed the penalty at death, denied appellant's motion to sever his trial pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code § 1098, and admitted evidence at the penalty phase that did not relate to any of the aggravating factors under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and a co-defendant were jointly charged with robbery, kidnapping, and murder. The trial court denied appellant's motion to sever his trial under Cal. Evid. Code § 1098. The jury found appellant guilty and imposed the death penalty. Upon automatic review, appellant argued that the denial of his motion to sever constituted an abuse of discretion by the trial court, and evidence was improperly admitted at the penalty phase because it did not relate to any of the aggravating factors under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3. The supreme court found that although the defense positions were antagonistic on the issue of the identity of the actual killer, it was undisputed that each appellant and his co-defendant participated in the incident. The supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying severance and appellant was not denied a fair trial by the joint trial, and although certain evidence was improperly admitted at the penalty phase because it did not relate to any of the statutory aggravating factors, it did not affect the verdict or mislead the jury in its penalty determination.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's severance motion and appellant was not denied a fair trial by the joint trial, and evidence that was unrelated to the statutory aggravating factors that was improperly admitted at the penalty phase did not affect the verdict or mislead the jury in its penalty determination.

Davis v. Municipal Court


S.F. No. 24900


Supreme Court of California


46 Cal. 3d 64; 757 P.2d 11; 249 Cal. Rptr. 300; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 160



July 28, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied October 13, 1988. Mosk, J., and Kaufman, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the case is remanded to the Court of Appeal with directions to deny the peremptory writ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff people sought review of the Court of Appeal (California) decision which reversed defendant's conviction for grand theft in proceedings involving Cal. Penal Code § 1001.2(b) relative to defendant's eligibility for diversion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with grand theft. Under Cal. Penal Code § 17(b) grand theft could be charged as a felony or a misdemeanor. The district attorney charged a felony. The municipal court reduced it to a misdemeanor. Thereafter, defendant sought pretrial diversion which was limited to persons charged with a misdemeanor. The municipal court denied defendant's request since she was charged with a felony. Defendant filed a writ of mandate in superior court contending that the local rule making a defendant's eligibility for diversion dependent on the prosecutor's decision to charge a felony or a misdemeanor violated the separation of powers doctrine and equal protection. The superior court denied the writ, and defendant filed a petition for an extraordinary writ in the court of appeal. The court of appeal held the statute unconstitutional as it violated the separation of powers doctrine by authorizing an unconstitutional infringement on the judicial power by the executive branch. The court reversed the judgment and remanded with directions to deny the writ finding that a local misdemeanor program was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority to the district attorney.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case to the court of appeal with directions to deny defendant's writ because there was no constitutional or statutory basis for the court of appeal invalidating the local misdemeanor program or its eligibility requirement as it was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority to the district attorney.

Supreme Court of California

CASE SUMMARY:

Penal Code § 187 and Cal. Penal Code § 189, one count of attempted robbery, and two counts of assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant was sentenced to death.

57 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Brown


No. S004424, Crim. No. 22501


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 1247; 756 P.2d 204; 248 Cal. Rptr. 817; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 151



July 11, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 15, 1988. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR18104, J. William Mortland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We find no error requiring retrial of the issues of guilt or penalty. However, we must reverse the penalty judgment on the basis of the section 190.4(e) error, and we do so. As in Rodriguez, the cause is remanded to the trial court solely for prompt consideration of the automatic motion for modification of verdict. (42 Cal.3d at pp. 794-795.) (See fn. 7.) The trial court's procedure, and the parties' appeal rights from the new judgment shall be as set forth in Rodriguez. 7



7 This case was tried in 1980 before Judge J. William Mortland of the Riverside County Superior Court. Judge Mortland still sits on that court, and he personally should consider the modification motion. This is the correct procedure wherever possible, but practical difficulties arise when the trial judge dies or becomes unavailable before the section 190.4(e) motion has been decided. By analogy to other similar situations, the modification motion may in such event be heard and determined by any other judge of the same court. (§ 1053 [completion of trial]; Code Civ. Proc., § 661 [new trial motion]; People v. Holzer (1972) 25 Cal. App. 3d 456, 463-464 [102 Cal. Rptr. 11] [same].)

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County, California, denying his motion for retrial of the issues of guilt or penalty following his conviction of capital murder and rape.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of the rape and unpremeditated murder of a fifteen-year-old girl, and imposed the death penalty. The U.S. Supreme Court concluded that the standard for the penalty phase instruction to disregard sympathy in a capital trial, pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 190.3, was not unconstitutional. On remand thereafter, the court determined that the trial court failed to fulfil its statutory duty to decide the automatic posttrial motion for modification of the penalty verdict. The court held that in addition to failure to state reasons, the record did not contain the motion for modification of verdict, as required for a valid death judgment, pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 190.4(e). Therefore, the court reversed the death penalty judgment solely for purposes of a limited remand to allow consideration of the modification motion.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for retrial of the issues of guilt or penalty. However, the court held that the death judgment was not valid because the automatic motion for modification of verdict was not decided on the record. It reversed the death penalty judgment solely for purposes of a limited remand to allow consideration of the modification motion.

 

58 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bunyard


No. S004356, Crim. No. 21844


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 1189; 756 P.2d 795; 249 Cal. Rptr. 71; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 154



July 11, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 1, 1988, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.
Subsequent appeal at People v. Bunyard, 2009 Cal. LEXIS 1102 (Cal., Feb. 23, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 31016, Frank S. Kim, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to guilt; the special circumstance finding is upheld; and the judgment is reversed as to penalty.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which entered convictions against him for two counts of first-degree murder after a jury found him guilty of the murders of his pregnant wife and her full-term fetus. The jury found true the special circumstance of multiple murders and sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial, the evidence was uncontroverted that his pregnant wife was murdered by his childhood friend. Defendant's childhood friend testified that defendant hired him to murder defendant's wife. The friend further testified that defendant's plan was to make the murder look like a suicide. The pathologist testified that the infant girl was full-term. The jury found defendant guilty of two counts of first-degree murder, found true the special circumstance of multiple murders, and sentenced him to death. On appeal, defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence of express malice to support his conviction of first-degree murder of the unborn infant and that he was entitled to a sua sponte jury instruction on second degree murder. The court thought that the particular murder scheme made the death of the infant inevitable because of the unique relationship between a mother and her unborn child. The court concluded that the multiple-murder special circumstance was applicable. However, the court reversed the death sentence because the trial court gave an unadorned Briggs instruction, which informed the jury of the governor's power to pardon.


OUTCOME: Defendant's first-degree murder convictions and multiple-murder special circumstance finding were affirmed. The court concluded that the multiple-murder special circumstance was applicable to the killing of a pregnant woman and her viable fetus. The death sentence was reversed because the trial court gave an unadorned Briggs instruction that informed the jury of the governor's pardoning power.

OVERVIEW: Defendant and codefendants were charged with robbery and first degree murder. A felony-murder-robbery special circumstance was also alleged. OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's robbery and first degree murder convictions and his death sentence. The court concluded that the trial court correctly overruled defendant's motion to suppress evidence and found no error that required reversal in any of defendant's other challenges.

Supreme Court of California

June 28, 1988

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety. The petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), which entered convictions against him for murder, burglary, robbery, kidnapping, and rape as well as a finding that the alleged felony-murder special circumstances were true. The trial court sentenced defendant to death. In conjunction with this appeal, defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus.

The jury found defendant guilty of murder, burglary, robbery, kidnapping, and rape. The jury also found true the alleged felony-murder special circumstances and returned a death verdict. The court also rejected defendant's argument that he was denied effective assistance of counsel by being forced to testify in narrative form. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions for murder, burglary, robbery, kidnapping, and rape were affirmed. The court also affirmed the alleged felony-murder special circumstances finding and the imposition of the death penalty. The court concluded that the trial court's decision to grant defense counsel's motion for a change of venue based on pretrial publicity did not require defendant's personal waiver of vicinage.

People v. Robbins


Crim. No. 23149


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 867; 755 P.2d 355; 248 Cal. Rptr. 172; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 131



June 27, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied August 18, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 140615, Ronald C. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), convicting him of murder and kidnapping, and finding special circumstances that the murder was committed during kidnapping and a lewd and lascivious act on a minor.


OVERVIEW: While in custody in an unrelated matter in another state, defendant spontaneously revealed in a tape-recorded confession to killing a young boy in the forum state. His description of the manner of kidnapping and killing the boy, the tools used, and the sexual abuse were consistent with that of the pathologist and other investigators. At trial, defendant sought to demonstrate he confessed falsely. The trial court permitted the prosecution to use evidence of two additional sexually motivated murders of boys in which defendant was involved, in order to establish defendant's intent to engage in the offenses charged. Defendant was convicted of the murder and kidnapping of the boy, with special circumstance of kidnapping and of a lewd and lascivious act on the minor. On appeal, the court held that there was adequate evidence, apart from defendant's admission, that the charged offenses occurred. The court further determined that evidence of other offenses was relevant, and that the offenses were substantially similar to the charged offense to raise an inference that that the recent offense was committed with the charged intent. The court affirmed the conviction and the death sentence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed because there was substantial similarity between evidence of other crimes admitted by the trial court with the charged offenses. Evidence of defendant's involvement in previous sexually motivated murders was admissible to establish that he committed murder and lewd and lascivious act with the charged intent.

People v. Silva


Crim. No. 22546


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 604; 754 P.2d 1070; 247 Cal. Rptr. 573; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 115



June 9, 1988  
June 9, 1988


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied July 28, 1988. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 38495, Joseph B. Campbell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The heinous-murder, witness-murder and financial-gain-murder special-circumstance findings are vacated. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that found him guilty of first degree murder, with special circumstances, and sentenced him to death. Defendant alleged many instances and species of prejudicial error.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and third parties kidnapped a man and woman. The woman was sexually abused, and both victims were eventually shot dead. Defendant was convicted of first degree murder for killing the man and sentenced to death. He raised many issues on appeal. The appellate court vacated the financial-gain murder, heinous-murder, and witness-murder special-circumstance findings. The court ruled that the financial-gain murder finding was not supported because the evidence did not indicate that the man's murder was an essential prerequisite to the financial gain sought by the defendant. The court vacated the witness-murder special finding because there was insufficient evidence to show that the murder was committed to stop the man from testifying against defendant rather than simply being part of the same continuous criminal transaction. The court ruled that the finding that the man's murder was heinous, atrocious, and cruel as defined by the law could not be sustained. The court affirmed all other aspects of the judgement. The court found that the jury's consideration of the vacated special-circumstance findings was harmless error and could not have affected its penalty verdict.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the heinous-murder, witness-murder, and financial-gain-murder special-circumstance findings. The court affirmed the judgment in all other respects. The court upheld the death sentence and ruled that the jury's consideration of the invalid special-circumstance findings was harmless error and could not have affected its penalty verdict.

People v. Siripongs


Crim. No. 23082


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 548; 754 P.2d 1306; 247 Cal. Rptr. 729; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 113



June 6, 1988

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.

Defendant was sentenced to death. 68 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hamilton


Crim. Nos. 21958, 25303, S001870


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 351; 753 P.2d 1109; 247 Cal. Rptr. 31; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 107



May 19, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of all parties for a rehearing were denied July 28, 1988, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 47283, Franklin B. Orfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The petition for writ of habeas corpus in Crim. 25303 is denied. The petition for writ of habeas corpus in S001870 is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This action was before the court on remand from the United States Supreme Court, which vacated the court's prior decision that affirmed the Superior Court of San Diego County's (California) conviction of defendant for first-degree murder, kidnapping, robbery, and burglary, but reversed the trial court's imposition of a death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found driving the victim's van, was in possession of the victim's credit cards, and made incriminating statements as to his role in murdering the victim. The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first-degree murder, kidnapping, robbery, and burglary and affirmed the death sentence. The trial court was not required to instruct the jury that intent to kill was an element of the felony-murder circumstance. The intent to kill instruction was required only when the evidence allowed the jury to find that a defendant aided and abetted the actual killer. The evidence showed defendant was the actual killer or not at all. Defendant's right to self-representation was not violated. Defendant did not have an absolute right of self-representation because his request during the guilt phase jury deliberations was untimely. Although the trial court erred in giving a Briggs instruction concerning commutation of sentence, the error was not prejudicial because the trial court directed the jurors not to use the instruction in determining defendant's sentence. Defendant's contentions for habeas corpus relief, ineffective representation and bias by the trial judge, were without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, affirmed defendant's death sentence, and denied his claims for habeas corpus relief. An intent to kill instruction was not required for purposes of felony murder where defendant was not charged with aiding and abetting the actual killer. The trial court's erroneous Briggs instruction was not prejudicial, as the jury was told not to consider it in rendering defendant's sentence.

People v. Lucky


Crim. No. 22572


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 259; 753 P.2d 1052; 247 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 94



May 16, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied July 21, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A082054, Leslie W. Light, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We find no error warranting reversal at either the guilt or penalty phases of defendant's trial. We therefore affirm the judgment in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) under the death penalty initiative, Cal. Penal Code § 190 et seq., of two counts of first degree murder and attempted robbery with the two special circumstances of murder during the commission of attempted robbery and multiple murder. Following defendant's convictions at the guilt phase, the jury fixed the penalty at death. Defendant's appeal was automatic.


OVERVIEW: Following defendant's convictions of attempted robbery and murder with the special circumstances of murder during the commission of attempted robbery and multiple murder, the jury fixed the penalty at death. His appeal was automatic. The court affirmed, finding no error in the four points raised as to the guilt phase or the nine as to the penalty phase. The court principally ruled that six other robbery counts were properly consolidated with the murder and attempted robbery charges under Cal. Penal Code § 954 because the evidence on each of the joined charges was so strong that consolidation was unlikely to have affected the verdict. The court also ruled that capital defendants did not have an absolute right to appointment of an additional attorney and the trial court, in its discretion, did not have to permit dual counsel because the circumstances did not seem to warrant it. The court ruled further that Cal. Penal Code § 190.3 permitted the penalty jury to consider the defendant's entire record of violent criminal conduct, including the crimes found true at the guilt phase, as well as violent juvenile offenses, and the errors alleged did not cumulatively prejudice defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and death sentence, finding no reversible error in either the guilt or penalty phase of trial. The court affirmed consolidation of six robberies with the murder charge because the evidence on the joined charges was so strong as to have been unlikely to have affected the verdict. The court ruled that his entire criminal record was relevant in the penalty phase, including violent juvenile offenses.

People v. Heishman


Crim. No. 21944


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 147; 753 P.2d 629; 246 Cal. Rptr. 673; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 93



May 9, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 30, 1988, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.
Writ of certiorari denied Heishman v. California, 488 U.S. 948, 109 S. Ct. 380, 102 L. Ed. 2d 369, 1988 U.S. LEXIS 4950, 57 U.S.L.W. 3333 (1988)
Stay granted by In re Heishman, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 443 (Cal., Feb. 1, 1989)
Decision reached on appeal by Heishman v. Ayers, 2010 U.S. App. LEXIS 18744 (9th Cir. Cal., Sept. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 69973, Harold D. Hove, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), where he was sentenced to death after being convicted of first degree murder with special circumstance in that the victim was a witness to a crime who was intentionally killed to prevent her testimony. Appellant's motion for new trial and automatic application for modification of the verdict were denied, and appellant then raised several issues on appeal.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with murdering a woman to prevent her from testifying in a criminal proceeding that had been brought against him for raping her. Appellant was sentenced to death after being convicted of the murder with a special circumstance and filed motions for new trial and automatic application for modification, which were denied. Appellant sought review and asserted numerous issues regarding jury instructions, evidentiary issues regarding testimony and admission of criminal history, and guilt and penalty phase errors. On appeal, the court found that any jury instruction error was harmless. The court found that appellant's criminal history was properly admitted in the context in which it was presented. Evidence that the victim expressed fear of appellant was corroborative of other testimony. There was no prejudice from the trial court's failure to admonish jurors. As to the penalty phase, the court found that the jury was not misled into treating the mere absence of any mitigating factor as an aggravating factor. The court found no error in the admission of other crimes shown as an aggravating factor. The judgment was affirmed accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of murder and the special circumstance finding and found no error in the penalty trial which imposed the death penalty upon defendant. The court did find that the trial judge should have stated reasons for his findings relative to the automatic application for modification of the penalty verdict, however, that omission did not require reversal or remand.

People v. Thompson


Crim. No. 23924


Supreme Court of California


45 Cal. 3d 86; 753 P.2d 37; 246 Cal. Rptr. 245; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 95



April 28, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 23, 1988, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-49758, Robert R. Fitzgerald, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered convictions for first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, forcible rape, Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), and found that defendant used a knife, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b). The jury found one special circumstance, that the murder occurred in the commission of the rape, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(iii), and returned a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, forcible rape, Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), and found to have used a knife in the commission of these crimes, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b). The jury found one special circumstance: that the murder occurred in the commission of rape, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(iii), and returned a sentence of death in the penalty phase. The court heard this matter on automatic appeal pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239. Defendant allegedly killed the victim to prevent her from reporting the rape, which could have prevented defendant from executing a plan to smuggle refugees out of Asia in return for gold. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and the sentence imposed on him. The court affirmed the trial court's decision to admit evidence relating to the plan and also the limiting instructions used by the trial court with respect to evidence that the victim feared defendant. Defendant's challenges to the submission to the jury of both aggravating and mitigating circumstances during the penalty phase, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.3, were held to have no merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions on the charges of first degree murder and forcible rape, and the death sentence imposed on defendant. The court held that the jury was correctly instructed with respect to both aggravating and mitigating circumstances, which were submitted for consideration during the penalty phase. The court also affirmed evidentiary rulings and limiting instructions made during the guilty phase.

 

74 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Evangelatos v. Superior Court


No. S000194


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


44 Cal. 3d 1188; 753 P.2d 585; 246 Cal. Rptr. 629; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 104; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,762



April 21, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Real Party in Interest Van Waters & Rogers, Inc., for a Rehearing was Denied June 23, 1988.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeal is affirmed insofar as it upholds the constitutionality of Proposition 51, but is reversed insofar as it holds that Proposition 51 applies to causes of action that accrued prior to the effective date of the initiative measure.

Each party shall bear its own costs in these proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both parties petitioned for review of a decision of the Court of Appeal (California). Defendants sought a determination that the Fair Responsibility Act of 1986, Cal. Civ. Code, §1431 et seq., applied retrospectively to pending tort litigation, and plaintiff asserted a constitutional challenge to the Act, popularly known as Proposition 51.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after the passage of the Fair Responsibility Act of 1986, Cal. Civ. Code, §1431 et seq., plaintiff's pending personal injury action was assigned for trial. The parties requested the trial court to determine whether the newly revised doctrine applied to the instant case, defendants contesting plaintiff's claims that the legislation was unconstitutional and did not apply retroactively. The intermediate appellate court upheld the statute, concluding that it applied to cases coming to trial after its effective date. The trial court's judgment was affirmed in conflict with a holding of another appellate court. The court granted review, upholding the statute, but refusing to apply the statute retroactively. Retrospective operation would not be given to a statute that interfered with antecedent rights, unless such was the unequivocal and inflexible import of its terms and the manifest intention of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The judgment below was affirmed as to constitutionality, since the right to recover for noneconomic injuries was not immune from legislative revision. The court reversed the judgment as to retrospective application, because the rule was that statutes operated prospectively unless the legislature's manifest intent was otherwise.

 

75 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



McClatchy Newspapers v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24865


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 1162; 751 P.2d 1329; 245 Cal. Rptr. 774; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 85; 15 Media L. Rep. 1529



April 18, 1988 


DISPOSITION: The petitions for writ of mandate or prohibition are denied. All alternative writs are hereby discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A grand jury, real parties in interest, challenged the order of the Court of Appeals (California), which issued a writ of mandate in favor of petitioners, media companies, which directed respondent superior court to release materials disclosed in the grand jury's report that were stricken and sealed. Respondent superior court found the disclosure to be in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 939.1, 939.9.


OVERVIEW: A grand jury, real parties in interest, disclosed raw evidentiary materials gathered during its investigation. Respondent superior court found the disclosure to be in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 939.1, 939.9 and ordered it stricken and sealed. Petitioners, media companies, sought to have the information produced. The appellate court ordered a writ of mandate directing respondent to release the materials. The court upheld respondent's decision and denied the petitions for writ of mandate. Respondent acted properly in view of the required secrecy of grand jury proceedings, the absence of explicit statutory authority for the grand jury to disclose raw evidentiary materials to the public, and respondent's power to ensure that the grand jury stayed within the bounds of its lawful authority. The court held that, because the grand jury failed to make any request for public sessions under § 939.1, the attempt to make wholesale disclosure of the materials constituted an illegal attempt to hold a public session. The court held that raw evidentiary materials did not constitute findings, and mere disclosure of those materials did not constitute a report.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petitions for writ of mandate filed by petitioners, media companies, against respondent superior court, which sought to disclose evidentiary materials in a report by grand jury, real parties in interest. Respondent acted properly in view of the secrecy of grand jury proceedings, the absence of statutory authority to disclose evidentiary materials, and respondent's power to ensure that grand jury stayed within its authority.

Jolly v. Eli Lilly & Co.


S.F. No. 25045


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 1103; 751 P.2d 923; 245 Cal. Rptr. 658; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 86; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,745



April 7, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. P39293, J. Barton Phelps, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. The cause is remanded with instructions to affirm the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendants.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant drug manufacturers appealed from the decision of the Court of Appeal (California) that reversed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment in their favor in an action by plaintiff for personal injuries sustained as the result of her mother taking the drug estrogen diethylstilbestrol (DES) while plaintiff was in utero.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff victim learned that while she was in utero her mother had ingested the synthetic drug estrogen diethylstilbestrol (DES). Plaintiff was diagnosed as having adenosis, a precancerous condition that required careful monitoring. Later, plaintiff had a surgical procedure to remove abnormal tissue. Two years later, plaintiff underwent a complete hysterectomy and a partial vaginectomy in order to remove malignancy. Plaintiff was aware, or at least suspected, that her condition was a result of her mother's ingestion of DES during pregnancy. Plaintiff was unable to identify the particular manufacturer of the drug her mother took, so she did not file suit believing that she had no cause of action. Subsequently, plaintiff brought an action and defendant drug manufacturers moved for summary judgment and the motion was granted. The appellate court reversed and defendants appealed. After review, the court concluded that the suit was time barred because plaintiff could not rely on the Sindell class action suit to toll the statute of limitations. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded to affirm the trial court's judgment in favor of defendants.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded with instructions to affirm the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant drug manufacturers in plaintiff's claim for personal injuries caused by her mother's ingestion of the drug DES while plaintiff was in utero. The court held that plaintiff's action was time-barred.

People v. Wade


Crim. No. 22654


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 975; 750 P.2d 794; 244 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 73



March 24, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:
Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 19, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Broussard, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR38259, Ben T. Kayashima, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt, the finding of one special circumstance, and the judgment of death are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of San Bernadino County (California), which imposed the death penalty following defendant's conviction for first-degree murder accompanied by two special circumstances findings, murder especially cruel and heinous, and torture murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant beat one of his wife's children to death in an attempt to discipline the child. It emerged during subsequent psychiatric evaluation that defendant may have had multiple personality disorder. The court affirmed defendant's first-degree murder conviction and death sentence. The murder especially cruel and heinous special circumstance was reversed because it was unconstitutionally vague. Defense counsel's decision to admit defendant's guilt and focus on defendant's multiple personalities did not constitute ineffective representation. Evidence of defendant's guilt was overwhelming, and defense counsel had little choice but to focus on convincing the jury of defendant's mental defenses. Admission of defendant's prior acts of child abuse was not error. The evidence was substantially relevant because it rebutted the defense theory that it was one of defendant's personalities, instead of defendant, that killed the child. Although the trial court gave an improper no sympathy instruction during the penalty phase, there was no legitimate basis for concluding the jury was misled regarding the propriety of considering defendant's background in making its penalty determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first-degree murder and death sentence. Defense counsel's decision to admit defendant's guilt and focus on defendant's mental deficiencies was not ineffective representation in light of defendant's overwhelming guilt. Admission of prior acts of child abuse by defendant was substantially relevant because it rebutted the defense theory that one of defendant's personalities committed the crime.

People v. Williams


Crim. Nos. 20911, 23904


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 883; 751 P.2d 395; 245 Cal. Rptr. 336; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 74



March 24, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:
Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 9, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. As Modified May 19, 1988. Rehearing Denied May 19, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 9660, Donald L. Fretz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision from the Superior Court of Merced County (California), which convicted him of three counts of first degree murder, found that he was sane during the commission of the offenses, and sentenced him to death. Defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus in conjunction with the appeal, and an order to show cause issued thereon.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of three counts of first degree murder, found that he was sane during the commission of the offenses, and sentenced him to death. Defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus in conjunction with his automatic appeal. On appeal, defendant challenged every phase of his trial, and argued that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during all aspects of his trial. The court reviewed the proceedings against defendant in their entirety. The court found that counsel had defendant enter an insanity plea, and that two psychiatrists examined defendant regarding his capacity to commit the charged offenses. The court found that competent representation did not require counsel to seek repetitive examinations of the defendant until he found an expert who would offer a supportive opinion. Moreover, an inexcusable omission or lapse by counsel would not warrant reversal unless defendant was prejudiced by counsel's conduct. There was nothing in the record to indicate defendant was prejudiced by counsel's conduct. Therefore, the judgment was affirmed and the petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment, as modified to reflect a single multiple-murder special circumstance, and the sentence of death was affirmed. The order to show cause was discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied.

Russ Bldg. Partnership v. City and County of San Francisco


No. S000156


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 839; 750 P.2d 324; 244 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 59



March 17, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 780795, 789365 and 790661, Morton R. Colvin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed as to plaintiffs Crocker and Pacific and affirmed in all other respects. The Court of Appeal shall order those sections of its opinion which do not address the issues disposed of herein to be published in the Official Reports. ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 976(d); see Agricultural Labor Relations Board v. Tex-Cal Land Management, Inc. (1987) 43 Cal.3d 696, 709, fn. 12.) 15



15 Specifically, the court shall not publish part V of its majority opinion, references to the "retroactivity" issue in the first and last paragraphs of the majority opinion, or any of the concurring and dissenting opinion except the first sentence. The court also is directed to correct a typographical error in the majority opinion by changing the "$ 6-per-square-foot" reference in part VI(D)(4) to "$ .60-per-square-foot."


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city and county sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeal (California) holding that its transit impact development fee ordinance could not have applied against plaintiff developers' projects which were under construction when the ordinance passed. Plaintiffs' suit was consolidated with that of developers' whose projects were not under construction, but their petition for supreme court review was denied.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff developers obtained permits from defendant city and county after environmental impact reports required by the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21000 et seq., indicated an adverse impact on public transportation. The permits stated that plaintiffs shall participate in a downtown assessment district, or similar fair and appropriate mechanism, to provide funds for maintaining and augmenting transportation service if defendant established such a mechanism. The ordinance required payment of a transit impact development fee from new developments. The court ruled that plaintiffs were required to pay the fees, holding it was consistent with the condition found in the permits. Furthermore, the court ruled the fees were similar to those from assessment districts, fair because democratically enacted by the board of supervisors, applying without distinction to all new downtown projects, and appropriate by serving the purpose of the condition in the permits. In the court of appeals, plaintiffs' suit was consolidated with that of other developers, but their petition for supreme court review was denied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals with regard to plaintiff developers whose projects were under construction when defendant city and county enacted ordinance requiring transit impact development fee. The judgment was affirmed in all other respects. Plaintiffs' suit had been consolidated with that of other developers, but their petition for supreme court review was denied.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to an automatic appeal upon the imposition of the death penalty, defendant challenged his convictions for first degree murder, burglary, and robbery by the Superior Court of Orange County (California). Penal Code § 190.3.

Defendant argued that the trial court had improperly denied his severance motion, improperly excluded prospective jurors, and improperly instructed on aggravating and mitigating circumstances.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted him of first-degree murder with special circumstances and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering two people with special circumstances and was sentenced to death. On his automatic appeal, he argued errors in the jury instructions.
