In the challenge at the penalty stage, the court found evidence of other crimes and photographs were properly admitted. It also ruled that the jury was properly instructed that it could assign whatever weight it deemed appropriate to the various aggravating and mitigating factors, and that its penalty decision was to be based on all the evidence in the case. Thus, the decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision convicting defendant of felony murder with special circumstances and imposing the death penalty because it found no errors in the jury instructions concerning his guilt as to theft and murder or in the jury's responsibility in weighing aggravating and mitigating factors before imposing the death penalty.

People v. Kimble

44 Cal. 3d 480; 749 P.2d 803; 244 Cal. Rptr. 148; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 34

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to an automatic appeal, defendant challenged his convictions for murder, burglary, robbery, and rape, along with the penalty of death, from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Defendant asserted errors in the guilt, special circumstance, and penalty phases of trial. Defendant challenged a nighttime search, the felony-murder special circumstance instructions, and the trial court's jury instructions during sentencing.

People v. Howard


Crim. No. 22647


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 375; 749 P.2d 279; 243 Cal. Rptr. 842; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 24



February 16, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 21, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 38187, Don A. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt and the judgment imposing the death penalty are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Superior Court, San Bernardino County (California), which convicted him of murder in the first degree, found that he used a firearm in the commission of the offense and intentionally committed the murder for financial gain, and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, and the jury found that he used a firearm and that he committed the murder for financial gain. Defendant was sentenced to death, and he appealed. The court held that defendant's confession was voluntary. Defendant's cellmate was not acting as a government agent; thus, there was no error in admitting his testimony. Defendant's right to confrontation was not violated by the admission of his codefendant's out-of-court statements because they were offered to show codefendant's state of mind. The trial court did not err in failing to give an instruction to clarify the meaning of murder for financial gain. The doctrine of collateral estoppel did not apply due to codefendant's having been found not guilty because defendant's liability was not vicarious. The corpus delicti rule did not apply to the financial gain finding. There was no error in the trial court's exclusion of veniremen for cause based on their beliefs against the death penalty. The untimely notice of the state's aggravating circumstance evidence did not prejudice defendant. There was no error in the penalty phase instructions. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of murder in the first degree and resulting death sentence because his confession was voluntary, there was no error in the admission of his cellmate's testimony or his codefendant's out-of-court statements, the instructions during both phases were proper, and neither collateral estoppel nor the corpus delicti rule applied to the financial gain finding.

 

87 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. May


Crim. No. 24991


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 309; 748 P.2d 307; 243 Cal. Rptr. 369; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 20



February 1, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:
Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 7, 1988. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 147416, James M. Slater, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from a court of appeal (California) that affirmed a trial court's ruling that incriminating statements made by defendant during interrogation were admissible as impeachment testimony.


OVERVIEW: While investigating two crimes, the police arrested defendant. After being advised of his Miranda rights, defendant stated that he wanted an attorney. Without providing him with an attorney, the police interrogated him and elicited various statements indicating that defendant was the perpetrator. Defendant was charged with numerous offenses and pleaded not guilty. Before trial, defendant moved under the California exclusionary rule from case law to bar any use of his statements on the ground that they were obtained in violation of his Miranda rights. Insofar as the motion sought to bar the prosecution from using the statements in its case in chief, it was granted. However, it was denied under the federal law that allowed the statements to be used as impeachment testimony. Defendant was convicted of the crimes charged and the court of appeal affirmed. Defendant contended that California case law authority rendered his statements inadmissible for all purposes. After review, the court concluded that such authority was abrogated by Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d). Thus, federal law governed the case. The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that upheld the trial court's ruling that defendant's extrajudicial statements could be used for impeachment. The court determined that the federal rule allowing impeachment by defendant's prior statements taken in violation of Miranda governed the case.

 

88 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Michael G.


S.F. No. 24917


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 283; 747 P.2d 1152; 243 Cal. Rptr. 224; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 21



January 25, 1988 


DISPOSITION: Applying these standards here, we hold that although the juvenile court ensured petitioner had notice of and understood the court's orders, and properly ordered petitioner's confinement be arranged to prevent contact with section 602 wards, it made no express findings that petitioner's contumacious conduct was egregious or that less restrictive alternatives to incarceration were not feasible. In the present case, of course, the trial court was unaware it must make these specific findings before ordering confinement pursuant to its contempt authority. The judgment of the Court of Appeal is therefore reversed with directions to remand the cause to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile sought a writ of habeas corpus to review the judgment of the Court of Appeal for the Fifth Appellate District (California), which affirmed the order of the juvenile court confining petitioner to a secure facility during nonschool hours for willfully disobeying a court order to attend school.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was adjudged a ward of the juvenile court pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 601(b) as a result of his truancy. As a condition of probation, petitioner was ordered to attend school regularly. Subsequently, petitioner was held in contempt for willfully disobeying this order and was ordered to confinement for 48 hours. The appellate court denied petitioner's request for writ of habeas corpus and/or prohibition. Petitioner then sought a writ of habeas corpus with the supreme court claiming Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 601(b) and 207 prohibited his confinement. The supreme court held that the limitations stated in these statutes were not intended to affect the juvenile court's power to punish a contemptuous § 601 ward with secure detention during nonschool hours. The juvenile court, however, was required to enter express findings that petitioner's conduct was egregious or that less restrictive alternatives to incarceration were not feasible prior to ordering confinement. Since the juvenile court failed to make such findings, the judgment of the appellate court was reversed with directions to remand the cause to the juvenile court for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the appellate court affirming the order of the juvenile court confining petitioner juvenile for 48 hours for his contemptuous conduct because the juvenile court failed to make express findings that his conduct was egregious or that less restrictive sanctions were not feasible. The appellate court was directed to remand the cause to the trial court for further proceedings consistent therewith.

 

89 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Altaville Drug Store, Inc. v. Employment Development Department


No. S000269


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 231; 746 P.2d 871; 242 Cal. Rptr. 732; 1988 Cal. LEXIS 272



January 4, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Calaveras County, No. 12936, Orrin K. Airola, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the case remanded with instructions to affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employer appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed the judgment of the trial court in plaintiff's favor on an appeal from defendant unemployment department's decision to charge plaintiff's reserve account for benefits paid under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256.


OVERVIEW: An employee voluntarily resigned from her job with plaintiff employer due to her impending marriage and relocation with her spouse. She applied for and received unemployment insurance benefits from defendant unemployment department, and plaintiff's reserve account was charged. Plaintiff appealed defendant's decision to charge its reserve account based on Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1032, and the unemployment appeals board affirmed. Plaintiff filed a petition for writ of mandate with the trial court, which was granted. The appellate court reversed the trial court's order, holding that § 1032 required that the employee be married at the time of resignation in order to relieve plaintiff's reserve account of the charges. The court reversed, holding that Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256 applied an expansive definition of spouse to include imminent spouses. The definition of spouse under § 1032 was ambiguous when viewed in conjunction with § 1256. The legislative intent to remedy the hardship imposed on an employer after a "domestic quit" applied equally to situations involving an imminent spouse. Therefore, the definition of spouse under § 1032 included imminent spouses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, and ordered that the judgment of the trial court be reinstated. The court held that plaintiff employer's reserve account should not have been charged because the statute setting forth circumstances in which benefits are not chargeable to the employer's reserve account included an impending marriage and move with an imminent spouse.

People v. Miranda


Crim. Nos. 22787, 25350


Supreme Court of California


44 Cal. 3d 57; 744 P.2d 1127; 241 Cal. Rptr. 594; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 456



November 12, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 7, 1988, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 362694, Kathleen Parker, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt and the judgment imposing the penalty of death are affirmed. Defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of, inter alia, first degree murder with findings that he was armed with and used a firearm, and was sentenced to death by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Appeal was automatic. Defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, which was consolidated with his automatic appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of numerous violent crimes, including first degree murder, with findings that he was armed and used a firearm to commit the murder. Defendant was sentenced to death which provided an automatic appeal. Defendant also filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus. Both actions were consolidated for review. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence, finding that although there were some errors in the proceedings, they were harmless and did not warrant reversal. The court found that defendant received a fair trial on the issues of guilt and penalty. The court denied the petition for habeas corpus on grounds defendant had failed to allege sufficient facts to demonstrate counsel's ineffective assistance or to warrant an evidentiary hearing. The court stated that defense counsel in a capital case was not necessarily compelled to introduce every conceivable item of arguably mitigating evidence and pointed out that, as an appellate court, it would not second-guess the judgment of competent counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of guilt and imposition of the death penalty. Defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied.

 

91 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Ingersoll v. Palmer


S.F. No. 25001


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1321; 743 P.2d 1299; 241 Cal. Rptr. 42; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 451



October 29, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  For the reasons stated, the judgment of the Court of Appeal denying the writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner motorists sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeal (California) that denied their petition for a writ of mandate challenging the constitutionality of sobriety checkpoints.


OVERVIEW: After respondent law enforcement officers began operating sobriety checkpoints, petitioner motorists sought a writ of mandate challenging the constitutionality of the checkpoints. The court of appeal denied their petition, and petitioners sought review. The court affirmed. Searches or stops conducted as part of a general regulatory scheme in furtherance of an administrative purpose, rather than as part of a criminal investigation to secure evidence of a crime, were permissible under U.S. Const. amend. IV without a showing of probable cause provided the search or stop was reasonable. In determining whether an administrative search was reasonable, the need for the inspection had to be weighed in terms of the reasonable goals of code enforcement. The reasonableness of the checkpoint stops turned on factors such as the location and method of operation of the checkpoints. Here, the court determined that the checkpoints were not unreasonably intrusive and therefore not unconstitutional because the operation of the checkpoints was done pursuant to specified neutral criteria. Further, there was an overriding important governmental interest in operating the checkpoints.


OUTCOME: Judgment denying petitioners' request for a writ of mandate challenging the constitutionality of sobriety checkpoints was affirmed. The court concluded that because the operation of the checkpoints was done pursuant to a set of guidelines whose criteria was neutral and because there was an important governmental interest in policing drunk driving, the checkpoints were not unconstitutionally intrusive.

 

92 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Barnes


S.F. No. 25124


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1371; 743 P.2d 915; 240 Cal. Rptr. 855; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 453



October 29, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. D188938, Jay Edgar Thomson Rutter II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed with directions to remand the cause to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the views set forth herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife challenged the order of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant's motion to modify the judgment distributing marital property to include division of respondent ex-husband's military retirement benefits under Cal. Civ. Code § 5124.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-wife and respondent ex-husband divorced after a United States Supreme Court decision rendered military pension benefits separate property not distributable during dissolution of marriage. Appellant received none of respondent's military pension benefits. The Federal Uniformed Services Former Spouses' Protection Act (FUSFSPA), 10 U.S.C.S. § 1408, expressly invalidated that holding, and permitted community property treatment of military retirement benefits. Cal. Civ. Code § 5124 was enacted to permit modification of judgments issued before enactment of FUSFSPA. Appellant's motion to modify her judgment to include respondent's pension benefits was denied by the trial and appeal courts. The appeal court found that § 5124 violated the Supremacy Clause, U.S. Const. art. VI. The court reversed, finding clear legislative intent in FUSFSPA to allow state courts to retroactively apply community property principles in modifying judgments. The court also concluded that respondent's rights to due process were not violated by the retroactive application of § 5124.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order affirming denial of appellant ex-wife's request for modification of the judgment distributing marital assets. The court found that under community property principles which applied retroactively, respondent ex-husband's military pension benefits were marital property subject to distribution.

 

93 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



King v. Meese


L.A. No. 32133


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1217; 743 P.2d 889; 240 Cal. Rptr. 829; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 446



October 26, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C565535, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Our order of December 5, 1985, enjoining enforcement of the provisions of Vehicle Code sections 16028 through 16035 is vacated. The order of the trial court denying the motion for a preliminary injunction is affirmed. The case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a group of uninsured drivers, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied plaintiffs' request for a preliminary injunction to enjoin operation of the 1984 Robbins-McAlister Financial Responsibility Act, 1984 Cal. Stat. 1322, which allowed a police officer to request proof of financial responsibility upon imposing a moving violation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a group of uninsured drivers, filed a motion to enjoin operation of the 1984 Robbins-McAlister Financial Responsibility Act (the 1984 Act), 1984 Cal. Stat. 1322, alleging a violation of procedural due process. The 1984 Act allowed a peace officer to request proof of financial responsibility upon imposing a moving violation. The trial court denied the injunction. The court granted plaintiffs' petition for review. The court affirmed, holding that the state fulfilled its due process duty by providing plaintiffs with insurance at reasonable rates under the California Automobile Assigned Risk Plan (CAARP), Cal. Ins. Code § 11620 et seq. CAARP complied with due process and did not improperly delegate legislative authority to the private insurance industry because, under CAARP, private insurers could not enact rules, but were merely permitted to decide to whom to sell and at what price. The court concluded that because plaintiffs were not likely to prevail on the merits, the trial court's denial of plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of plaintiffs', a group of uninsured drivers, request for a preliminary injunction to enjoin operation of the state's financial responsibility act because the state fulfilled its due process obligations under the statute by providing plaintiffs with auto insurance under a state-sponsored plan.

 

94 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gates


Crim. No. 22263


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1168; 743 P.2d 301; 240 Cal. Rptr. 666; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 445



October 15, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied December 17, 1987, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.
Writ of certiorari denied Gates v. California, 486 U.S. 1027, 100 L. Ed. 2d 236, 108 S. Ct. 2005, 1988 U.S. LEXIS 2285, 56 U.S.L.W. 3805 (1988)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by, Stay granted by Gates v. Woodford (Rohan ex rel. Gates), 2003 U.S. App. LEXIS 12838 (9th Cir. Cal., June 25, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 70866, Stanley P. Golde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, assault with a deadly weapon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 245, robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211 and other offenses and sentenced him to be put to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted first degree murder, assault with a deadly weapon, robbery and other offenses and was sentenced to death after he shot and killed one man and wounded another after robbing them at their home. At defendant's trial, defendant testified he went there to collect money that he claimed was rightfully his for participating in a forgery ring. However, the trial court refused to instruct the jury on the claim of right defense. Defendant appealed from his conviction and sentence, and the court affirmed. The trial court properly refused to instruct the jury on claim of right. Ordinarily, a bona fide belief, even though mistakenly held, that one had a right or a claim to property negated felonious intent and accordingly precluded a finding of theft or robbery. However, one could not have such a good faith belief that he had a right to property when that "right" was rooted in a notoriously illegal transaction. The court also found no merit to defendant's claim of ineffective assistance of counsel stemming from his attorney's failure to object to the admission of the wounded victim's taped statement. There were plausible strategic reasons for his attorney's actions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder, assault with a deadly weapon, robbery and other offense and the imposition of the death penalty were affirmed. Defendant was not entitled to an instruction on the claim of right defense because even though he claimed he went to the home to collect money rightfully his, this money was earned through his participation in an illegal forgery ring.

 

95 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Harbor v. Deukmejian


S. F. No. 24837


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1078; 742 P.2d 1290; 240 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 426



October 13, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified October 20, 1987. The petitions of all the parties for a rehearing were denied November 25, 1987, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above on October 29, 1987.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed insofar as it denies the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate. The cause is transferred to the Court of Appeal with instructions to award reasonable attorney fees to petitioners.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, individuals who applied for aid to families with dependent children grants, sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeal (California) that denied their petition for a peremptory writ of mandate challenging the veto by respondent governor of a part of a bill making budget appropriations.


OVERVIEW: After the legislature enacted a budget bill which also enacted and amended various provisions in numerous code provisions, respondent governor reduced the appropriation made for aid to families with dependent children (AFDC) and further announced he was vetoing a section of the bill which pertained to welfare. Petitioners, individuals who applied for AFDC grants, sought a peremptory writ of mandate that was denied by the court of appeal. Petitioners appealed, and the court affirmed but transferred the case for an award of reasonable attorney's fees to petitioners. The court reiterated that the exercise of the veto power by respondent governor was constitutional. However, in exercising this power, respondent governor could act only as permitted by the state constitution. His constitutional authority was limited to vetoing a bill or reducing or eliminating one or more items of appropriation. The section that was vetoed here was not an item of appropriation; however, the court upheld the invalid exercise of the veto power because the legislature had improperly included the section in the bill contrary to the single subject rule rather than pass the section as a separate bill.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying a request for a peremptory writ of mandate challenging respondent governor's veto of a section of a bill. Though the veto itself was in excess of respondent governor's grant of authority under the state constitution, the court nevertheless upheld the exercise because the legislature improperly included the section in the bill contrary to the single subject rule. The case was transferred with instructions.

People v. Anderson


Crim. No. 21287


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1104; 742 P.2d 1306; 240 Cal. Rptr. 585; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 444



October 13, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied November 12, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. ICR-6219, Frank R. Moore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to guilt; with the exception of one of the multiple-murder special-circumstance findings, the special circumstance findings are upheld; and the judgment is reversed as to penalty.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside (California) that convicted defendant of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 209, and robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211 and sentenced him to be put to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and robbery and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed the conviction but reversed as to the death penalty. At the time of the offense, defendant, his girlfriend, and her brother stopped to assist women whose car had broken down. Defendant and his girlfriend took the women into a grove where they were later found strangled. Defendant and his girlfriend were tried together. Experts testifying on behalf of defendant's girlfriend stated that she told them defendant committed the murders. A nontestifying codefendant's extrajudicial statement inculpating the other defendant was inadmissible as violative of defendant's right of confrontation, even if a limiting instruction was given. The error was harmless because the properly admitted evidence was overwhelming. The court overruled Carlos v. Superior Court, 35 Cal. 3d 131, 197 Cal. Rptr. 79, 672 P. 2d 862, holding that intent to kill was not an element of the felony murder special circumstance. The death penalty was improperly imposed because the trial court erroneously instructed the jury on the governor's commutation power.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder, kidnapping for the purpose of robbery, and robbery was affirmed but the imposition of the death penalty was reversed. The trial court erred by allowing expert witnesses presenting a codefendant's diminished capacity defense to testify she admitted defendant committed the murders; however, the error was harmless in light of the overwhelming evidence of guilt.

Schmidt v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 32110.


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 1060; 742 P.2d 209; 240 Cal. Rptr. 160; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 416



October 1, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Plaintiffs' petition for rehearing GRANTED December 17, 1987. Argument will be limited to the following issue: Does either section 798.76 of the Civil Code or the Unruh Civil Rights Act prohibit defendants from excluding adults under the age of 25 from residing in their mobilehome park?
Plaintiffs shall serve and file a supplemental brief addressing this issue on or before January 15, 1988. Defendants shall serve and file a responsive supplemental brief on or before Febuary 16, 1988. Plaintiffs may serve and file a reply brief on or before February 26, 1988. This Document has been deleted [Deleted 1060-1077]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. SM-40062, Zel Canter, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff purchasers sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied plaintiff's motion for summary judgment, holding that Cal. Civ. Code § 798.76 authorized defendant mobilehome park owner to enforce a rule limiting residence to "adults only."


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff purchasers, challenged the decision of real party in interest, mobilehome park owner, denying plaintiffs' application to purchase a mobilehome because park policy only permitted persons 25 years of age or older to live in the park. The court held that the Mobilehome Residency Law, Cal. Civ. Code § 798.76, permitting parks to enforce rules limiting residence to "adults only" was an exception to the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51 et seq., which prohibited discrimination against families in housing. The court further held that § 798.76 did not violate plaintiffs' right to equal protection because children were not a suspect class entitled to strict scrutiny. Nor did § 798.76 violate the right to privacy because the statute only indirectly burdened plaintiffs' right to live as a family and was rationally related to a legitimate state goal. Section 798.76 was not an invalid restraint on alienation, because it simply required a purchaser to comply with rules limiting residency to adults. Finally, § 798.76 did not improperly delegate zoning duties because the restrictions were imposed by private owners. The court reversed the trial court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals, holding that the mobilehome residency law permitted residency to be restricted to adults only and that the law did not violate the constitutional rights of plaintiff purchasers, whose purchase application was denied by mobilehome park owner, real party in interest, because they were not adults.

People v. Ghent


Crim. No. 21311


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 739; 739 P.2d 1250; 239 Cal. Rptr. 82; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 397



August 13, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied October 1, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 68399, John Schatz, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which imposed the death penalty following defendant's conviction of first degree murder, attempted rape, and assault with intent to commit rape.


OVERVIEW: The trial court imposed the death sentence on defendant after defendant was convicted of first degree murder, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, attempted rape, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 261, and assault with intent to commit rape, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 220. Although defendant's original jury was deadlocked regarding a special circumstances allegation, a subsequent jury found the allegation to be true and returned the death penalty verdict. The court affirmed the judgment. The court found that a Miranda violation occurred but that the error was harmless because the testimony related primarily to defendant's ability to deliberate and premeditate prior to the murder and such evidence was irrelevant to the charges which did not require such mental capacity. The court held that the State took reasonable measures to adequately preserve the evidence. The court held that there was no basis for concluding that the jury failed to give proper mitigating weight to defendant's evidence of his mental state. The court held that no prospective jurors were improperly excused on the basis of attitudes concerning the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

99 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. May


Crim. No. 24991.


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 436; 729 P.2d 778; 233 Cal. Rptr. 344; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 284



Jan. 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted March 26, 1987; 44 Cal.3d 309. Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 147416, James M. Slater, Judge.) The Court of Appeal, Second Dist., Div. Six, affirmed (B004825).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which convicted him of burglary and assault arguing that trial court erred in finding that his statements, taken in violation of Miranda, could be used for impeachment.


OVERVIEW: In the course of their investigation into the two sets of crimes, the police arrested defendant. Immediately after he was advised of his Miranda rights, stated stated: "Before I answer a thing I want an attorney present here." Without providing him with an attorney, however, the police interrogated him and elicited, in addition to denials of involvement in the crimes, various statements that had a tendency to show he was the perpetrator. Prior to trial, defendant moved to bar any use of his statements to the police on the ground that they were obtained in violation of his Miranda rights. The court found that defendant's statements could be used for impeachment purposes. Defendant was convicted of burglary and assault. The ruling that defendant's statements were admissible for impeachment was error. It was beyond dispute that once a defendant had asserted his right to counsel the interrogation must cease. The judgment was reversed because the trial court erred in ruling that defendant's extrajudicial statements were admissible for purposes of impeachment.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for burglary and assault was reversed to permit defendant to make an offer of proof as to what his testimony would have been had he taken the stand, because the trial court erred in finding that the statements taken in violation of Miranda could be used for impeachment.

 

100 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



O'Hare v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 32209


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 86; 729 P.2d 766; 233 Cal. Rptr. 332; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 275



January 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On February 24, 1987, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division One (California), which found that the jury selection process used by the judicial branch was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with kidnapping, assault, wilful cruelty to a child, and false representation of identity to a police officer. Defendant sought to transfer his trial from one judicial branch to another branch contending that the jury selection in the present judicial branch contained a significantly lower percentage of jury-eligible black persons than the other judicial district. The appeals court denied defendant's request, finding the jury selection proper. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. According to the court, U.S. Const. amend. VI right to an impartial jury, was interpreted to entitle a criminal defendant to a jury selected from a fair cross section of the community. The court held that U.S. Const. amend. VI included the requirement that the defendant be tried by a jury drawn from an area which included the location of the crime, or a jury of the vicinage. The court stated that congress was granted the power to determine the actual size of the vicinage by the creation of judicial districts. The court held that the judicial district in the present case was proper because there was legislative authority creating the judicial branches.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment finding the jury selection process was constitutional because the right to a jury selected from a fair cross section of the community also included the location of the crime or vicinage. The court also affirmed because there was legislative authority for the judicial branch to draw jurors from less than the entire county.

 

101 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of Los Angeles v. State of California


L.A. No. 32106


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 46; 729 P.2d 202; 233 Cal. Rptr. 38; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 273



January 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied February 26, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 424301 and C 464829, Leon Savitch and John L. Cole, Judges. The Court of Appeal, Second Dist., Div. Five, affirmed the first action; the second action was reversed and remanded to the State Board of Control for further and adequate findings (B001713 and B003561).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. Each side shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county and city sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals, Third Appellate District, Second Division (California), which held that state-mandated increases in workers' compensation benefits, that do not exceed the rise in the cost of living, were not costs which must be borne by respondent state under Cal. Const. art. XIII B, and its legislative implementing statutes.


OVERVIEW: Proceedings were initiated to determine whether legislation, which increased certain workers' compensation benefit payments, was subject to the command of Cal. Const. art. XIII B that local government costs mandated by respondent state must be funded by respondent. Appellant county and city sought review of the appellate court decision which held that state-mandated increases in workers' compensation benefits, that did not exceed the rise in the cost of living, were not costs which must be borne by respondent under Cal. Const. art. XIII B. On appeal, the court agreed that the State Board of Control properly denied appellants' claims but the court's conclusion rested on entirely new grounds. Thus, the judgment was reversed on a finding that appellants' petitions for writs of mandate to compel approval of appellants' claims lacked merit and should have been denied outright. The court concluded that Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 6 had no application to, and respondent need not provide subvention for, the costs incurred by local agencies in providing to their employees the same increase in workers' compensation benefits that employees of private individuals or organizations received.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeal was reversed in favor of respondent state. The court concluded that appellant county and city's reimbursement claims were both properly denied by the California State Board of Control. Their petitions for writs of mandate seeking to compel the board to approve the claims lacked merit and should have been denied by the superior court without the necessity of further proceedings before the board.

 

102 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Myers


Crim. No. 21991


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 250; 729 P.2d 698; 233 Cal. Rptr. 264; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 279



January 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied April 2, 1987.
Habeas corpus proceeding at Myers v. Ylst, 897 F.2d 417, 1990 U.S. App. LEXIS 2683 (9th Cir. Cal., 1990)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Dismissed by, Judgment entered by Myers v. California, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 9089 (C.D. Cal., Jan. 26, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A524228, Loren Miller, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment as to guilt, and the finding of a special circumstance, are affirmed. With respect to penalty, the judgment is reversed and the case remanded for a new penalty trial before a properly instructed jury. If the prosecution, for any reason, chooses not to retry the issue of penalty, defendant shall be sentenced to life imprisonment without possibility of parole.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, convicted of first-degree murder, robbery, firearm use, and great bodily injury, challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which, after a second jury trial, sentenced him to death. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to grant his motion for mistrial based on the procedure used in selecting the jury panel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant committed two armed robberies of liquor stores and shot the store clerk and the patron. The jury convicted defendant of first degree murder, assault with intent to commit murder, robbery, firearm use, and great bodily injury, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187,189,217,211, and 12022. At the penalty phase, the jury was unable to agree on penalty and a new jury returned a sentence of death. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in failing to grant his motion for mistrial based on the procedure used in selecting the jury panel, based upon a case precedent decided after his case. The court, affirming the trial court's judgment as to guilt and finding of a special circumstance, concluded that the precedent could not be applied retroactively to invalidate convictions returned by juries that were selected and empanelled before that decision was rendered. The court reversed and remanded for a new penalty trial on the basis that the trial court failed to give any supplementary instructions to the jury, which expanded or clarified the potentially ambiguous language of the statute. If defendant was not retried, then he was to be sentenced to life without parole.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions for first degree murder, assault with intent to commit murder, robbery, firearm use, and great bodily injury, as well as finding of a special circumstance. The court reversed and remanded as to the penalty phase and ordered a new penalty trial before a properly instructed jury, directing that if the prosecution chose not to retry the penalty issue then defendant would receive life imprisonment without parole.

 

103 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wade


Crim. No. 22654.


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 366; 729 P.2d 239; 233 Cal. Rptr. 48; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 282



Jan. 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for rehearing Granted, March 26, 1987; Rehg. granted March 26, 1987. Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR-38259, Ben T. Kayashima, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of conviction and sentence for first-degree murder, entered in Superior Court, San Bernardino County (California), which imposed the death penalty based on a finding of two special circumstances, torture-murder and murder especially heinous and cruel. Appellant asserted that he was denied effective assistance of counsel and that the trial court erred in admitting prior acts of abuse and in finding special circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his first-degree murder conviction and death sentence, which was based on two special circumstances. Appellant lived with his wife and her children. One day, appellant beat his wife's 10-year-old daughter with a board across the chest and stomach, killing her. He pleaded not guilty by reason of insanity. His medical experts opined that he had dissociative disorder, multiple personality, or possession syndrome. The state's experts testified that defendant was disturbed but that the multiple personality was malingerered. On review, the court held that appellant was not denied effective assistance of counsel where the failure of counsel's defense theory was no reflection on the quality of the representation. Evidence of prior acts of child abuse was not cumulative and was admissible under Cal. Evid. Code § 352 to refute the multiple personality theory. The conviction was affirmed. The death penalty and the heinous special circumstance finding were reversed based on unconstitutional vagueness. The torture-murder special circumstance was affirmed where the provision was clear and was unlikely to mislead the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's first-degree murder conviction and torture-murder finding, but reversed the death penalty and heinous murder special circumstance. The torture-murder special circumstance was clear and the jury was unlikely to be misled on the issue, but the heinous special circumstance was vague. Admission of prior acts of abuse was proper where it was relevant rebuttal evidence. An ineffective assistance claim was without merit.

 

104 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mitchell v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24790.


Supreme Court of California


43 Cal. 3d 107; 729 P.2d 212; 232 Cal. Rptr. 900; 1987 Cal. LEXIS 276



Jan. 2, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehg. granted March 26, 1987. Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, theater owners and manager, sought a writ of certiorari to review a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) convicting petitioners of contempt pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 11229, a provision of the Red Light Abatement Law, for violation of a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, theater owners and manager, were convicted in a nonjury trial of contempt pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 11229, for violating an injunction restraining petitioners from permitting acts of lewdness or prostitution at their theater. The court annulled the judgment of contempt, holding that § 11229, a provision of the Red Light Abatement Law, provided punishment for a public offense and subjected a defendant to punishment greater that that authorized by general contempt provisions. The court held that, therefore, a § 11229 contempt was a criminal contempt and that, because punishment could be as much as six months in jail, petitioners were entitled to a jury trial under Cal. Const. art. I, § 16. The court ordered a retrial of petitioner theater owners but barred a retrial for petitioner manager because the evidence was not sufficient to prove that he had the authority to stop the conduct prohibited by the injunction. The court also held that petitioners could not be charged with more than one count of contempt for each day that the prohibited conduct took place, because the substance of the contempt was the maintenance of a public nuisance.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the judgment of contempt under the Red Light Abatement Law. The court ordered a retrial for petitioner theater owners because the crime of contempt for which they were charged required a trial by jury under the California Constitution, and barred a retrial for petitioner theater manager because the evidence was insufficient to prove that he had the authority to stop the prohibited conduct.

Sundance v. Municipal Court


L.A. No. 31785


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 1101; 729 P.2d 80; 232 Cal. Rptr. 814; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 304



December 31, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Defendants and Respondents for a Rehearing was Denied February 5, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA 000257, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is affirmed. The cause is remanded for a determination whether County defendants should be enjoined from incarcerating section 647(f) arrestees in prearraignment jail facilities that do not offer medical screening and monitoring by trained personnel.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public inebriates sought review from respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted declaratory and injunctive relief regarding constitutional abuses of the drunk in public statute surrounding conditions relative to the arrest, incarceration, prosecution, and sentencing of the intoxicated and homeless. Appellants argued issues surrounding the enforcement of the statute and the treatment of the accused.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, city, county, court, police, and city attorneys, were accused by appellant public inebriates of unconstitutional practices relative to the enforcement of the drunk in public statute. Appellants filed for and were granted declaratory and injunctive relief regarding constitutional abuses surrounding the enforcement of the statute. Appellants still sought review and asserted error as to the enforcement of the statute and contended that the statute, as applied to chronic, homeless alcoholics, violated the cruel and unusual punishment clause of the state and federal constitutions. On review, the court noted that appellants were not arrested under the statute for conduct that involved the exercise of constitutional rights, and the arrests were not made with the intent of harassment and discouraging exercise of such rights. The court noted that civil detoxification was a legislative prerogative. The judgment of respondent trial court was affirmed. However, the case was remanded for a determination whether respondent county should be enjoined from using pre-arraignment jail facilities that did not offer medical screening and monitoring by trained personnel.


OUTCOME: The judgment of respondent trial court was affirmed because appellant public inebriates were not arrested under the drunk in public statute for conduct that involved the exercise of constitutional rights and appellants' contentions regarding civil detoxification was a legislative issue. However, the case was remanded for a determination whether respondent county should be enjoined from using jails that did not offer appropriate medical care.

People v. Allen


Crim. No. 22879


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 1222; 729 P.2d 115; 232 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 319



December 31, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 2, 1987. Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 11, 1987.
Writ of certiorari denied Allen v. California, 484 U.S. 872, 108 S. Ct. 202, 98 L. Ed. 2d 153, 1987 U.S. LEXIS 3389, 56 U.S.L.W. 3248 (1987)
Stay denied by, Writ of certiorari denied Allen v. California, 487 U.S. 1264, 109 S. Ct. 201, 101 L. Ed. 2d 977, 1988 U.S. LEXIS 3327 (1988)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Allen v. Woodford, 366 F.3d 823, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 8897 (9th Cir. Cal., 2004)
Injunction denied by, Complaint dismissed at Allen v. Hickman, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 39211 (N.D. Cal., Dec. 15, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Glenn County, No. 18240, Roy G. MacFarland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt, the finding of three special circumstances, and the judgment of death are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Glenn County (California), which convicted him, by jury verdict, of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 and solicitation of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 182(1). Special circumstances were found and defendant was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction and death sentence for murder and solicitation of murder was automatically appealed. Defendant was charged with masterminding murders from prison for the purpose of eliminating witnesses who had testified against him or were going to testify against him in the future. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence. The court held that defendant was not denied a fair trial because of a plea bargain between the district attorney's office and defendant's son, a key witness for the prosecution. The court found that photographs introduced to show the location and manner in which the victims were shot were relevant. The trial court did not err in ordering a witness to be restrained during his testimony in light of the evidence regarding his violent, unpredictable personality and the unobtrusiveness of the restraints. Contrary to defendant's claims, the court stated that there was no evidence that any juror consumed any alcohol at any time during the trial. The admission of evidence of uncharged crimes was proper under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3. Defendant's sentence was not disproportionate to the crimes committed, and his constitutional objections were without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence for murder and solicitation of murder. The court found that the plea bargain between the state and defendant's son was proper, that evidence of defendant's uncharged crimes involving violence was admissible, and that the death sentence was not disproportionate to defendant's culpability.

 

107 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Mills v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24947


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 951; 728 P.2d 211; 232 Cal. Rptr. 141; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 296



December 11, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Real Party in Interest for a Rehearing was Denied March 5, 1987.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the petition for peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which denied his petition for writ of prohibition, wherein defendant challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code § 872(b) and (c), which authorized the admission at preliminary hearings of written statements in lieu of testimony of any witness who was not an eyewitness to or a victim of a crime against his person.


OVERVIEW: At a preliminary hearing in defendant's prosecution for auto burglary, the magistrate ruled that Cal. Penal Code § 872, which authorized the admission at preliminary hearings written statements of certain witnesses, was constitutional and held that defendant's telephone call to the prosecutor could not be considered "reasonable efforts" under § 872(c). The magistrate found the prosecution's affidavit to be admissible to show probable cause, found the evidence sufficient to support the information, and denied defendant's motion to set it aside. Defendant sought a writ of prohibition from the appeals court, which denied relief. On further appeal, the court ruled that the "reasonable efforts" requirement violated defendant's Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 due process rights and that the statute improperly lightened the prosecution's task of establishing probable cause by allowing hearsay testimony to be admitted without affording defendant his right to cross-examine the declarant unless he initiated diligent actions to produce his own accuser, but the court held that there remained ample independent evidence to support the information and denied defendant's petition for preemptory writ.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for peremptory writ. The court concluded that although the magistrate erred in admitting a hearsay affidavit of a victim-witness, there was sufficient evidence apart from the affidavit to support the magistrate's finding of probable cause and that the error was harmless.

 

108 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lyons v. Wickhorst


L.A. No. 31991


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 911; 727 P.2d 1019; 231 Cal. Rptr. 738; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 293



December 1, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C327390, Irving A. Shimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We, therefore, reverse the trial court's order dismissing appellant's action and remand for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, landlord's representative, challenged the dismissal of his action for unlawful arrest and false imprisonment against respondents, tenant and others, from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) after the trial court ordered arbitration pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1141.11, and appellant failed to present evidence at the arbitration.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, landlord's representative, filed a lawsuit against respondents, tenant and others, for unlawful arrest and false imprisonment. The trial court ordered arbitration pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1141.11. Appellant did not present any evidence at two arbitration hearings. The arbitrator entered an award for respondents, and appellant requested a trial de novo pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1141.20. Instead, the trial court dismissed the case with prejudice. There was neither notice nor opportunity to be heard. The court of appeal reversed and remanded. The trial court's dismissal was not expressly authorized by statute. While the trial court had the discretionary authority to dismiss actions, this authority was limited by the policy of deciding cases on the merits. The rule was supported by federal law, which held that dismissal was a drastic measure that should be used only where lesser sanctions could not serve the interests of justice. Lesser sanctions could have been entered under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 128.5, which authorized the award of fees and costs for unreasonable delay or failure to participate.


OUTCOME: The dismissal with prejudice of appellant's, landlord's representative's, lawsuit against respondents, tenant and others, for unlawful arrest and false imprisonment reversed. Dismissal under the circumstances was not authorized by statute, and policy favored adjudication on the merits. The trial court could have taken the less drastic step of awarding fees and costs for appellant's delay.

People v. Rodriguez


Crim. No. 22090


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 730; 726 P.2d 113; 230 Cal. Rptr. 667; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 279



October 23, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied December 31, 1986, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-8773, Joseph Karesh, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in all respects, except that the judgment of death is vacated and the cause remanded to the trial court solely for prompt reconsideration of appellant's automatic application for modification of the death verdict (§ 190.4, subd. (e)) under the standards set forth in this opinion. If, after applying such standards, the court again denies the application for modification of verdict, it shall reinstate the judgment of death. If it grants the application, it shall modify the judgment to reflect a penalty of life without possibility of parole. Defendant's appeal from any reinstated death judgment shall be automatic (§§ 190.4, subd. (e), 1239, subd. (b)) and limited to issues arising on the modification application. The People shall have an appeal, similarly limited, from any modified penalty judgment (§§ 190.4, subd. (e), 1238, subd. (a)(6)).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which sentenced him to death for first degree murder of two peace officers performing their duties, under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(7), and denying his application for modification of the death verdict.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder of two peace officers that were performing their duties and sentenced to death, under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(7). The trial court also denied defendant's automatic application for modification of the death verdict. Defendant sought review and the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court did not commit reversible error by limiting defense counsel's cross-examination of prosecution witnesses. The court ruled that the trial court's comments on the evidence when the jury was deadlocked were not improper. The court found that there was no reversible error in the prosecution's remarks on aggravating circumstances during the penalty phase of the trial. The court held that the trial court failed to make independent review of defendant's application for modification as required by Cal. Penal Code § 190.4, and thus vacated the death penalty verdict and remanded for reconsideration of defendant's application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, holding that the trial court did not commit reversible error, but vacated the death penalty verdict because the trial court did not properly consider defendant's application for modification and remanded for reconsideration of that application.

In re Arias


Crim. No. 24307


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 667; 725 P.2d 664; 230 Cal. Rptr. 505; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 273



October 9, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  Accordingly, the YA is ordered to permit wards to attend chapel at the Karl Holton School free from the presence of electronic listening devices. However, since petitioner Arias shows no present right to release from constructive custody and petitioner Bolton is no longer subject to the YA's jurisdiction, the order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied. ( In re Harrell, supra, 2 Cal.3d at p. 706; In re Estrada (1965) 63 Cal.2d 740, 750-751 [48 Cal.Rptr. 172, 408 P.2d 948].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner wards appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which denied a habeas corpus petition to enjoin the installation of electronic listening devices in a chapel at one of respondent Youth Authority's facilities.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner wards were confined at a school facility operated by respondent Youth Authority. Respondent installed electronic listening devices in the facility including the chapel complex. Petitioners filed a petition for habeas corpus to enjoin the installation of the devices in the chapel complex at the facility and also sought a determination that the use of the devices violated petitioners' privacy and religious freedom under the state and federal constitutions. The trial court found that the security system did not constitute a restriction of petitioners' religious freedom and did not infringe on petitioners' religious or privacy rights. The court reversed, holding that the use of the chapel microphone and monitoring system violated Cal. Penal Code §§ 636, 2600 because it was used to eavesdrop on conversations between respondents and their religious advisors. The court also held that petitioners' right to religious freedom under Cal. Const., art. I, § 4, and the free exercise clause of U.S. Const. amend. I was violated. Additionally, the court held that petitioners' right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, and Cal. Penal Code §§ 636, 2600, 2601 was violated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, because the use of the chapel microphone and monitoring system violated state law to the extent it was used to eavesdrop on conversations between petitioner wards and their religious advisors, and because the use of the monitoring system restricted the free exercise of religion to an extent greater than necessary to satisfy the reasonable security concerns of respondent Youth Authority.

 

111 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Bowen v. Bd. of Ret.


L.A. No. 32072


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 572; 724 P.2d 500; 229 Cal. Rptr. 814; 51 Cal. Comp. Cases 639; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 263



September 25, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied October 30, 1986. Lucas, J., and Panelli, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 432732, Bruce R. Geernaert, Judge. The Court of Appeal, 2d Dist. Div. 2, affirmed (No. B004563).



DISPOSITION: In this case, both the trial court and Court of Appeal rejected the DePuy test for a service-connected disability retirement, reasoning that DePuy was decided before the 1980 amendment to section 31720. However, we conclude that the 1980 amendment to section 31720 rejected Heaton only, and did not change the test for service-connected disability. Accordingly, we reverse the Court of Appeal, and direct that court to remand this case to the trial court so that it may apply the proper test of causation for a service-connected disability retirement when it exercises its independent judgment in evaluating the evidence produced before the Board. ( Strumsky v. San Diego County Employees Retirement Assn. (1974) 11 Cal.3d 28, 34 [112 Cal.Rptr. 805, 520 P.2d 29].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of the denial of his writ petition for a service-connected disability retirement under Cal. Gov't Code § 31720 by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) on the ground that § 31720 did not change the former test for service-connected disability.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee applied and was rejected for a service-connected disability retirement under Cal. Gov't Code § 31720 by the county employees' retirement board. Appellant sought a writ petition for the service-connected disability retirement and, upon the trial court's denial of same, appealed. Appellant based his appeal on the contention that rules of legislation construction prevented retroactive application of a statute unless the legislature expressly intended otherwise, and the retroactive application of a stricter standard of causation would unconstitutionally impair his vested contractual right to a pension. The court reversed the denial of the writ, and held that the 1980 amendment to § 31720, which added the qualification of "contributes substantially to such incapacity" to the test for permanent incapacity retirement for public servants, did not change the existing law. Since the legislature intended to require more than an infinitesimal connection between the employment and the disability, but something less than the principal connection, the court remanded the case to the trial court to apply the proper causation test in evaluating the evidence produced by the board.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant employee's writ of petition for a service-connected disability retirement from appellee retirement board of county employees' retirement association, because it concluded that the amendment to the government code dealing with service-connected disability retirement did not change the causation test . The court remanded the case to the trial court to apply the proper causation test.

 

112 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Huffman


Crim. No. 24654


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 552; 724 P.2d 475; 229 Cal. Rptr. 789; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 261



September 22, 1986 



DISPOSITION:  Petitioner has demonstrated no right to the relief he seeks. Hence, the Court of Appeal's judgment denying a writ of habeas corpus is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed a judgment of the California Court of Appeal which denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus alleging a violation of equal protection after petitioner was denied conduct and participation credits under Cal. Penal Code §§ 2930-2935.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner inmate was convicted of child rape and committed to a hospital for treatment as a mentally disordered sex offender (MDSO) pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 et seq. Petitioner was found unamenable to treatment and returned to court for sentencing. At the sentencing hearing, petitioner claimed his sentence should have been reduced by conduct and participation credits under Cal. Penal Code §§ 2930-2935. The trial court refused to grant the credits, and the decision was affirmed by the appellate court. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus, claiming a violation of equal protection because narcotics offenders in rehabilitation programs were allowed conduct and participation credits. The court denied the writ, holding that that the statutory exclusion of conduct and participation credits from computation of a MDSO's term of commitment did not deny petitioner equal protection of the laws when compared with criminals directly sentenced to state prison. The court found a compelling interest in public safety served by the MDSO statute's rule of exclusion. The court noted the primary purpose of a MDSO commitment was treatment as an alternative to punishment.


OUTCOME: Petitioner inmate's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied. The court held that the statutory exclusion of conduct and participation credits from computation of a mentally disordered sex offender term of commitment was not violative of equal protection because it was based on a compelling interest in public safety.

 

113 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Martin


Crim. No. 24474


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 437; 722 P.2d 905; 229 Cal. Rptr. 131; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 231



August 21, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied September 18, 1986. Lucas, J., and Panelli, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, Nos. 71690 and 74219, John R. Kennedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons the order imposing sentence is reversed, and the case remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion. 13



13 People v. Herrera, supra, 127 Cal.App.3d 590, People v. Mitchell, supra, 152 Cal.App.3d 433, and People v. Shepeard, supra, 169 Cal.App.3d 580, are disapproved to the extent that they are inconsistent with the views stated herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which affirmed a 12-year sentence for 7 robberies. Defendant argued that he should have been resentenced to the 5-10 year term recommended by the Board of Prison Terms after the Board found, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1170(f), that defendant's sentence was disparate when compared to sentences imposed in similar cases.


OVERVIEW: After he committed seven robberies, defendant was sentenced for five years for the principal robbery with a one-year deadly weapon enhancement, and six consecutive terms of one year for the remaining six robberies. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1170(f), the Board of Prison Terms (Board) found the sentence was disparate in comparison to sentences imposed in similar cases, and that subordinate prison terms were limited to a total of five years. The appellate court affirmed the order that denied the board's motion for resentencing to a term between 5 and 10 years. The court reversed the sentence, and held that a statement of articulated reasons was required to support a decision that reaffirmed a disparate sentence. The court reasoned that a two-step approach that determined whether a sentence was disparate and if it should be recalled did not fully satisfy a trial court's obligation. Specific findings were required so a decision about a disparate sentence could be attacked on specific grounds and reviewed for abuse of discretion by appellate courts. Accordingly, the matter was remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was reversed, because neither the trial court's finding that the sentence was not disparate, nor its refusal to impose the reduced sentence recommended by the Board of Prison Terms, was supported by any reason in the record. The case was remanded so the trial court's ruling could be reconsidered and the requisite findings issued with respect to the sentence imposed upon defendant.
