Wilks v. Mouton


No. S.F. 24814


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 400; 722 P.2d 187; 229 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 229



August 21, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-275654, John F. Cruikshank, Jr., Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant voters challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which held that there was no violation of Cal. Const. art. II, § 7 or of any mandatory provisions of the Elections Code in the election to incorporate the community of East Palo Alto, and confirmed the passage of the incorporation measure.


OVERVIEW: Appellant voters sought to invalidate an election in which a measure to incorporate East Palo Alto was passed by 15 votes, on the grounds that there were irregularities in the handling of absentee ballots. The trial court confirmed the passage of the measure, and the court affirmed, finding that Cal. Elec. Code § 1007 did not prevent election officials from mailing ballots to voters at addresses other than their residences. The court found that the secret voting provision of Cal. Const. art. II, § 7 was not violated because, in each case where someone had assisted a voter in completing an absentee ballot, the assistance had been provided at the voter's request, had been provided without fraud or coercion, and all disclosures had been made voluntarily by the voter. The court found that the trial court's finding that there had been no fraud, coercion or tampering was supported by substantial evidence. Because there was substantial compliance with the essential provisions of the absentee voter provisions, the court held that it would not deprive the individuals who cast the challenged ballots of the exercise of their fundamental right to vote.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's confirmation of the passage of the measure to incorporate East Palo Alto. The court found that there was substantial compliance with the essential provisions of the absentee voter law, that receiving help in completing a ballot did not render that ballot unconstitutional, and that the court would not deprive individuals who cast the challenged ballots of the exercise of their fundamental right to vote.

 

115 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Pennell v. City of San Jose


S.F. No. 24767


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


42 Cal. 3d 365; 721 P.2d 1111; 228 Cal. Rptr. 726; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 227



August 11, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 435458, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it declares the tenant hardship provisions facially unconstitutional. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a judgment of the Santa Clara Superior Court (California) declaring the tenant hardship provisions of defendant's rent control ordinance facially unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city enacted a rent control ordinance to address the city's housing situation. The ordinance allowed a landlord to raise his rents according to the highest of any four methods of computation during a 12-month period. Thereafter, plaintiff landlords and association filed suit challenging the constitutionality of the ordinance. The trial court found that the ordinance's tenant hardship provisions were facially unconstitutional because they allowed a hearing officer to disallow a rent increase that would otherwise be supported by the initial six factors of the fourth method for calculating a rent increase. Defendant sought appellate review, and the appeals court reversed the judgment insofar as it declared the tenant hardship provisions facially unconstitutional. The court reasoned that the hearing officer was required merely to "consider" and "balance" the tenant hardship evidence; nothing in the ordinance precluded him from granting an increase necessary to assure the landlord a fair return and thus avoid confiscatory results despite any resulting hardship. The court also noted that the ordinance granted an automatic eight percent increase to all landlords.


OUTCOME: The appeals court reversed the trial court judgment insofar as it declared the tenant hardship provisions of the rent control ordinance facially unconstitutional. The court ruled that the ordinance did not preclude those who administered it from avoiding confiscatory results, because a hearing officer could consider and balance the evidence, and still grant a rent increase despite any resulting hardship.

 

116 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Perkey v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


S.F. No. 24708


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


42 Cal. 3d 185; 721 P.2d 50; 228 Cal. Rptr. 169; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 213



July 24, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 307275, Ronald W. Tochterman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, this matter is remanded to the trial court, with directions to fashion appropriate declaratory and/or injunctive relief in light of the relevant statutory privacy provisions. In all other respects the judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate seeking an order compelling defendant Department of Motor Vehicles to grant plaintiff a driver's license, even though she would not submit to mandatory fingerprinting because she claimed that such a procedure violated her right to privacy.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff driver petitioned for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Department of Motor Vehicles to renew her driver's license after she refused to be fingerprinted. Fingerprinting, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 12800(c), was mandatory before receiving a driver's license. The trial court denied plaintiff's petition, and plaintiff sought review, arguing that requiring the submission of a fingerprint in a statewide identification system violated the right to privacy, and that Cal. Veh. Code § 12800(c) was unconstitutional because a nexus had not been established between the fingerprint requirement and highway safety. The court, using the rational basis test, disagreed, stating that the fingerprinting requirement had lowered the number of fraudulent licenses. However, as the requirement infringed on the right to privacy, the court held that under Cal. Civ. Code § 1798.1, the right to privacy was a fundamental right, and fingerprinting was personal information, the dissemination of which was strictly limited by Cal. Civ. Code § 1798.3. The court also noted that under Cal. Veh. Code § 1808.5, all records related to the physical attributes of a person were to be kept confidential.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court with directions to fashion appropriate declaratory and/or injunctive relief. The court stated that fingerprint records should be kept confidential due to privacy statutes. However, the court affirmed the trial court's finding that the department of motor vehicles could require fingerprints as a condition of receiving a driver's license.

 

117 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Casas v. Thompson


L.A. No. 32131


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 131; 720 P.2d 921; 228 Cal. Rptr. 33; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 199



July 21, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 455959, G. Dennis Adams, Judge. The Court of Appeal, Fourth Dist., Div. One, affirmed except as to the award of disposable pay, holding that the award for the period after the filing of the petition should have been based on defendant's gross pay (No. D001019).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife and respondent ex-husband challenged the decision of the Court of Appeal, Fourth District, Division One, (California) which affirmed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted wife a portion of respondent's retirement pay from the date of appellant's petition.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-wife requested partition of respondent ex-husband's pension that was omitted from the property division in the divorce action. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court and held that appellant was entitled to 30 percent of respondent's gross retirement pay from the date she filed the partition action. It decided that the award must be based on gross pay, not disposable income, because disposable income would vary greatly among retirees with the same gross retirement pay since the statutorily specified deductions were under the control of the retiree, and there was no legitimate governmental interest to support such disparate treatment. The court held that the concept of disposable pay or income had nothing to do with the characterization of an asset. It found that it was not an abuse of discretion to not award payments from the date respondent began receiving retirement income because any facts relevant to the fairness of such payments could be considered, and it upheld the finding that it would be inequitable to force respondent to compensate appellant for her past community share after he provided the sole support for their children during minority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granting appellant ex-wife part of respondent ex-husband's gross retirement pay from the date the partition action was filed. It held that the award was to be based gross pay to avoid disparate treatment of retirees' ex-spouses and held it was not an abuse of discretion for the award to be tailored to provide benefits from the date of the petition, not from the date respondent began receiving retirement income.

 

118 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Star-Kist Foods, Inc. v. County of Los Angeles


L.A. No. 31918


Supreme Court of California


42 Cal. 3d 1; 719 P.2d 987; 227 Cal. Rptr. 391; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 186



June 30, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 28, 1986. Lucas, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C2280083, Julius M. Title, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant importer challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied appellant's suit for refund of taxes paid to respondent municipalities, after respondents refused to apply a state statutory exemption. Respondents asserted that the statutory exemption was invalid in that it violated the commerce clause, U.S. Const. Art. I, § 8, cl. 3, by discriminating against interstate commerce.


OVERVIEW: Appellant importer paid ad valorem personal property taxes to respondent municipalities. Respondents sought a refund of taxes paid on inventory that was imported claiming an exemption under Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 225. Respondents claimed the exemption violated the commerce clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant's refund claim. The court ruled that respondents had standing to raise a commerce clause challenge. The court determined the tax was constitutional if it was applied to an activity with a substantial nexus with the taxing state, was fairly apportioned, did not discriminate against interstate commerce, and was fairly related to the services provided. The court found that the exemption applied both to inventories of foreign companies shipping through California, and of domestic companies involved in importing or exporting. The court ruled that if domestic companies could use § 225's tax exemption to gain an advantage over domestic competitors operating within the U.S., then discrimination against a distinct class of interstate commerce was occurring, and the exemption violated the commerce clause.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the challenged statutory tax exemption violated the Commerce Clause because it unfairly discriminated against domestic corporations, a distinct class of interstate commerce. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied appellant importer's application for refund of ad valorem personal property taxes, paid to respondent municipalities.

Long Beach City Employees Assn. v. City of Long Beach


L.A. No. 32051


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 937; 719 P.2d 660; 227 Cal. Rptr. 90; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 184; 1 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 465; 105 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,633; 40 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P36,309



June 19, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-429898, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying CEA's request for a preliminary injunction to halt administration of the polygraph examination is therefore reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, city employees association, sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied appellant's request for a preliminary injunction to prevent respondents, city and others, to compel certain public employees to undergo polygraph examinations because it violated their rights of privacy and equal protection under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, § 7, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, city employees association, sued on behalf of a group of public employees who asserted that orders by respondents, city and others, to submit to polygraph examinations, in order to get to the bottom of certain thefts at the place of employment, violated their rights of privacy and equal protection under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, § 7, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The trial court denied appellant's request for a preliminary injunction. On appeal, the court found respondents failed to demonstrate that the classifications drawn by Cal. Lab. Code § 432.2 and Cal. Gov't Code § 3307 were justified by a compelling governmental interest. Public employees were not second-class citizens within the ken of the constitution. The court concluded that the orders to the employees to submit to polygraph examinations as a condition of their employment intruded upon the employees' constitutionally protected zone of individual privacy and also violated their right to equal protection under the law. The judgment denying appellant's request for a preliminary injunction to halt administration of the polygraph examinations was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied appellant's request for a preliminary injunction to halt administration of the polygraph examinations because to compel certain public employees to undergo such examinations as a condition of employment violated their rights of privacy and equal protection under the state and federal constitutions.

 

120 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Young v. Haines


S.F. No. 24775


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 883; 718 P.2d 909; 226 Cal. Rptr. 547; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 181



June 5, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Glenn County, No. 18092, Noel Watkins, Jr., and Bruce H. Thomas, Judges. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of dismissal is reversed and the case remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Glenn County (California) dismissed plaintiff's medical malpractice action against defendant health care providers when plaintiff failed to file an amended complaint alleging facts which would toll the statute of limitations under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.5. Plaintiff appealed, arguing that the tolling period of Cal. Civ. Code § 29 applied.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff child brought a medical malpractice action against defendant health care providers, alleging birth injuries. Defendants demurred on the grounds that the statute of limitations, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.5, barred plaintiff's claims, and the court dismissed plaintiff's claim. Plaintiff appealed, arguing that the court should have applied the tolling period of Cal. Civ. Code § 29. The court reversed the dismissal and remanded the case for plaintiff to allege facts giving rise to tolling under § 340.5. The court disapproved of the holding in Kite v. Campbell, 142 Cal.App.3d 793, that a common law tolling provision could be read into § 340.5. Rather, the court held the tolling period of the second sentence of § 340.5 itself was applicable to minors, despite the specific tolling period given minors in the fourth sentence of § 340.5. Such a reading avoided the equal protection problems posed by allowing adults a concealment tolling period, but not minors. The court remanded to allow plaintiff to state facts amounting to concealment if she could.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of plaintiff's action and remanded the case to give plaintiff the opportunity to allege intentional concealment so as to toll the statute of limitations. The court held that the later, more specific statute of limitations applied. Within the statute, the court held that the tolling provisions of the second sentence applied to minors, because such a construction avoided an equal protection violation.

Coleman v. Gulf Ins. Group


L.A. No. 31916


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 782; 718 P.2d 77; 226 Cal. Rptr. 90; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 175; 62 A.L.R.4th 1083



May 22, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NEC 34119, Robert M. Olson, Judge. The Court of Appeal, Second Dist., Div. One affirmed in part and reversed in part (No. 68723).



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant survivors challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed their suit against respondent insurer. Appellants' action alleged that respondent instigated a frivolous appeal in order to delay and coerce a settlement with the tortfeasor.


OVERVIEW: Appellant survivors filed a claim for wrongful death against the tortfeasor and obtained a judgment. The tortfeasor filed an appeal and a settlement was later reached. Appellants filed suit against respondent, tortfeasor's insurer, alleging that it had instituted the meritless appeal in order to delay and coerce settlement. The trial court dismissed the action and appellants sought review. The court affirmed the dismissal, holding that in the absence of specific statutory authorization, appellants could not bring an independent action on the basis of respondent's alleged bad faith prosecution of the earlier appeal. The court noted that appellants could have requested sanctions under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 907 in the initial appeal, and had the alleged facts been established, damages could have been awarded. The court held that the potential "chilling effect" on appeals would be greatly exacerbated if every appellant faced the prospect that a jury might impose additional damages in a subsequent action based on its assessment of his or her motive in prosecuting the appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of appellant survivors' claim for damages against respondent insurer based on a frivolous appeal. The court held that, in the absence of explicit legislation authorizing an independent cause of action for malicious appeal, appellants' remedy was to request sanctions for a frivolous appeal.

Building Industry Assn. v. City of Camarillo


L.A. No. 32097


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 810; 718 P.2d 68; 226 Cal. Rptr. 81; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 176



May 22, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, Nos. 74162, 77065, William L. Peck, Judge. The Court of Appeal, Second Dist., Div. Seven, affirmed (No. B004233).



DISPOSITION: The portion of the Court of Appeal opinion which holds that Evidence Code section 669.5 does not apply to growth control ordinances enacted by means of the initiative process is reversed. The balance of the opinion is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developers challenged the summary judgment granted by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) and affirmed by the appellate court, in favor of respondent city, in appellants' suit challenging the growth control ordinance enacted by respondent. Appellant argued that the ordinance violated Cal. Gov't Code §65863.6, and that the burden of proof should have been shifted to respondent.


OVERVIEW: A growth control ordinance was passed in respondent city by voter initiative. Appellant developers filed suit challenging the ordinance. The trial court refused to shift the burden of proof to respondent pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code § 669.5 and refused to hold that Cal. Gov't Code § 65863.6, which required a balancing between the need for housing and public services concerns, applied to ordinances passed by initiative. By agreement between the parties, a stipulated judgment was entered in favor of respondents, and appellant challenged the trial court's holdings regarding the applicability of the statutes to initiatives. The appellate court affirmed the trial court, and appellants filed an application for writ. The court granted the writ and affirmed the trial court on its finding that Cal. Gov't. Code § 65863.6 did not apply to initiatives, and reversed the trial court's finding that Cal. Evid. Code § 669.5 did not apply to initiatives. The court reviewed the legislative history of Cal. Evid. Code § 669.5, and held that the legislature intended the rule to apply to initiatives, and that there was no undue burden on local governments to require them to defend initiatives.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and affirmed in part. The court held that there was a presumption of harm to those in need of housing in growth control regulations, and the burden of proof was on the local government to show the necessity of the growth control, even when the ordinance was passed by initiative. The court held that an ordinance passed by initiative was exempt from the statutory balancing test.

People v. Thomas


Crim. No. 24713


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 837; 718 P.2d 94; 226 Cal. Rptr. 107; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 178



May 22, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 91047, Paul R. Teilh, Judge. The Court of Appeal, First Dist., Div., Five affirmed (No. A026533).



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment and sentence entered by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted him of rape. Defendant asserted that the trial court erred when it imposed a sentence based upon serious felony enhancements.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court's imposition of an enhanced sentence under Cal. Penal Code § 667. Defendant asserted that his admission of prior convictions of burglary was insufficient because he did not specially admit that the prior burglaries were committed in a residence. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and sentence. The trial court properly sentenced defendant to four five-year enhancements because defendant admitted to prior burglaries that were serious felonies. The burglaries did not have to be on a residence to be serious felonies. The admissions were binding because they were made voluntarily by a defendant who the court assumed had been informed of his constitutional rights by his counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment enhancing defendant's sentence for a criminal conviction, because the trial court's enhanced sentence was proper, because defendant admitted he had been convicted four times of burglary that were serious felonies. Defendant's admission was sufficient to establish that the burglaries were serious felonies.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prosecution sought review of the order of the Superior Court of San Bernardino county (California), which denied extradition of defendants to the state of Louisiana. Prosecution petitioned the court for a writ of mandate to order the trial court to vacate its order.


OVERVIEW: Defendants father and grandfather challenged extradition to Louisiana on charges of kidnapping based upon an underlying child custody dispute. OUTCOME: The court denied prosecution's petition for writ of mandate and affirmed the trial court's order because the indictment, which was part of the extradition papers from Louisiana, did not charge defendants father and grandfather with a crime.

 

125 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Figueroa


Crim. No. 24252


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 714; 715 P.2d 680; 224 Cal. Rptr. 719; 1986 Cal. LEXIS 171



April 7, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 78425, James Duvaras, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeal of California that reversed the convictions of two co-defendants for allegedly selling unqualified securities in violation of Cal. Corp. Code §§ 25110 and 25540.


OVERVIEW: Defendant businessmen were charged with selling unqualified securities, in violation of Cal. Corp. Code §§ 25110 and 25540, after an investor paid them $ 2,500 in exchange for a "corporation promissory note." Defendants claimed the transaction was a private offering, exempted from qualification under Cal. Corp. Code § 25102. At trial, the jury was instructed that defendants had the burden of proving the transaction was exempted, but the trial court failed to specify the requisite degree of proof. The trial court also instructed the jury, over objection, that the promissory note was a "security" as defined by California corporate securities law. Defendants were convicted, and they appealed. In reversing, the court held that the failure to instruct the jury on the proper degree of proof was reversible error. The court further held that the giving of the "security" jury instruction was tantamount to an unconstitutional directed verdict on that essential element of the offense. Whether defendants had sold a "security" was an issue for the jury to decide and which the state had the burden of proving beyond a reasonable doubt. It was reversible error to give the instruction.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded for retrial because the trial court committed reversible error by failing to instruct the jury as to the proper degree of proof on defendants' affirmative defense that offering was exempted from qualification and by directing the verdict by instructing the jury that a promissory note involved in the transaction was a "security" under California corporate securities law.

In re Marriage of Fabian

DISPOSITION:  We conclude, therefore, that the statute may not be applied to these proceedings. The judgment of the trial court is affirmed. 13



13 Statements to the contrary in the following cases are disapproved: In re Marriage of McDole (1985) 169 Cal.App.3d 175 [215 Cal.Rptr. 116]; In re Marriage of Palmer (1985) 168 Cal.App.3d 190 [214 Cal.Rptr. 79]; In re Marriage of McNeill (1984) 160 Cal.App.3d 548 [206 Cal.Rptr. 641]; Taylor, supra, 160 Cal.App.3d 471; In re Marriage of Benart (1984) 160 Cal.App.3d 183 [206 Cal.Rptr. 495]; In re Marriage of Huxley (1984) 159 Cal.App.3d 1253 [206 Cal.Rptr. 291]; In re Marriage of Koppleman (1984) 159 Cal.App.3d 627 [205 Cal.Rptr. 629]; In re Marriage of Martinez (1984) 156 Cal.App.3d 20 [202 Cal.Rptr. 646]; In re Marriage of Buford (1984) 155 Cal.App.3d 74 [202 Cal.Rptr. 20]; In re Marriage of Anderson (1984) 154 Cal.App.3d 572 [201 Cal.Rptr. 498]; In re Marriage of Neal (1984) 153 Cal.App.3d 117 [200 Cal.Rptr. 341].

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered an interlocutory judgment of dissolution of marriage, and found, inter alia, that a certain rental property and its income were community property. The court concluded that because appellant failed to evidence any intent, by written or oral agreement during marriage, that his contributions to the community asset were to remain separate property, the law in effect at that time presumed a gift to the community.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's holding that certain rental property and its income were community property. The court found appellant's separate property contribution to the property constituted a gift to the community. Because appellant failed to evidence any intent that his contributions to the community asset were to remain separate property, the law in effect at that time presumed a gift to the community.

People v. Walker


Crim. No. 21707.


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 116; 711 P.2d 465; 222 Cal. Rptr. 169; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 444



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing granted March 20, 1986. This Document has been deleted. [Deleted 116-143]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 73790, John Schatz, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) which convicted him of various crimes including first-degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced to death for various crimes including first-degree murder. Special circumstance findings as to murder, robbery, and assault with the intent to commit murder were found true following two separate robberies in which defendant shot four people, one of whom died. Defendant raised issues on appeal concerning jury selection, the guilt phase, and the penalty phase. The court affirmed the convictions and the special circumstances, holding that the use of peremptory challenges was proper. The denial of defendant's motion to sever counts from the two separate crimes was proper because the offenses were properly joined under Cal. Penal Code § 954. The trial court's failure to instruct the jury of the required intent to kill for the felony murder special circumstance was not prejudicial because the jury's finding of guilt as to assault with intent to commit murder necessarily included a determination of intent. The court reversed death judgment because the admission of evidence during the penalty phase was without timely notice, and the jury was not instructed on the use of character and background evidence in the death penalty determination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and the special circumstance findings as to murder, robbery, and assault with the intent to commit murder because there was no error in the jury selection, defendant's motion to sever offenses was properly denied, and the failure to instruct the jury of the required intent for the felony murder special circumstance was not prejudicial. The death judgment was reversed due to error in the penalty phase.

 

128 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hamilton


Crim. No. 22311.


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 211; 710 P.2d 937; 221 Cal. Rptr. 858; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 446



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing granted March 20, 1986. This Document has been deleted. [Deleted 211-246]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 25591, William R. Channell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) imposing death. Defendant was convicted of attempted robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, and three counts of first-degree murder. Special circumstances as to each murder count found two multiple murders, based on the two other murder verdicts, and the fact that the murder was committed in the course of a robbery or attempted robbery.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of attempted robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, and three counts of first-degree murder. Special circumstances as to each murder count included two multiple murder findings, based on the other two murder verdicts, and a finding that the murder was committed during the course of a robbery or attempted robbery. After the trial court imposed the death penalty, defendant's appeal was automatic. In reversing the judgment of death, the court held that the special circumstance findings had to be set aside because the jury was not instructed to find that defendant had the requisite intent to kill. The court found that none of the exceptions to the rule of per se reversal applied since defendant did not concede the issue of intent and was not acquitted of any charge affected by the erroneous instructions. Moreover, since defense had received a pretrial ruling that intent was not an element of the special circumstance allegations, the parties did not realize that intent was an issue and did not present all the evidence at their command on that issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of death because the special circumstance findings had to be set aside since the trial court failed to instruct the jury to find that defendant had the requisite intent to kill. The trial court's erroneous omission affected the outcome of the special circumstance findings, and none of the exceptions to the rule of per se reversal applied.

 

129 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lucky


Crim. No. 22572.


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 315; 710 P.2d 959; 221 Cal. Rptr. 880; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 449



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing granted February 20, 1986. This Document has been deleted. [Deleted 315-352]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 082054, Leslie W. Light, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of two counts of first degree murder and attempted robbery with two special circumstances and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder and attempted robbery with two special circumstances. The jury fixed the penalty at death. On appeal, the court found no error affecting the guilt portion of defendant's trial. However, the court held that, during the penalty phase, the court's failure to give an instruction on the duty of the jury to weigh mitigating character and background evidence was error. The court declared that capital juries had to be permitted to consider any mitigating evidence in making an individualized determination of the appropriate penalty in a particular case. The court reasoned that instructions which did not implement this constitutional imperative of individualized determination denied defendant the procedural safeguards demanded by the U.S. Const. amend. VII. The court also held that it was improper for the trial court to instruct the jury to reach a just verdict regardless of what the consequences of such verdict might be. The court reasoned that such an instruction should not be given to a jury because it allowed the jury to disregard sympathy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree murder and attempted robbery with two special circumstances. However, the court reversed the sentence of death and held that the penalty phase was affected by errors that were sufficient to create a reasonable possibility of affecting the verdict.

 

130 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Lybarger v. City of Los Angeles


L.A. No. 32002


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 822; 710 P.2d 329; 221 Cal. Rptr. 529; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 436



December 31, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 448021, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied his petition for peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondents, city and police department, to set aside the administrative decision removing him from his position as a police officer, pursuant to the Public Safety Officers Procedural Bill of Rights Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3300 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officer was discharged from his position for insubordination when he refused to answer questions posed at an administrative interview conducted by respondents, police department and city, as a result of a criminal investigation. After a hearing, an administrative board found appellant guilty of insubordination and recommended that appellant be removed from his position with loss of pay. The chief of police adopted the board's recommendation. Appellant filed a petition in court for peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondents to set aside the administrative decision. The trial court denied the petition, finding that there had been no deprivation of appellant's due process right at the interrogation and that his removal was justified because he had refused to testify. The court reversed on appeal, holding that, although appellant had neither a constitutional nor a statutory right to remain silent free of administrative sanction, respondents never advised appellant that any statements he made could not be used against him in a subsequent criminal proceeding as required by Cal. Gov't Code § 3303(g).


OUTCOME: The judgment denying appellant police officer his petition for peremptory writ of mandate to order respondents, city and police department, to set aside their decision to discharge him from his job was reversed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings. Appellant was never advised that any statements he made during the administrative interview could not be used against him in a subsequent criminal proceeding.

 

131 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Leach


Crim. No. 21586


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 92; 710 P.2d 893; 221 Cal. Rptr. 826; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 443



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied January 30, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 250977-6, Wilbur K. Kessler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to penalty. The special circumstance finding pursuant to section 190.2, subdivision (a)(18), is set aside, and the judgment of guilt is affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's appeal was automatic after a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) which imposed the death penalty following convictions for first-degree murder and robbery. The jury found that defendant used a deadly weapon and found special circumstances that the murder was intentional, involved the infliction of torture, and was committed while defendant was engaged in or was an accomplice in the robbery's commission.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court convicted defendant of first-degree murder and robbery and imposed the death penalty, defendant's appeal was automatic. A jury found special circumstances that the murder was intentional, involved infliction of torture, and was committed while defendant was engaged in or was an accomplice in the commission of the robbery. In reversing the judgment as to the death penalty, the court set aside the torture-murder special circumstance finding due to the trial court's failure to instruct the jury that defendant had to have intended to inflict extreme pain. In concluding that such omission was reversible error per se, the court noted that none of the exceptions to the rule of reversible error per se applied. The court found that the strong evidence of defendant's intent to kill militated against a finding of intent to inflict pain. The court also held that the trial court committed reversible error by foreclosing the jury, during the penalty phase, from considerations of sympathy and compassion when there was evidence in mitigation, including defendant's age, family situation, and lack of prior felony convictions. The court affirmed judgment in all other aspects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment as to the death penalty because the trial court committed reversible error per se in failing to instruct the jury that defendant had to have intended to inflict extreme pain to support a finding of a torture-murder special circumstance. The trial court also erred in precluding the jury from considering evidence of sympathy at the penalty phase. The court affirmed all other aspects of the judgment.

 

132 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Silbertson


Crim. No. 22357


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 296; 709 P.2d 1321; 221 Cal. Rptr. 152; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 448



December 31, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 176461, Hugh Rose III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to the special circumstance finding and penalty. On the issue of guilt, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder and robbery and sentenced him to death. Defendant also appealed the special circumstance determinations that the murder was committed during a robbery and that defendant used a firearm in the commission of both felonies.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of killing a former lover of his wife during a robbery. The trial court sentenced defendant to death after finding that special circumstances existed because the murder was committed during a robbery pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i) and that defendant used a firearm in the commission of both the robbery and the murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5. The court affirmed the judgment as to guilt, but set aside the robbery-murder special circumstance. The court held that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury that it had to find that defendant intended to kill in order to find the robbery special circumstances to be true. The court noted that, due to an anomaly in the law, defendant could be sentenced to death for an accidental killing during a robbery but not for a premeditated murder. Accordingly, the defense strategy de-emphasized evidence that the gun was accidentally discharged. The court noted that defendant did not present all of the evidence available that might have suggested that the killing was unintentional. The court also noted that much of this evidence came from the prosecution's witnesses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of guilt, but set aside the robbery-murder special circumstance. The court held that it was per se reversible error for the trial court to fail to instruct the jury that it had to find defendant intended to kill in order to find the robbery special circumstances to be true. The court also noted that there was significant evidence that defendant did not intend to kill the victim.

 

133 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Deere


Crim. No. 22878


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 353; 710 P.2d 925; 222 Cal. Rptr. 13; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 450



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied February 14, 1986. Lucas, J., and Panelli, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. ICR-7552, Fred R. Metheny, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to penalty and affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) which imposed the death penalty following his conviction of one count of first-degree murder and two counts of second-degree murder, accompanied by a finding of a multiple-murder special circumstance.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court convicted defendant of first and second-degree murder, accompanied by a finding of a multiple-murder special circumstance, the trial court imposed the death penalty. Defendant appealed, contending that the trial court erred in failing to hold a hearing to determine his competence to plead guilty and to waive a penalty jury. In affirming the convictions, the court held that a competence hearing was not mandatory because defendant did not introduce substantial evidence of his incompetence. The court held that defendant's acts of waiving a penalty jury and pleading guilty to a capital offense did not constitute substantial evidence of incompetence. In reversing the sentence of death, the court held that the trial court violated the fundamental public policy against misusing the judicial system to commit a state-aided suicide when it permitted defendant, who wanted to die, to bar his counsel from introducing mitigating evidence at the penalty phase. Moreover, the defense counsel's honest but mistaken belief that he had no right to infringe upon his client's decisions about his own life operated to deny defendant his right to the effective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the trial court's judgment that imposed the death penalty because the public policy against state-assisted suicides prohibited defendant from barring his counsel's introduction of mitigating evidence at the death penalty phase. The court affirmed the convictions because the trial court did not err in denying a competence hearing since defendant failed to introduce substantial evidence of his incompetence.

People v. Balderas


Crim. No. 21979


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 144; 711 P.2d 480; 222 Cal. Rptr. 184; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 445



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On March 27, 1986, the opinions were modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, Nos. 21050, 21672, 20849, 19490, Marvin E. Ferguson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt is upheld as to all counts. The single special circumstance finding -- that defendant committed a first degree murder while engaged in a robbery -- is set aside. Accordingly, the judgment of death is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Kern County (California) convicting him of capital murder, finding that he committed first degree murder while engaged in a robbery, and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and assessed the death penalty. On appeal, the court ruled that the decision to consolidate the trial and to deny severance was not improper. It also ruled that defendant was not prejudiced by the denial of his motion for a change of venue. The court ruled there was no error or prejudice in the trial court's restricting his counsel's voir dire of the jury panel, nor in the exclusion from jury of persons unable to impose death penalty. However, the court reversed the sole "special circumstance" determination--that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in a robbery--because the jury was not instructed that this special circumstance required a finding of specific intent to kill. Thus, the sentence to death was reversed and the matter remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court upheld defendant's conviction as to all counts. However, the single special circumstance finding that he committed a first degree murder while engaged in a robbery was set aside. Accordingly, the judgment of death was reversed and the cause was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

People v. Davenport


Crim. No. 22356


Supreme Court of California


41 Cal. 3d 247; 710 P.2d 861; 221 Cal. Rptr. 794; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 447



December 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied January 30, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-44790, Phillip E. Cox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed. However, the instructions at the penalty phase were in error under the decisions of People v. Robertson, supra, 33 Cal.3d 21, People v. Easley, supra, 34 Cal.3d 858 and Lockett v. Ohio, supra, 438 U.S. 536, and People v. Brown, supra, 40 Cal.3d 512. On this record the errors may have misled the jury as to its proper function and there is, therefore, a reasonable possibility that the penalty verdict was affected. Accordingly, the sentence of death is reversed and the cause is remanded for a new penalty trial consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) convicting him of murder and imposing the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Criminal appellant was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. Appellant made three challenges to the finding that the murder was committed under special circumstances. He contended that torture-murder special circumstance was unconstitutional. He contended that the instructions to the jury defining the special circumstance were inconsistent with the definition of first degree murder by torture and that the combination was likely to have misled the jury on the vital matter of the truth of the special circumstance. In addition, he contended that the special verdict returned by the jury failed to meet the requirements of Cal. Penal Code §§ 190.2 and 190.4 (a). The court affirmed the judgment that the special circumstance was reasonably susceptible of a construction which was consistent with constitutional mandates, that the trial court correctly instructed the jury, and that the special verdict met statutory requirements. However, the court held that the instructions at the penalty phase were in error and might have misled the jury as to its proper function. The sentence of death was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed criminal appellant's conviction at the guilt phase of the trial. However, the instructions given at the penalty phase were in error and might have misled the jury as to its proper function. The sentence of death was reversed and remanded for a new penalty trial.

Cranston v. City of Richmond


S.F. No. 24834


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 755; 710 P.2d 845; 221 Cal. Rptr. 779; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 433



December 30, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 213317, Robert Joseph Cooney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-employee sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) which denied his petition for writ of administrative mandamus to compel his reinstatement as a police officer with appellee city/employer. Appellee had terminated appellant for "conduct unbecoming to an employee of the City Service" pursuant to Richmond, Cal., City Personnel Rule XII(2)(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-employee, who was warned that his conduct while driving was under observation, drove his police car without lights in the middle of the night and led fellow police officers on a wild chase over wet and slippery streets at a high speed. Pursuant to Richmond, Cal., City Personnel Rule XII(2)(a), appellee city/employer discharged appellant for this conduct. After the trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of mandamus seeking to compel his reinstatement, appellant challenged, contending that Rule XII(a)(2), which allowed appellee to discharge police officers for "conduct unbecoming an employee of the City Service," was unconstitutionally vague. In affirming, the court held that Rule XII(a)(2), when viewed in the context of other rules and practices regarding police service, was not so vague as to preclude discipline for appellant's conduct. The court construed the term "employee of City Service" to mean a police officer and construed "conduct unbecoming" to refer to conduct indicating a lack of fitness to perform the functions of an officer. The court found that appellant's reckless and unlawful conduct fell within the purview of conduct proscribed by the Rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment denying appellant ex-employee's petition for writ of mandamus to compel his reinstatement as a police officer with appellee city/employer because the city rule, which provided for discharge of police officers for "conduct unbecoming an employee of City Service," was not unconstitutionally vague when viewed in the context of other police service practices and rules.

Canaan v. Abdelnour


L.A. No. 32013


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 703; 710 P.2d 268; 221 Cal. Rptr. 468; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 431; 69 A.L.R.4th 915



December 30, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged, and the petition for peremptory writ is denied. ( United Farm Workers of America v. Superior Court (1975) 14 Cal.3d 902, 906, 914 [122 Cal. Rptr. 877, 537 P.2d 1237]; Ramirez v. Brown (1973) 9 Cal.3d 199, 203, 217 [107 Cal. Rptr. 137, 507 P.2d 1345]; Zeilenga v. Nelson, supra, 4 Cal.3d at p. 724.) Petitioners shall recover costs herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners challenged respondent city's municipal code prohibiting write-in voting in municipal elections.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city enacted regulations governing the conduct of municipal elections. The regulations prohibited write-in voting in all primary and general municipal elections. Petitioners filed an original petition for writ of mandate seeking to compel respondent city clerk to accept petitions for write-in candidates. The trial court transferred the case to the court of appeal, which upheld the regulations. Subsequently, petitioners sought a hearing in the court. Petitioners argued that respondent city's ban on write-in candidates and write-in voting violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions. Respondent argued that the ban was permissible because the regulation of municipal elections in charter cities was a strictly municipal affair. The court held that although the city's power to regulate municipal elections was broad, that fact was not relevant to a constitutional challenge. The court found that the right to seek public office and the right to the unrestricted exercise of the franchise were fundamental. Moreover, the court held that those rights were protected by U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2.


OUTCOME: The court held that respondent city's municipal code prohibiting write-in voting in municipal elections was invalid under the state and federal constitutions.

People v. Massie


Crim. No. 21003


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 620; 709 P.2d 1309; 221 Cal. Rptr. 140; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 426



December 23, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 98806, Daniel M. Hanlon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: On counts I and II the judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to strike from its records defendant's pleas of guilty and his admissions of charged enhancements and special circumstances allegations. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of a death sentence for the crime of murder, entered by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, following appellant's entry of a guilty plea to charges over his counsel's objections.


OVERVIEW: Appellant plead guilty to charges of murder, robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, and felon in possession of a concealable weapon. The trial court accepted appellant's guilty pleas, despite the fact that appellant's counsel indicated that the pleas were against the advise of counsel. The trial court sentenced appellant to death, and the court reversed the death sentence. The court held that, under a recent amendment to Cal. Penal Code § 1018, a trial court could not accept a guilty plea to a capital charge without the consent of counsel. While the court observed that defense counsel did not formally withhold consent, it was clear from his comments that he did not agree with appellant's decision to plead guilty. Relying on a recent decision interpreting the amendment to § 1018, the court found that a pre-amendment decision holding that a defendant need not take his counsel's advise in choosing to plead guilty to a capital offense, no longer applied. Moreover, the court determined that defense counsel only agreed not to withhold his consent to appellant's guilty plea because he was unaware of his obligation to exercise independent judgment over his client's decision to plead.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment of conviction for the crime of murder, admissions of charged enhancements, and sentence because the trial court erred in accepting appellant's guilty plea for a capital offense over the objection of counsel. The court directed the trial court to strike appellant's guilty plea and admissions associated with the murder charge. The court affirmed all other aspects of the trial court judgment.

In re Kevin G.


Crim. No. 24086


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 644; 709 P.2d 1315; 221 Cal. Rptr. 146; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 428



December 23, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Merced County, No. 14599, George C. Barrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of judgment from the Superior Court of Merced County (California) which determined that respondent juvenile's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol was constitutionally infirm because he was denied his right to counsel after electing to proceed informally before a traffic hearing officer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state challenged the trial court judgment holding that respondent juvenile who elected to proceed informally before a traffic hearing officer was entitled to appointed counsel and that an adjudication under Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(a) was constitutionally infirm as the result of the denial of appointed counsel. The court affirmed the trial court judgment holding that a minor charged with a traffic misdemeanor had a right to counsel, including a right to appointed counsel if he could not afford to retain counsel. The court found that respondent, however, was told that he could have counsel appointed to represent him only if he elected to proceed in juvenile court where he would be subject to greater penalties, therefore his decision under the circumstances to forego counsel and have his case heard by the traffic hearing officer was not a valid waiver of the right to counsel, and the resulting conviction was constitutionally infirm.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that held that respondent juvenile's conviction for driving under the influence was constitutionally infirm because he was denied his right to counsel. The court held that respondent was told that he was entitled to counsel only if he proceeded in juvenile court where he could be subjected to higher penalties, thus his waiver of right to counsel was not voluntary.

Waters v. Bourhis


S.F. No. 24611


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 424; 709 P.2d 469; 220 Cal. Rptr. 666; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 415



December 5, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied January 23, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 447221, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Because of the limited ground of the reversal, each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former client appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant attorney in plaintiff's excessive-fee lawsuit.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former client appealed a trial judge's order granting defendant attorney summary judgment in plaintiff's excessive-fee lawsuit. The court reversed. Plaintiff had retained defendant after she was sexually molested by a health care provider, and she then sued him on both intentional tort and negligence grounds. When the case settled, defendant took a fee that on its face exceeded the statutorily permitted fee for medical malpractice actions. The court held that defendant's fee was not improper under the statute, since the intentional tort claim he brought was not covered by the fee limitations statute, which covered only lawsuits for medical negligence. Nevertheless, summary judgment was inappropriate since plaintiff asserted she was not informed that the fee limitations statute would not apply when an intentional tort lawsuit was brought against a health care provider. Given that factual dispute, the matter was remanded to the trial judge for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed, and the case was remanded. Summary judgment in favor of defendant attorney was inappropriate in plaintiff former client's excessive-fee claim, because it was not clear whether or not plaintiff knew that the fee limitations statute would not apply if an intentional tort lawsuit was brought against her health care provider.

 

142 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Brown


Crim. No. 22501


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


40 Cal. 3d 512; 726 P.2d 516; 230 Cal. Rptr. 834; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 420



December 5, 1985, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 30, 1986, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Lucas, J., and Panelli, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted. Previously Reported at: 220 Cal. Rptr. 637; 709 P.2d 440.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-18104, J. William Mortland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment as to guilt, and the finding of a special circumstance, are affirmed. The penalty judgment is reversed. 19



19 The parties and other persons have asked us to adopt a specific jury instruction to guide the jury's determination of the penalty in future capital cases. We have been advised that the Committee on Standard Jury Instructions, Criminal has drafted a proposed instruction to be inserted into CALJIC No. 8.84.2. It directs the jurors that:

"The weighing of aggravating and mitigating circumstances does not mean a mere mechanical weighing of factors on each side of an imaginary scale, or the arbitrary assignment of weights to any of them. You are free to assign whatever moral or sympathetic value you deem appropriate to each and all of the various factors you are permitted to consider. In weighing the various circumstances you simply determine under the relevant evidence which penalty is justified and appropriate by considering the totality of the aggravating circumstances with the totality of the mitigating circumstances. To return a judgment of death, each of you must be persuaded that the aggravating evidence [circumstances] is so substantial in comparison with the mitigating circumstances that it warrants death instead of life without parole."

We do not adopt the exact language of this instruction, which, in any case, is subject to revision by CALJIC before it is finally adopted. By way of guidance to the trial courts, however, we believe it appropriate to state that this language, if inserted to replace the language in the current third paragraph of CALJIC No. 8.84.2 (which says that if aggravating circumstances outweigh mitigating circumstances the jurors "shall" return a verdict of death), would conform to our opinion.

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted and sentenced defendant for rape with the infliction of great bodily injury and first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of rape with the infliction of great bodily injury and of first degree murder. The jury made a special finding that the murder was premeditated. A special circumstance that the murder was committed in the course of a rape was found true. Defendant admitted allegations that he had suffered a prior conviction and prison term for rape. Defendant challenged his convictions and sentence. He raised several claims of error at the guilt and special circumstance phase of his trial, and he objected to instructions that the jury must not be swayed by sympathy or consequences in choosing a sentence. The court affirmed the convictions, but it reversed the penalty judgment. The court held that the alleged evidentiary errors were harmless because of the overwhelming incriminating evidence against defendant. However, the court reversed the penalty judgment, because prior caselaw authority prohibited the giving of antisympathy instructions at a capital penalty trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded. Although defendant was properly convicted for rape and murder, because the alleged evidentiary errors were harmless, defendant was improperly sentenced, because prior caselaw authority prohibited the giving of antisympathy instructions at a capital penalty trial.

County of Sonoma v. State Energy Resources Conservation etc. Com.


S.F. No. 24377


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 361; 708 P.2d 693; 220 Cal. Rptr. 114; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 411



November 18, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner having agreed to withdraw its request for review of the Energy Commission's decision if we rule that section 25531 is valid, that decision is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought review of a decision by respondent State Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (California) granting a public utility certification of a geothermal power plant and related electric transmission line. Petitioner contended that the judicial review provisions of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 25531 were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner county claimed numerous defects in a decision by respondent State Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission in its decision granting real party in interest, a gas and electric company, certification of a geothermal power plant and an electric transmission line. In the alternative, petitioner prayed that the court deny its petition for lack of jurisdiction on the ground that judicial review provisions of Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 25531 were unconstitutional. Petitioner contended that because Cal. Const. art. XII did not specify respondent or any other commission or agency except the Public Utilities Commission (PUC), it did not allow for the judicial review provisions of § 25531. The court affirmed and found that the close relationship between the functions of the PUC and the narrow class of decisions by respondent affected by § 25531, brought § 25531's judicial review provisions within the broad legislative authority over PUC matters conferred by art. XII. Since § 25531 was a means of implementing and facilitating the PUC's licensing of thermoelectric power facilities, its enactment was authorized by art. XII.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent State Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission, granting a certification of a geothermal power plant and electric transmission line. The court found that the statutory provision giving the court exclusive jurisdiction of decisions by respondent was not unconstitutional and appellant county agreed to withdraw its request for review upon a determination of validity of the statute.

Cohen v. Board of Supervisors


S.F. No. 24873


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 277; 707 P.2d 840; 219 Cal. Rptr. 467; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 406



October 31, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 27, 1985. Grodin, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 784036, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since it is appropriate that the Court of Appeal first determine whether, on the remaining issues, the trial court abused its discretion in denying appellants' application for a preliminary injunction, the cause is retransferred to the Court of Appeal, First Appellate District, Division Two. ( Taylor v. Union Pac. R.R. Corp., supra, 16 Cal.3d at p. 895; see ante, at p. 290.) Each side shall bear its own costs in this portion of the appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a taxpayer and an attorney, challenged an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), denying their application for a preliminary injunction against the enforcement of an ordinance regulating local escort services enacted by respondent municipality.


OVERVIEW: Respondent municipality enacted an ordinance regulating local escort services. Appellants, a taxpayer and a lawyer, filed an action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief based on the claim that the ordinance was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amends. I, IV, VI, and XIV. The trial court declined to grant the requested relief, and appellants sought review. The court transferred the cause to the Court of Appeal for a determination of whether the trial court abused its discretion in denying appellants' application for preliminary injunction. However, the escort service ordinance was not unconstitutional. The ordinance was preempted by state law to the extent that it prohibited escorts from engaging in criminal conduct, or from aiding and abetting such conduct. In all other aspects, the ordinance was not in conflict with the general laws of California. Additionally, the requirement that a license had to first be obtained before conducting a business or activity had long been recognized as a valid exercise of the state's police power.


OUTCOME: The court transferred the cause to the Court of Appeal for a determination of whether the trial court abused its discretion in denying an application by appellants, a taxpayer and an attorney, for a preliminary injunction against an ordinance enacted by respondent municipality. However, the ordinance was not unconstitutional.

Isbister v. Boys' Club of Santa Cruz

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On December 10, 1985, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 19, 1985. Mosk, J., Lucas, J., and Kaus, J., * were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 69410, Christopher C. Cottle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant boys' club challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which found that appellant's membership policy violated the Unruh Civil Rights Act, Cal. Civ. Code § 51, and permanently enjoined appellant from denying membership or access to its facilities to girls because the policy caused harm to the rejected girls, and deprived members of a nondiscriminatory environment.


OVERVIEW: Respondent girls were denied access to appellant boys' club membership and facilities solely on the basis of their gender. Respondents filed an action for injunctive and declaratory relief. The trial court found that the appellant's membership policy violated the Unruh Civil Rights Act (Act), Cal. Civ. Code § 51, and permanently enjoined appellant from denying membership or access to its facilities to girls. Appellant challenged the injunction on the basis that the club was not among those business establishments covered by the Act. The court affirmed. The court held that there was no substantial evidence that appellant's programs, services, and facilities were unsuitable for girls, or that inclusion of both sexes in the programs would diminish their value or effectiveness. Nor was there proof that female memberships would cause serious and permanent danger to appellant's funding or its relationship with its national organization. The court held that appellant was a business establishment covered by the Act, and that its male-only membership policy was an arbitrary form of discrimination prohibited by that statute.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Appellant boys' club was properly enjoined from denying membership or access to its facilities to girls, because appellant was a business establishment whose male-only membership policy was an arbitrary form of discrimination prohibited by statute, appellant's programs, services, and facilities were not unsuitable for girls, and inclusion of both sexes in the programs would not diminish their value or effectiveness.

 

146 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Conservatorship of Valerie N.


S.F. No. 24745


Supreme Court of California


40 Cal. 3d 143; 707 P.2d 760; 219 Cal. Rptr. 387; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 401



October 21, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 100974, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Inasmuch as there was neither evidence of necessity for contraception, nor sufficient evidence that less intrusive means of contraception are not presently available to Valerie, the judgment is affirmed. The affirmance is, however, without prejudice to a renewed application for additional powers at such time as appellants have available adequate supporting evidence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant conservators challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) denying appellants' petition for authorization to have a tubal ligation (salpingectomy) performed on respondent, their adult developmentally disabled daughter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant conservators sought authorization from the court to have a tubal ligation (salpingectomy) performed on respondent, appellants' adult developmentally disabled daughter. The probate court denied the petition for authorization on the ground that the court lacked jurisdiction to grant the petition. Appellants challenged the judgment and contended that Cal. Prob. Code § 2356(d) deprived respondent of her constitutional right of procreative choice by precluding the only means of contraception realistically available to her. Cal. Prob. Code § 2356(d) provided that no ward or conservatee could be sterilized under the provisions of that code division. The reviewing court found that the legislative scheme did absolutely preclude the sterilization option and impermissibly deprived developmentally disabled persons of privacy and liberty interests protected by U.S. Const. amend XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. However, there was not sufficient evidence of a necessity for contraception for respondent nor sufficient evidence that less intrusive means of contraception were not presently available to respondent. Therefore, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying appellants' petition for authorization to have a tubal ligation performed on respondent was affirmed. Although the statutory scheme which deprived developmentally disabled persons the right to sterilization presented an unconstitutional violation of rights to privacy and liberty interests, evidence was not sufficient to show necessity or lack of less intrusive means of contraception.

In re Ramirez


Crim. No. 23684


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 931; 705 P.2d 897; 218 Cal. Rptr. 324; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 344



September 26, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied October 25, 1985.



DISPOSITION: The writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner sought review of the order from the Court of Appeal (California) that denied his request for a writ of habeas corpus. Petitioner claimed that he was subjected to increased sanctions for proscribed behavior under a new statutory scheme governing sentence reduction credits in which the appellate court held that the 1982 amendments could be applied to petitioner without violation of the state or federal ex post facto clauses.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prisoner was incarcerated before the effective date of the new statutory scheme governing sentence reduction credits. The sentence reduction credits went into effect and petitioner did not elect to be governed by the new scheme. Petitioner committed a proscribed act and lost 95 days of behavior credits under the new plan. Under the old scheme, petitioner would have only lost a maximum of 15 days. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus challenging the application of the new system to him. The court denied the writ, finding that the new statutory scheme was not retrospective as it only applied to and imposed sanctions for petitioner's misconduct after the new scheme became effective. The court noted that petitioner had fair warning that if he committed certain offenses after the new scheme became effective, petitioner would be subjected to increased sanctions. Accordingly, the court held that the new scheme did not violate state and federal constitutional ex post facto clauses.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner prisoner's writ of habeas corpus and found that the new statutory scheme governing sentence reduction credits did not violate ex post facto principles. The court held that the new law only applied to misconduct that occurred after it became effective and petitioner had fair warning of increased sanctions for proscribed conduct under the new scheme.
