People v. Montiel


Crim. No. 21243


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 910; 705 P.2d 1248; 218 Cal. Rptr. 572; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 343



September 26, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 31, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 19948, Marvin E. Ferguson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt is affirmed as to all counts, the special circumstances finding pursuant to section 190.2, subdivision (a)(1), is set aside, and the judgment as to penalty is reversed. The cause is remanded to the superior court for a retrial of the penalty phase in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On automatic appeal from the Superior Court of Kern County (California), defendant challenged his convictions for robbery, burglary, and murder with special circumstances and the imposition of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded not guilty to the charges of robbery, burglary, and murder with special circumstances. The people claimed that the murder was intentional and for financial gain, was especially heinous, atrocious and cruel, and occurred during commission of a robbery. After the trial started, defendant moved to enter a not guilty plea by reason of insanity, but his motion was denied. Defendant was found guilty of all counts and special circumstances except for the one alleging that the murder was especially heinous. Defendant was sentenced to death and this appeal automatically followed. Defendant contended that the trial court erred in removing persons opposed to the death penalty from the jury, in not allowing him to change his plea, and in not ruling on his motion to exclude photographs. Defendant also argued that the jury was not required to find that he intended to kill his victim. The court concluded that the intentional murder special circumstance overlapped with the felony murder special circumstance. Judgment was affirmed, but the intentional murder special circumstance finding was set aside. The penalty was reversed and remanded for retrial of the penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of robbery, burglary, and murder with special circumstances. The court set aside the special circumstances finding that the murder was intentional and for financial gain. The court held that the trial court erred in submitting it to the jury. The judgment as to penalty was reversed and the case was remanded to the superior court for a retrial of the penalty phase.

People v. Weidert


Crim. No. 23649


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 836; 705 P.2d 380; 218 Cal. Rptr. 57; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 339



September 19, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Transferred by Weidert v. Yates, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 26644 (E.D. Cal., Apr. 19, 2006)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Weidert v. Grounds, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 87194 (N.D. Cal., Aug. 8, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 264317-9, Robert L. Martin, Judge.
People v. Weidert, 150 Cal. App. 3d 759, 198 Cal. Rptr. 106, 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 1490 (Cal. App. 5th Dist., 1984)



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt is affirmed. The special circumstance findings are reversed. The cause is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. 15



15 In accordance with the parties' stipulation, the abstract of judgment should reflect the trial court's order staying judgment on the kidnaping conviction. (See § 654.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which convicted him of kidnapping and murder, found special circumstances under Cal. Penal Code §§ 190.2(a)(17)(ii), 190.2(a)(10) true, and sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the findings of true as to special circumstances were improper. Because the alleged kidnapping-murder special circumstance under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(ii) could not be sustained when the primary goal was not to kidnap, but to kill, the court set aside the special circumstance. The court determined that the alleged killing-of-a-witness special circumstance under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(10) applied to witnesses in criminal proceedings and was not intended to and could not apply to witnesses in juvenile proceedings. Because the murdered witnesses would have only been a witness against defendant in a pending juvenile proceeding and because there had been no consideration at trial of whether defendant subjectively thought that the witness would have testified against him in a criminal proceeding, the court reversed and remanded the § 190.2(a)(10) special circumstance, but otherwise affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, but reversed the kidnapping-murder special circumstance because it was not supported by defendant's primary intent to kill and remanded the killing-of-a-witness special circumstance for a determination of whether defendant thought the murdered witness was going to testify against him in a criminal proceeding.

People ex rel. Clancy v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 32041


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 740; 705 P.2d 347; 218 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 333



September 16, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The People's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied November 20, 1985, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue directing the court to vacate its order that Thomas Ebel need not produce the magazines listed in the subpoena, and issue a new order enforcing the subpoena and dismissing Clancy as the City's attorney in the pending action. In all other respects the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant business owner, real party in interest, petitioned for writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Riverside County (California) to vacate its order denying defendant's motion to disqualify a private attorney hired by petitioner city and to bar petitioner from proceeding in its abatement action against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city had a contingent fee arrangement with a private attorney to bring abatement actions against business owners selling obscene materials in the city. In a public nuisance action against defendant business owner, real party in interest, respondent superior court granted the extraordinary relief of disqualifying petitioner's private attorney. The court held that petitioner's attorney must adhere to greater standards of neutrality when performing tasks on behalf of the government. The contingent fee arrangement was antithetical to the standard of neutrality that an attorney representing the government must meet. As discussed in the Model Code of Prof'l Responsibility EC 7-13, 7-14, attorneys representing the government have special duties to seek justice for the sovereign and should not have an interest in the case extraneous to their official function. The court found that the attorney's hourly rate would double if petitioner was successful in the litigation. Thus, in the interests of justice, the court affirmed the disqualification of petitioner's attorney from representing petitioner in the pending abatement action.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate to direct respondent superior court to vacate its order that denied defendant business owner's motion to disqualify a private attorney hired by petitioner city. The court held that the contingent fee arrangement was antithetical to the standard of neutrality required for an attorney representing the government.

 

151 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Buol


S.F. No. 24823


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 751; 705 P.2d 354; 218 Cal. Rptr. 31; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 334



September 16, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 98723, Richard H. Breiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Marin County (California), awarding home as separate property to appellee upon evidence of an enforceable oral agreement in an action decided under a civil code not requiring a writing to rebut the presumption that property was community property. Appellant sought to retroactively apply Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1 because it required a writing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant alleged that there was insufficient evidence to support a finding of an oral agreement that the home acquired during marriage was owned by appellee. While the appeal was pending, Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1 was enacted, providing that the only means of rebutting the presumption that property acquired during marriage in joint tenancy was community property was by providing evidence of a written agreement that the property was separate. The issue was whether § 4800.1 might be given retroactive effect without violating the constitution. The court found the retrospective application was unconstitutional because it was an ex post facto law and deprived appellee of a vested right without due process of law. At time of trial, appellee had a vested property interest in the residence as her separate property and a requirement of writing operated to substantially impair that interest. Retroactive application of the writing requirement did not advance the goal of insuring equitable division of community property.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed, holding that retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1 would substantially impair appellee's vested property right without due process of law. State interest in equitable dissolution of marital partnership was not furthered by retroactive effect. Retroactivity served only to destroy appellee's legitimate separate property expectations as a penalty for lack of prescience of changes in the law occurring after trial.

State Personnel Bd. v. Fair Employment & Housing Com.


S.F. No. 24716


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 422; 703 P.2d 354; 217 Cal. Rptr. 16; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 314; 44 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1050; 37 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P35,480



August 8, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On September 19, 1985, the Judgment was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 280206, Irving H. Perluss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. We reserve jurisdiction for the purpose of considering the request for an award of attorney's fees by real parties in interest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the California Fair Employment and Housing Commission (FEHC) and certain state employees, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) granting an injunction on behalf of respondent State Personnel Board prohibiting the FEHC and its investigative arm from processing complaints of any civil service employees and applicants.


OVERVIEW: This case presented the question whether the agencies charged with carrying out the California Fair Employment and Housing Act (FEHA), Cal. Gov't. Code § 12900 et seq., could constitutionally exercise jurisdiction over state civil service employees. Appellants included applicants for state employment who claimed they were denied positions because of discrimination on the basis of physical handicap. They sought to have their claims investigated and adjudicated by the Department of Fair Employment and Housing (DFEH) and the Fair Employment and Housing Commission (FEHC). In opposition, respondent State Personnel Board secured an injunction prohibiting DFEH and FEHC from processing these complaints, as well as complaints of all state civil service employees. Respondent claimed that Cal. Const. art. VII afforded it exclusive jurisdiction over all aspects of the examination and selection of such employees. On review, the court rejected the exclusive jurisdiction interpretation of article VII. Accordingly, after determining that the Legislature intended the FEHA to cover state civil service employees, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court and dissolved the injunction entered.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision granting an injunction for respondent State Personnel Board was reversed because the reviewing court determined that there were no constitutional or statutory barriers barring appellant California Fair Employment and Housing Commission from processing discrimination complaints from state civil service employees.

Michelle W. v. Ronald W.


L.A. No. 31758


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 354; 703 P.2d 88; 216 Cal. Rptr. 748; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 311



August 5, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 467492, Judith McConnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the alleged biological father of a child born to a woman who was married to defendant, sought review of the order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which, based on Cal. Evid. Code § 621, held that defendant was the legal father.


OVERVIEW: The trial court was requested to determine who was the legal father of a child born to a married woman: plaintiff with whom the mother had an extramarital affair or defendant her husband. The trial court applied the presumption of Cal. Evid. Code § 621 and held that defendant was the legal father. The court noted that Cal. Evid. Code § 621 declared a presumption that the issue of a wife cohabiting with her husband was presumed to be a child of the marriage. Plaintiff argued that the presumption deprived him of a liberty interest protected by the due process clause. The court concluded that plaintiff's private interest in establishing a biological relationship in a court of law was overridden by the substantial state interests in familial stability and the welfare of the child. Plaintiff also argued that Cal. Evid. Code § 621 accorded the biological father fewer protections than the biological mother and this constituted an impermissible gender-based distinction. The court held that the statute indicated that the rights of the biological married mother and the biological unwed father were conditioned upon each other and held that it did not violate equal protection.


OUTCOME: Trial court's holding that defendant was the legal father of a child born to his wife while they cohabited was affirmed. The court held that the statutory presumption the trial court based its holding upon was not violative of plaintiff biological father's due process or equal protection rights.

Griffin Dev. Co. v. City of Oxnard


L.A. No. 31930


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 256; 703 P.2d 339; 217 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 305



August 1, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP 50174, William L. Peck, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of the denial of its petition for a writ of mandate from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California). Appellant contended that appellee city's denial of a special use permit for the conversion of apartments to condominium units was a denial of due process and a taking.


OVERVIEW: Appellant developer sought a special use permit from appellee city to convert its apartment complex to a condominium. It was undisputed that the complex did not conform to the mandatory parking requirements, as found in appellee's ordinances, and did not comply with some of appellee's advisory standards. Appellee denied the special use permit, variance, and tentative subdivision map. Contrary to appellant's argument, the court held that the Subdivision Map Act (Act), Cal. Gov't Code § 66410 et seq., did not preempt appellee's condominium conversion ordinance. The court stated that even under the Act appellee was free to regulate condominium conversions by local ordinance. The court also held that the regulation of condominium conversion was consistent with due process because the regulations were reasonably related to a legitimate governmental purpose and was a valid exercise of police power. The court stated that appellant failed to demonstrate that appellee's actions resulted in a taking because appellant was free to continue to rent its apartments, unaffected by the ordinance, it just was prevented from converting them to condominiums. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Trial court's denial of a writ of mandate was affirmed. Appellee city's ordinance was not preempted by state law and it was reasonably related to a legitimate governmental purpose and was a valid exercise of police power.

Barrington v. A. H. Robins Co.


L.A. No. 31939


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 146; 702 P.2d 563; 216 Cal. Rptr. 405; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 301



July 29, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC 75502, Fred Rimerman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of dismissal) is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner patient sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that dismissed her amended complaint against respondent manufacturer for a failure to serve a summons and complaint within three years of the date of the original filing in a medical malpractice and failure to warn action.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed petitioner patient's complaint against respondent manufacturer pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a, for a failure to serve a summons and complaint within three years of the filing of the original complaint. The court noted that service of the amended complaint on respondent was not made until two years and six months after the filing of the amended complaint, but three years and seven weeks after the filing of original complaint. Petitioner contended that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a should be construed in light of the relation back doctrine. The court stated that if a cause of action in an amended complaint did not relate back to original complaint then the three-year period under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a would commence from the filing of amended complaint. The court stated that the first three causes of action related back to the original complaint and were properly dismissed. However, the fourth cause of action, did not relate back because it was based on a different set of operative facts and involved a different "instrumentality" and "accident" than the first three causes of action. Therefore, the trial court's order was reversed.


OUTCOME: Trial court's order of dismissal was reversed because petitioner patient's fourth cause of action in her amended complaint did not relate back to the original complaint; therefore, the three year period to serve respondent manufacturer did not commence until the amended complaint was filed.

 

157 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Babylon


Crim. No. 23440.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


39 Cal. 3d 70; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 296



July 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reprinted as mod. (See 39 Cal. 3d 719).


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Municipal Court for the Sacramento Judicial District of Sacramento County, Nos. 48874-M and 48983-M, Roger K. Warren, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoners sought review of the decision of the intermediate appellate court (California), which affirmed their convictions, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 593e (1980),.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prisoners were convicted, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 593e (1980), of selling devices that could be used for the unauthorized reception of an over-the-air transmission of program material, such as Home Box Office (HBO). At the time appellants were convicted, Cal. Penal Code § 593e (1980) provided that it was a violation of the statute to sell a device that intercepted or decoded any over-the-air transmission. In 1984, the statute was amended to state that it was a violation to sell a device that was designed to decode, descramble, intercept any encoded, scrambled or nonstandard signal. The court stated that, absent a savings clause, appellants were entitled to the benefit of a change in law that occurred during the pendency of their appeal. The transmission that appellants were convicted of intercepting was not encoded or scrambled. The only possible means to convict appellants under the amended statute was if the signal was nonstandard. The court stated that the signal in question was not distorted, or altered in some form to have rendered it unintelligible without the use of a special device provided by the signal sender and thus was not nonstandard.


OUTCOME: Appellant prisoners' convictions were reversed. Because the legislature chose to amend the statute that appellants were convicted under and failed to insert a savings clause, that meant that appellants were entitled to the benefit of the more recent statute that decriminalized their behavior.

 

158 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Babylon


Crim. No. 23440


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 719; 702 P.2d 205; 216 Cal. Rptr. 123; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 331



July 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 29, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Sacramento Judicial District of Sacramento County, Nos. 48874-M and 48983-M, Roger K. Warren, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (California), which affirmed their convictions for violating Cal. Penal Code § 593e.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were charged with selling devices to be used for the unauthorized reception of an over-the-air transmission broadcast by a licensed Multipoint Distribution Service (MDS) in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 593e. The issue in the lower court was whether the statute extended beyond decoders to devices which merely received microwave transmissions. Appellants were convicted. The Court of Appeals affirmed the convictions. The court reversed finding that recent amendments to the statute required reversal under the general principle that, absent a saving clause, a criminal defendant was entitled to the benefit of a change in the law during the pendency of his appeal. The court found that the amended provision did not proscribe appellant's activities because there was no evidence that the sender of the MDS transmission varied the waveform in an effort to distort the signal such that any individual equipped with a microwave antenna and a standard amateur tuneable down converter -- multipurpose devices which had been lawfully marketed for over 20 years -- could receive the transmission in intelligible form without any special device or information provided by the sender.


OUTCOME: Appellants' convictions were reversed on the basis that, absent a saving clause, a defendant was entitled to the benefit of a more recent statute which mitigated the punishment for the offense or decriminalized the conduct altogether. The amended statute did not proscribe appellants' activities.

Saleeby v. State Bar


S.F. No. 24695


Supreme Court of California


39 Cal. 3d 547; 702 P.2d 525; 216 Cal. Rptr. 367; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 321



July 25, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 15, 1985.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding the bar to (1) reformulate the existing rules to conform generally to such procedural requirements as are described herein, (2) afford petitioner a reasonable opportunity to be heard thereunder, and (3) provide sufficient findings upon which review may be based. Jurisdiction is reserved for the purpose of awarding attorney fees as may be hereinafter determined.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this original proceeding petitioner sought an order requiring the State Bar, (California) to adopt rules governing its exercise of discretion in administering the Client Security Fund (CSF) and to set aside its order granting petitioner less than the full amount of reimbursement which he requested.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was represented by an attorney who retained virtually all of an award obtained for petitioner. That attorney was later disbarred. Petitioner then sought compensation from the Client Security Fund (CSF) administered by the State Bar. Petitioner received a portion of the funds he lost to his attorney, but not all that he requested. Petitioner sought an order requiring the State Bar to adopt rules governing its exercise of discretion in administering the CSF and to set aside its order pertaining to petitioner's request. The court concluded that the procedures followed by the bar were insufficient because they failed to provide a record for judicial review and denied due process. Therefore, the court directed that the bar formulate new rules. The court also concluded that decisions relating to reimbursement from the CSF were reviewable by a writ of mandamus heard in the first instance in the superior court. Finally, as to attorney fees, the court held that the wholesale prohibition against payment of such fees in CSF matters was impermissible, and that petitioner's attorney was entitled under private attorney general principles to fees in an amount to be fixed later.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the State Bar to revise its rules pertaining to how it hears and issues decisions pertaining to reimbursements from the Client Security Fund and the allowance of attorneys fees, and then afford petitioner a reasonable opportunity to be heard under the new rules.

San Jose Teachers Assn. v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24793


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 839; 700 P.2d 1252; 215 Cal. Rptr. 250; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 287; 120 L.R.R.M. 3190



June 24, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Order on remand from U.S. Supreme Ct. 42 Cal. 3d 130.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the Superior Court for the County of Santa Clara to vacate its order of August 23, 1983, entered in San Jose Teachers Association v. Sandra Abernathy, etc., No. 508996, denying plaintiff's motion for summary judgment, and to enter a new and different order granting said motion.

The parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher's association sought review of a decision of a California court of appeal, which denied its request for a writ of mandate in its action to compel real party in interest employees to pay a service fee pursuant to the terms of a collective bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner teacher association entered into a collective bargaining agreement. Under the agreement, the covered employees were required to join petitioner or pay a service fee. Petitioner brought suit against real party in interest employees, who refused to join or pay the fee. Real parties in interest objected to the fee on the grounds that a portion of the monies would be used for political and ideological purposes, which they alleged to be a violation of their U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The trial court denied petitioner's motion for summary judgment to compel payment. The appellate court denied petitioner's request for a writ of mandate. The court reversed, holding that petitioner was entitled to collect the entire fee, pending a collateral proceeding to determine the amount actually used for collective bargaining purposes, because it placed in escrow the portion of the fees which it reasonably estimated would otherwise be spent for political or ideological purposes and had implemented rebate procedures. Further, the court held that the California Public Employment Relations Board had jurisdiction over the collateral proceeding under Cal. Gov't Code 3540 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision denying petitioner teachers association's request for a writ of mandate because petitioner was entitled to collect a service fee from real party in interest employees pending a collateral proceeding to determine the amount of the fees used for collective bargaining purposes. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for entry of an order granting petitioner's motion for summary judgment.

 

161 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



E. S. Bllls v. Tzucanow


L.A. No. 31839


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 824; 700 P.2d 1280; 215 Cal. Rptr. 278; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 286



June 24, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied August 1, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N 17826, Daniel C. Leedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court erroneously excluded evidence that was crucial to defendants' ability to disprove the good cause requisite to a judgment for plaintiff under sections 1174(a) and 20999.1. Accordingly, the judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant lessees appealed from a judgment entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in favor of plaintiff lessor in its action to recover possession of a gasoline service station.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff lessor, a petroleum distributor, and defendant lessees entered into a lease and a gasoline purchase franchise agreement by which defendants became the lessees and dealer at a service station owned by plaintiff. Plaintiff terminated the lease when the parties became embroiled in a pricing dispute. Plaintiff then commenced an unlawful detainer action to regain possession of the station. Following a bench trial, the trial court found that plaintiff had good cause to terminate the lease because defendants refused to purchase gasoline from plaintiff under the terms set forth in the franchise agreement. The trial court entered a judgment awarding possession of the station to plaintiff. The court reversed, concluding that the trial court erred when it excluded evidence offered by defendants regarding the retail prices at stations operated directly by plaintiff and the prices charged to large volume customers. The court found that the error was not harmless because the evidence was relevant to the issues of whether the price plaintiff was charging defendants was excessive under the franchise agreement and whether plaintiff had good cause to terminate the lease.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of plaintiff lessor because the trial court excluded evidence offered by defendant lessees that was relevant to the issues of whether plaintiff had good cause to terminate the lease and whether plaintiff was entitled to regain possession of the disputed service station.

 

162 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Frank


Crim. Nos. 21353, 22347


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 711; 700 P.2d 415; 214 Cal. Rptr. 801; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 281



June 6, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied August 20, 1985, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Lucas, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-42956, Byron K. McMillan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it imposes the penalty of death and is affirmed in all other respects. The petition for habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which found him guilty of murder during the commission of a rape, kidnapping, child molestation, and infliction of torture. Defendant was sentenced to death under the 1977 death penalty law, former Cal. Penal Code §§ 190-190.6. He contended that it was error for the trial court to admit certain writings seized from his apartment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death under the 1977 death penalty law, former Cal. Penal Code §§ 190-190.6. Defendant was found guilty of first degree murder with special circumstances, rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261(2), kidnapping under Cal. Penal Code § 207, and child molesting under Cal. Penal Code § 228. Defendant had a history of committing crimes against children. Police obtained a search warrant for defendant's apartment and found a notebook in which he described his emotions and urges. The court held that the search warrant for defendant's apartment was overbroad because it did not impose meaningful restrictions on the objects to be seized, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 1525, 1527, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 13, and therefore determined that evidence from the notebook was erroneously admitted. The court engaged in harmless error analysis pursuant to Cal. Const. art. VI, § 13, and determined that the error was harmless during the guilt stage because the evidence was cumulative, but found that the error was prejudicial to defendant in the penalty phase. Accordingly, the court affirmed defendant's convictions but reversed the death sentence imposed on him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder, rape, kidnapping, and child molesting, but reversed the death sentence. The court held that evidence seized under an overbroad warrant was erroneously admitted, but found the error harmless in the guilt phase of the trial. The court held that the error was prejudicial in the penalty phase because the prosecutor relied heavily on it to argue that defendant should receive capital punishment.

People v. Boyd


Crim. No. 21704


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 762; 700 P.2d 782; 215 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 282



June 6, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A525264, Roy L. Norman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of guilt is affirmed. The special circumstance finding is set aside. The judgment as to penalty is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of first-degree murder under the 1978 death penalty initiative, Cal. Penal Code §§ 190-190.5, with the special circumstance of murder during the commission of attempted robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17). A second jury, selected for the penalty phase, fixed defendant's punishment at death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder under the 1978 death penalty initiative, Cal. Penal Code §§ 190-190.5, with the special circumstance of murder during the commission of attempted robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17). Defendant shot a man on the street after drawing a gun and asking for money. The trial was bifurcated. During the guilt phase, the trial court failed to instruct the jury that intent to kill was an essential element of finding the special circumstance based on a murder during an attempted robbery. Defendant presented evidence on his diminished capacity based on the behavior of PCP users. On appeal, the conviction was affirmed but the special circumstance finding was set aside and the penalty was reversed. Although the court found that the evidence suggested that defendant was not strongly under the influence of PCP, the court held that failure to instruct on intent to kill as an essential element of felony-murder special circumstance was reversible error. The court went on to state that under the 1978 death penalty initiative, the prosecution was limited in the aggravating circumstances it could present by the language of Cal. Penal Code § 190.3.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of guilty of first-degree murder but set aside the special circumstance finding and reversed the death sentence imposed on defendant. The court held that failure to instruct on intent to kill as an essential element of felony-murder special circumstance was reversible error.

Fox v. Alexis


S.F. No. 24712


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 621; 699 P.2d 309; 214 Cal. Rptr. 132; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 277



May 20, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 303878, William H. Lally, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Motor Vehicles challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent repeat offender's petition for a writ of mandate directing appellant to set aside its order revoking respondent's driver's license. Appellant revoked respondent's license pursuant to the amended version of Cal. Veh. Code § 13552, which took effect after the time of respondent's offense.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Department of Motor Vehicles revoked respondent repeat offender's driving license pursuant to the amended versions of Cal. Veh. Code §§ 13352 and 13352.5, which were effective at the time of respondent's third conviction for driving while under the influence of alcohol. Under the versions of §§ 13352 and 13352.5 that were effective when respondent committed his third offense for driving under the influence, appellant would have been prevented from revoking respondent's license for so long as respondent's court-ordered participation in an alcoholism rehabilitation program was satisfactory. The court affirmed the judgment setting aside appellant's order of revocation, holding that the statutes that were in effect at the time of respondent's offense controlled appellant's actions to revoke respondent's driver's license. The court determined that appellant retroactively applied the amended statutes and that the legislature did not intend for the amended statutes to have retroactive application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment setting aside appellant Department of Motor Vehicle's order revoking respondent repeat offender's driver license. The court held that the statutes in effect at the time of respondent's offense of driving under the influence of alcohol controlled appellant's actions because the legislature did not intend the statutes in effect at the time of respondent's conviction to have retroactive application.

 

165 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County Sanitation Dist. No. 2 v. Los Angeles County Employees' Assn.


L.A. No. 31850


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 564; 699 P.2d 835; 214 Cal. Rptr. 424; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 275; 119 L.R.R.M. 2433



May 13, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 27, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 166219, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, public employee labor unions, appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found defendants' strike against plaintiff sanitation district to be unlawful and consequently awarded damages, interest, and costs.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, public employee labor unions, went on strike against plaintiff sanitation district. Plaintiff filed suit in tort, alleging that the strike was unlawful. Plaintiff secured a judgment, damages, interest, and costs against defendants. The court reversed, finding that neither the language, history, nor judicial interpretation of the Meyers-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3500-3511, justified a ruling that strikes by public employees were a prohibited tort. To the contrary, the court's survey of the common law, sister state legislation, and policy arguments led it to conclude that California should not recognize the common law prohibition against public sector strikes. While the court found that public employees had a fundamental right to organize for the purpose of collective bargaining, the court held that they did not have an absolute right to strike. The court therefore ruled that a strike by public employees was permissible unless it was clearly demonstrated that the strike created a substantial and imminent threat to the health or safety of the public.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that the strike of defendants, public employee labor unions, against plaintiff sanitation district was a prohibited tort because strikes by public employees were not unlawful at common law unless it was clearly demonstrated that such a strike created a substantial and imminent threat to the health or safety of the public.

 

166 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Misener


Crim. No. 23850


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 543; 698 P.2d 637; 213 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 274



May 9, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied July 11, 1985.



DISPOSITION: An order of contempt cannot stand if the underlying order is invalid. ( In re Blaze (1969) 271 Cal.App.2d 210, 212 [76 Cal.Rptr. 551].) Let a writ of habeas corpus issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner lawyer sought a writ of habeas corpus after the trial court granted the prosecution's motion, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1102.5, to discover any prior statements made by a defense witness to defense counsel, and petitioner was held in contempt for refusing to obey the court's order to disclose the content of interviews of the witness.


OVERVIEW: Alleging that Cal. Penal Code § 1102.5 was unconstitutional and the trial court's grant of the prosecution's motion for discovery was improper, petitioner lawyer was held in contempt after refusing to comply with an order to disclose the contents of interviews with a defense witness. On appeal, the court granted petitioner's writ of habeas corpus as the discovery motion and the contempt order were invalid. The court ruled that § 1102.5 was facially unconstitutional because it violated that aspect of a defendant's privilege against self-incrimination that required the prosecution carry the entire burden of proving a defendant's guilt. The court held that § 1102.5 violated a defendant's privilege against self-incrimination because, to the extent that the prosecution was aided by the compelled disclosures, § 1102.5 lightened the prosecution's burden by requiring a defendant to hand over evidence to impeach the defense's witnesses. The court found that, although a defendant waived his right to object to his witnesses' cross-examination, the defendant's right to refuse to supply the prosecution with the means to conduct a cross-examination of the defense witnesses was not waived.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner lawyer's writ of habeas corpus. The trial court's order granting the prosecution motion's for discovery and order of contempt against petitioner were improper as the discovery statute was facially unconstitutional because it violated that aspect of a defendant's privilege against self-incrimination that required the prosecution to carry the entire burden of proving guilt.

 

167 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Castro


Crim. No. 23605


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 301; 696 P.2d 111; 211 Cal. Rptr. 719; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 261



March 11, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied April 18, 1985. Bird, C. J., and Lucas, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. CR 8964, Maurice Jourdane, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgement of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which convicted her of receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496. Defendant challenged the trial court's ruling that prior convictions were admissible by virtue of Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(f).


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied defendant's motion to bar impeachment with prior convictions, holding that Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(f), was more specific than Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), and therefore, the trial court lacked discretion to exclude defendant's prior felony convictions for impeachment purposes. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction but held that § 28(f) did not abolish the trial court's discretion to exclude felony convictions. The court held that a witness could be impeached with a felony conviction if the conviction involved moral turpitude. The court found that defendant's conviction of simple possession of heroin did not involve moral turpitude, so defendant should not have been impeached with that prior conviction. However, at trial, defendant volunteered testimony that she had been incarcerated before any attempt was made to impeach her. Based on the entire record, the court held that it was not reasonably probable that a result more favorable to defendant would have occurred in the absence of the trial court's error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of receiving stolen property because defendant testified at trial regarding her criminal past before the prosecution attempted to impeach her. The court concluded, after reviewing the entire record, that it was not reasonably probable that a result more favorable would have occurred had the trial judge not erred in ruling that he did not have discretion to exclude prior convictions.

Robbins v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24599


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 199; 695 P.2d 695; 211 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 256



March 4, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  Therefore, a peremptory writ of mandate shall issue directing the superior court to: 1) vacate its order denying the preliminary injunction; and 2) to enter an order granting the preliminary injunction. The statewide importance of these issues suggests that this case should be set for trial at the earliest possible date.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, impoverished individuals, sought a writ of mandamus directing respondent, the Superior Court of Sacramento County, to enter an order granting petitioners' request for a preliminary injunction to enjoin defendant county, a real party in interest, from conditioning receipt of general assistance benefits upon residence at a facility, pending determination of petitioners' suit challenging defendant's "in-kind" benefits policy.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, impoverished individuals, challenged the "in-kind" benefits policy of defendant county, a real party in interest. Respondent superior court denied petitioners' request for a preliminary injunction enjoining defendant from conditioning receipt of general assistance benefits upon residence at a "poorhouse" facility. The trial court denied the injunction. The court granted petitioners' request for a writ of mandate, holding that petitioners met the first prong of the test for a preliminary injunction when petitioners would suffer great and immediate harm from the denial of the injunction, whereas the alleged harm to defendant was minimal. The court held that second prong of the test, likelihood of success on the merits, was met because petitioners demonstrated a strong likelihood of success on their claims that defendant's policy violated the goals of the state's welfare statutes as reflected in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10000 and unconstitutionally violated their right to privacy. The court ordering respondent to grant the injunction and, because of the statewide importance of the issues in the case, urged that the matter be set for trial at the earliest possible date.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that petitioners, impoverished individuals, were likely to suffer greater injury from denial of the injunction than defendant county, a real party in interest, was likely to suffer from its grant and there was a reasonable probability that petitioners would prevail on the merits. Thus, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent superior court to grant the preliminary injunction.

Huntington Park Redevelopment Agency v. Martin


L.A. No. 31861


Supreme Court of California


38 Cal. 3d 100; 695 P.2d 220; 211 Cal. Rptr. 133; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 252



February 28, 1985 



DISPOSITION:  Let the writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Huntington Park Redevelopment Authority sought a petition for writ of mandate compelling respondent secretary to publish an ordinance which imposed a sales and use tax pursuant to the Community Redevelopment Law, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Although respondent secretary, for petitioner Huntington Park Redevelopment Authority, had a statutory duty under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7202.6(b) and Cal. Gov't Code § 36933(a), to publish an ordinance imposing a sales and use tax pursuant to the Community Redevelopment Law, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 33000 et seq., he refused to do so claiming it violated Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 4 and Cal. Const. art. XIIIB. Upon review, the court issued a writ of mandate compelling respondent to publish the ordinance because it found that the ordinance did not violate the California Constitution. Because it impinged on the democratic process, the court read Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 4 narrowly and found that the ordinance did not fall within the article because petitioner was not a special district. The ordinance also did not violate Cal. Const. art. XIIIB because there had been a transfer of financial responsibility from the city to petitioner, and therefore, the appropriations limit could be adjusted accordingly.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for writ of mandate, sought by petitioner Huntington Park Redevelopment Authority, compelling respondent secretary to publish an ordinance imposing a sales and use tax pursuant to the Community Redevelopment Law because the ordinance was not violative of the California Constitution.

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 265659, Michael J. Virga, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant medical provider appealed from the judgment entered on the jury verdict by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which awarded plaintiff patient damages in his medical malpractice claim. Plaintiff appealed from the superior court's post-verdict application of the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3333.1, 3333.2 to modify the award of damages.

Plaintiff appealed from the post-verdict award modification under the Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3333.1, 3333.2. XIV due process or equal protection rights as plaintiff had no vested property right in a particular measure of damages and the statutes were rationally related to their legislative purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment awarding plaintiff patient medical malpractice damages against defendant medical provider and affirmed the post-verdict modification of the award to the statutory noneconomic damages limit and to account for collateral sources pursuant to the statutorily modified collateral source rule.

In re Lance W.


Crim. No. 23551


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 873; 694 P.2d 744; 210 Cal. Rptr. 631; 1985 Cal. LEXIS 241



February 1, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 21, 1985, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. J 255206, Richard A. Curtis, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article 6, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order for camp-community placement) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained the petition filed against him for possession of marijuana for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359 and sentenced him to serve time in a camp-community placement program and that, upon release from camp placement, he be confined in a juvenile hall for 30 days.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, was arrested after the police conducted an unconstitutional search of a third person's car. A petition was filed in juvenile court that defendant had violated Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359. Defendant's motion to suppress the evidence and subsequent statements was denied. The petition was sustained and a disposition order was entered, from which defendant appealed. The court affirmed the judgment, holding that Cal. Const. art. I, § 28(d), abrogated the vicarious exclusionary rule. That rule was created by the California judiciary and provided more extensive protection than U.S. Const. amend. IV. The constitutional amendment was authorized, Cal. Const. art. II, § 8, reflected the will of the people, and did not raise separation of powers issues. The court held that defendant did not have standing for exclusionary rule protection as his personal constitutional rights were not violated. The court affirmed the disposition order that confined defendant to a camp-community placement program and, upon his release, to a 30-day confinement in the juvenile hall, holding that this was not a dual disposition and was authorized as an element of probation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement sustaining the petition against defendant juvenile for possession of marijuana for sale, because the vicarious exclusionary rule was abrogated by an amendment to the state constitution. The court affirmed the disposition order that confined defendant to the juvenile house for 30 days upon his release from the camp-community placement program, because it was not a dual disposition.

Ramona R. v. Superior Court

CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: Defendant minor was 17 years old when her guardian was shot and killed. The prosecution filed a murder charge in juvenile court against defendant pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602. The prosecution filed a motion to have defendant declared unfit for juvenile court proceedings under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707(c). Fisher v. City of Berkeley


S.F. No. 24675


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 644; 693 P.2d 261; 209 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 141; 1985-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P66,473



December 27, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 536602-6, Donald P. McCullum, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The 1982 amendment to section 14, purporting to create a presumption affecting the burden of proof, is invalid. This provision is clearly severable. (§ 16.) All other provisions of the ordinance are valid and enforceable. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlords sought review of a judgment of the Alameda County Superior Court (California), which declared that defendant city's rent control ordinance was constitutional on its face.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landlords filed an action against defendant city for declaratory relief regarding the enforcement of a rent control ordinance. Plaintiffs alleged that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The trial court granted defendant's motion for judgment on the pleadings. On appeal, the court substantially affirmed the judgment and held that the ordinance was not preempted by the Sherman Antitrust Act (act), 15 U.S.C.S. § 1, even if there was a conflict between the ordinance and the act. The court held that the ordinance did not constitute a monopoly under 15 U.S.C.S. § 2 because the per se rule of price fixing and the traditional rule of reason did not apply. The court determined that the ordinance was valid under traditional antitrust rules and found that the ordinance was rationally related to defendant's legitimate exercise of police power. The court held the ordinance was constitutional under the principles of due process because the ordinance guaranteed each landlord a fair return on their investment. The court held the ordinance was invalid only to the extent it created an evidentiary presumption that affected the burden of proof in regard to retaliatory evictions.


OUTCOME: The court substantially affirmed the decision of the trial court, which declared defendant city's rent control ordinance was constitutional. The court held that federal antitrust laws did not preempt the ordinance and that the ordinance did not constitute a monopoly under traditional antitrust rules. The court held that a provision that enacted a presumption, which affected the burden of proof in regard to retaliatory evictions, was invalid.

 

175 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Unger v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24659


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 612; 692 P.2d 238; 209 Cal. Rptr. 474; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 140



December 27, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ heretofore issued is discharged as having served its purpose, and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two registered voters, sought a peremptory writ directing respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) to vacate its order, which sustained the demurrer filed by the Republican Party and ordered dismissal of petitioners' application for writ of mandate that alleged the Republican Party exceeded its powers pursuant to Cal. Elect. Code §§ 6, 9276, and 9440.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, two registered voters, filed an application for writ of mandate and alleged that the Republican Party, real parties in interest, exceeded its powers pursuant to Cal. Elect. Code §§ 6, 9276, and 9440. The trial court sustained the Republican Party's demurrer and ordered dismissal of petitioners' writ. On appeal, the petitioners argued that the political parties were expressly restrained from supporting or opposing candidates for nonpartisan office pursuant to section 6. The court denied petitioners' application and held that the Republican Party acted within their rights in taking a position on the confirmation of the supreme court justices. After consulting the legislative history of Cal. Elect. Code § 6, the court held that neither section 6 nor any other provision of law was intended to restrain political parties from participating in the election campaign for nonpartisan office. The only prohibition that the court recognized was found in Cal. Elect. Code § 37, which stated that political parties could not nominate a candidate for a nonpartisan office.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners', two registered voters, application for writ of mandate and based on the legislative history, the court held that the election code did not prohibit political parties from supporting or opposing nonpartisan candidates. However, political parties were constrained from nominating candidates for nonpartisan office.

People v. Turner


Crim. No. 21456


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 302; 690 P.2d 669; 208 Cal. Rptr. 196; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 128



November 21, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 17, 1985. Bird, C. J., and Lucas, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. CR VCR 807, Joseph B. Campbell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The findings of special circumstances are set aside and the judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to penalty; in all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which convicted him under the 1978 death penalty law, Cal. Penal. Code § 190 et seq., on two counts of first degree murder. A special circumstance allegation that the murders were committed during the commission of burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17), was found to be true.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted under the 1978 death penalty law, Cal. Penal. Code § 190 et seq., on two counts of first-degree murder. Special circumstance allegations that the murders were committed during the commission of burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17), and that defendant was convicted of more than one offense of murder, Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3) were found to be true. The jury fixed the punishment at death. Defendant's conviction was affirmed on appeal after the court determined that the trial court had not abused its discretion in consolidating his trial with that of a codefendant, that no impropriety took place when the prosecution used peremptory challenges on persons with reservations about capital punishment, and that no reversible errors were committed by the trial court in the course of its evidentiary rulings. However, the findings of special circumstances were set aside and the judgment was reversed insofar as it related to penalty because prejudicial error was committed when the court below failed to instruct the jury that the felony-murder special circumstances required an intent to kill or to aid in a killing.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for two counts of first degree murder was affirmed because, among other things, the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it consolidated defendant's trial with that of a codefendant. However, the findings of special circumstances were set aside and the sentence of death was reversed because the trial court failed to instruct the jury that the felony-murder special circumstances required an intent to kill.

Barme v. Wood

Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 174; 689 P.2d 446; 207 Cal. Rptr. 816; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 120

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant and intervenor city sought review of a decision of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) that granted the motion of defendants, hospital and health care providers, for summary judgment, maintaining that recovery by appellant was barred in a medical malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: A police officer, employed by appellant and intervenor city (appellant), suffered a heart attack while on duty. The officer underwent open-heart surgery and sustained brain damage. Plaintiffs, the officer and his wife, filed a medical malpractice action against defendants, a hospital and health care providers. Appellant intervened, seeking to recover self-insured workers' compensation benefits. Defendants made a motion for summary judgment contending that recovery by appellant was barred under Cal. Civ. An employer's right to seek reimbursement from a third party for workers' compensation benefits was subject to legislative regulation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment grant to defendants, hospital and health care providers, holding that an employer's right to seek reimbursement for workers' compensation benefits was subject to legislative regulation. Due process did not require allocation of liability based on fault.

Macgregor v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


S.F. No. 24706


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 205; 689 P.2d 453; 207 Cal. Rptr. 823; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 122



November 8, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 478975, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: As the record clearly supports the trial court's finding that plaintiff had good cause for leaving work, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which ordered defendant to reconsider its decision and to award benefits to plaintiff employee if she met other eligibility requirements.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff employee was on maternity leave when she, the father of her child and the man she was engaged to, and her baby moved with the fiance to New York to care for her fiance's sick father. When plaintiff was unable to find work, she applied for unemployment insurance benefits and was referred to the California Employment Department (department), which determined that pursuant to Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256, she was ineligible for benefits. Plaintiff appealed and the hearing judge affirmed. Defendant California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board adopted the administrative law judge's (ALJ) decision. The trial court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing defendant to set aside its decision and to reconsider its action. The court affirmed the judgment and held that pursuant to § 1256, plaintiff had good cause for leaving work. Plaintiff had established a family unit consisting of herself, her fiance, and their child that that she chose to relocate to maintain and preserve their family unit. The lack of a legally recognized marriage did not prevent plaintiff from demonstrating that compelling familial obligations provided good cause for leaving employment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in favor of plaintiff employee and against defendant California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board because plaintiff showed good cause for leaving work. Plaintiff established a family unit that consisted of herself, her fiance and the father of their child, and their child. The lack of a legally recognized marriage did not prevent plaintiff from showing good cause for leaving employment.

People v. Ramos


Crim. No. 21352


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 136; 689 P.2d 430; 207 Cal. Rptr. 800; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 118



November 1, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 13, 1984. Lucas, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-42382, Richard J. Beacom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons discussed in part II, the judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to the special circumstance finding and penalty. On the issue of guilt, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) convicting him of felony murder special circumstance. On remand from the United States Supreme Court, the court addressed whether the jury instructions regarding felony murder special circumstances were valid under the state constitution and whether remand for a new determination of special circumstances and penalty was required.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the trial court convicting him of felony murder special circumstance. On remand from the United States Supreme Court, the court addressed whether the jury instructions regarding felony murder special circumstances were valid under the state constitution and whether remand for a new determination of special circumstances and penalty was required. The court ordered a retrial of the special circumstances and penalty phase because a case decided subsequent to the trial court's judgment but while defendant's appeals were still pending held, contrary to the trial court's instructions, that an intentional killing was a required element of the felony-murder special circumstance pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17). The court also held that the instruction on felony murder sentencing, which only stated that a sentence of life without possibility of parole may be commuted by the governor, was incompatible with the state constitutional guarantees of fundamental fairness both because it was misleading and because it invited the jury to be influenced by speculative and improper considerations.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a retrial of the special circumstances and penalty phase because a case decided subsequent to the trial court's judgment, but while the appeals were pending held, contrary to the trial court's instructions, that an intentional killing was a required element of the special circumstance. In addition, the felony murder instructions which stated that life without parole could be commuted violated the state constitution.

Nash v. City of Santa Monica


L.A. No. 31798


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 97; 688 P.2d 894; 207 Cal. Rptr. 285; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 116



October 25, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 311502, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the questioned provision, as applied to respondent Nash, is valid. Accordingly, we reverse the judgment below.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that Santa Monica, Cal., Santa Monica City Charter § 1803(t) deprived respondent of due process of law and constituted a taking without just compensation and of the order granting respondent a removal permit.


OVERVIEW: Respondent owned an apartment building that he wanted to destroy in order for the land to appreciate in value. Santa Monica, Cal., Santa Monica City Charter § 1803(t) prohibited the removal of rental units from the housing market by conversion or demolition unless a removal permit was obtained. When it became clear to respondent that he would not be issued a permit, respondent challenged the ordinance. The court concluded that the requirement was reasonably related to the legitimate goal of maintaining adequate rental housing and that the relationship of the ordinance to the public welfare was real and substantial. Furthermore, the respondent's obligations related to property ownership, and services provided were universal and necessary incidents of apartment buildings. If respondent sold the building, he would be in no worse position than if the sale were compelled by eminent domain. The court, therefore, upheld the ordinance as constitutionally valid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, holding that the ordinance requiring a removal permit to demolish rental property was constitutional because the requirement was reasonably related to a legitimate goal, the relationship to the public welfare was real and substantial, and respondent's obligations related to property ownership.

In re Baby Girl M.


L.A. No. 31786


Supreme Court of California


37 Cal. 3d 65; 688 P.2d 918; 207 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 114



October 22, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. A 24905, Judith McConnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment terminating the father's custodial rights is reversed. The matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural father sought review of a judgment entered in the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), in favor of respondent department of social services, which terminated appellant's parental rights by considering only the best interests of the child. Appellant contended that the trial court erred by not first considering whether an award of custody to him would be detrimental to the child.


OVERVIEW: Appellant natural father challenged a judgment in favor of respondent department of social services, terminating his parental rights. Appellant contended that the trial court should not have only considered the best interests of the child, without first considering whether an award of custody to him would be detrimental to the child. Appellant and child's mother dated and neither knew mother was pregnant at the time the relationship ended. The child was born and the mother requested adoption assistance. The child was placed in a foster home. Appellant requested that the child be placed with a family who provided daycare for his sons. Five days later, a petition to terminate his rights under Cal. Civ. Code § 7017 was filed and appellant discovered that the child had been relinquished for adoption. The termination petition was granted. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the trial court had to make an additional finding of detriment, after considering the child's best interest, in order to terminate appellant's parental rights. The court held that a mother's release for adoption should not deprive appellant of an opportunity to retain and develop a parent/child relationship.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment terminating appellant natural father's custodial rights and remanded. The court concluded that when appellant's rights did arise, upon relinquishment by the mother, and he requested a hearing, an additional finding of detriment was necessary to terminate his parental rights. The court held that appellant had a right to the opportunity to retain and develop a parent/child relationship.

AFL v. Eu


S.F. No. 24746


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 3d 687; 686 P.2d 609; 206 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 210



August 27, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On October 25, 1983, the Judgment was Modified to Read as Printed Above.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding respondents not to take any action, including the expenditure of public funds, to place the proposed Balanced Budget Initiative on the November 6, 1984, General Election ballot. We reserve jurisdiction for the purpose of considering petitioners' request for an award of attorney's fees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner organizations and taxpayers filed an original action in the Supreme Court of California for a writ of mandamus to order respondent state officials to refrain from taking any action, including the expenditure of public funds, to place a proposed balanced federal budget statutory initiative on the November 1984 ballot.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners taxpayers and organizations initiated an action to order respondents state officials to refrain from taking action to place the proposed balanced federal budget statutory initiative on the November 1984 ballot. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate commanding respondents not to take any action, including the expenditure of public funds, to place the proposed balanced budget initiative on the general election ballot. The court found that the initiative, to the extent that it applied for a constitutional convention or required the legislature to do so, did not conform to U.S. Const. art. V which provides for applications by the legislatures of two-thirds of the several states, not by the people through the initiative. The court also concluded that the measure exceeded the scope of the initiative power under the controlling provisions of Cal. Const. art. II, § 8 and art. IV, § 1 because the balanced budget initiative did not adopt a statute or enact a law. It therefore concluded that the balanced budget initiative was invalid as a whole.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ commanding respondents state officials not to take action to place the proposed balanced budget initiative on the November 6, 1984, general election ballot because it found that Article V of the United States Constitution provided for applications by the legislatures of two-thirds of the several states, not by the people through the initiative, and the measure exceeded initiative power.

 

183 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Olsen


Crim. No. 23510


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 3d 638; 685 P.2d 52; 205 Cal. Rptr. 492; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 207



August 23, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 80443, James A. Wright, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court properly rejected appellant's claim that his good faith, reasonable mistake as to the victim's age was a defense to a lewd or lascivious conduct charge with a child under 14 years of age. Accordingly, the judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) convicting him of lewd or lascivious conduct with a child under the age of 14 years, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of lewd of lascivious conduct with a child who was 13 years and 10 months old, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288(a). Section 288 prohibits lewd or lascivious acts with a child under 14. On appeal, the court rejected defendant's good faith, reasonable mistake as to the victim's age defense. The language in Hernandez, together with the reasoning in Tober, Toliver, and Guiterrez compelled this conclusion. Cal. Penal Code § 1203.066, which rendered eligible for probation certain persons convicted of lewd or lascivious conduct who honestly and reasonably believed the victim was 14 or older, strongly indicated that the legislature did not intend such a defense to a § 288 charge. Section 288 and several other penal statutes reflected the strong public policy to protect children under age 14 from lewd or lascivious conduct.


OUTCOME: The conviction of lewd or lascivious conduct with a child under the age of 14 years was affirmed because good faith, reasonable mistake as to the victim's age was not a defense.

People v. Garcia


Crim. No. 22799


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 3d 539; 684 P.2d 826; 205 Cal. Rptr. 265; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 202



August 6, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 29, 1984, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Bird, C. J., and Lucas, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.
Writ of certiorari denied California v. Garcia, 469 U.S. 1229, 84 L. Ed. 2d 366, 105 S. Ct. 1229, 1985 U.S. LEXIS 1116 (1985)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 14923, Lawrence Storch, Judge.
People v. Garcia, 134 Cal. App. 3d 106, 184 Cal. Rptr. 353, 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1839 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., 1982)



DISPOSITION: The judgment convicting defendant of murder and attempted robbery is affirmed, the finding of a special circumstance under Penal Code section 190.2, subdivision (a)(17) is set aside, and the cause is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), entered upon a jury verdict, convicting him of attempted robbery and first-degree murder, with the special circumstance of felony murder, upon evidence of a confession and following the failure of the trial court to instruct the jury on the issue of intent as an element of the special circumstance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with attempted robbery and first-degree murder, with the special circumstance of felony murder, and his motion to suppress a confession as being induced by an implied promise of leniency was denied. At trial, evidence was presented that defendant had driven the get-away car during the robbery of a liquor store while his nephew robbed the store and fatally shot the store clerk. The jury was not instructed that felony-murder special circumstances required an intent to kill or to aid in a killing. Defendant was convicted and he appealed. In affirming the convictions, the court ruled that the statements by police to which defendant objected did not constitute an offer of leniency and he was not improperly induced to confess. In reversing the special circumstance finding, the court ruled that recent precedent, which held that proof of intent to kill or to aid a killing was essential to a finding of felony-murder special circumstances, had retroactive application. The failure to instruct the jury on the issue of intent as an element of the special circumstance violated due process and was per se error because it took an essential element from the jury.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed because defendant's confession was not induced by an implied promise of leniency. The special circumstance finding was reversed and the case remanded for retrial on that issue because the failure to give a jury instruction on intent to kill as an element of the felony murder special circumstance, as to take that issue from the jury, violated due process of law and was per se reversible error.
