Williams v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 31817


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 3d 441; 683 P.2d 699; 204 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 197



July 16, 1984 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the trial court to set aside its order denying defendant's motion to sever and to enter an order granting said motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to direct the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to set aside its order denying his motion to sever one count of murder from the information charging him in connection with two shootings on the ground that the two shootings charged in the information took place more than nine months apart.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged by information with five serious felony offenses, including two murder counts. The charges arose out of two shooting incidents that took place nine months apart. There was no evidence linking the shootings together, and their only similarity was that petitioner was identified in both. The trial court denied petitioner's motion to sever one of the murder counts and order a separate trial on that count. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to direct the trial court to set aside its order. The court granted the petition and directed the trial court to set aside its order denying petitioner's motion to sever, holding that the offenses were not sufficiently similar to allow evidence of one incident to be admissible in the trial of the other and that petitioner showed that he would be prejudiced if the two incidents were tried together.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition and directed the trial court to set aside its order denying petitioner's motion to sever, holding that the evidence of each shooting incident would not be admissible to prove identity in the respective trial of the other because the two incidents did not bear the requisite similarity to each other. The court further held that petitioner demonstrated that he would be prejudiced by the joinder of the charges.

 

186 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



American Bank & Trust Co. v. Community Hospital


S.F. No. 24171


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 3d 359; 683 P.2d 670; 204 Cal. Rptr. 671; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 193; 41 A.L.R.4th 233



July 9, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 375491, David S. Lull, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The appeal from the order denying the motion for periodic payments is dismissed. (See fn. 4, ante.) On remand, the trial court shall reduce the judgment in accordance with the stipulation of the parties. (See fn. 5, ante.) In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hospital appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) entering a lump sum judgment in favor of plaintiff patient in the amount of the jury verdict for plaintiff's medical malpractice claim and denying defendant's motion for an order of periodic payments of future damages pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 667.7.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient asserted that Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 667.7 was unconstitutional because it violated due process of law, equal protection of law, and the right to a jury trial. The court disagreed and held that § 667.7, which involved the periodic payment of future damages in medical malpractice actions, was constitutional. Rejecting the claim that the statute violated due process by reducing the rights of a class without a corresponding quid pro quo, the court observed that plaintiff had no vested property right in a particular measure of damages, and that the legislature had broad authority to modify the scope and nature of such damages. The court further declared that the statute did not violate equal protection because the statute was a reform measure and the equal protection clause did not prohibit the legislature from implementing reform "one step at a time." Finally, the court declared that the provision was not an unconstitutional interference with the right to jury trial, such right being protected so long as there was no impairment of the substantial features of a jury trial by improper interference with the jury's decision.


OUTCOME: The appeal from the order denying the motion for periodic payments was dismissed. On remand, the trial court was ordered to reduce the judgment in accordance of the stipulations with the parties. Otherwise, the judgment was affirmed. The court held that the statutory provision for the periodic payment of future damages in a medical malpractice action was constitutional because it did not deny due process, equal protection, or trial by jury.

People v. Zimmerman


Crim. No. 21858


Supreme Court of California


36 Cal. 3d 154; 680 P.2d 776; 202 Cal. Rptr. 826; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 180



May 24, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 12, 1984. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. AO77363, Charles H. Woodmansee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his conviction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of two counts of burglary, rape, and two counts of first degree murder with special circumstances, and his mandatory sentence on the latter counts to life imprisonment without possibility of parole pursuant to former Cal. Penal Code §190.4(b), par. 2, on the grounds that it violated the cruel and unusual punishment clause of U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of burglary, rape, and first degree murder with special circumstances. The jury could not reach unanimity on punishment, so the trial court, following former Cal. Penal Code §190.4(b), par. 2, imposed a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. Defendant maintained that §190.4(b), par. 2 did not permit the sentencing authority to consider evidence of mitigating circumstances, and did not furnish specific guidelines to determine whether life imprisonment without possibility of parole was the appropriate penalty. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that U.S. Const. amend. VIII did not mandate an individualized sentence process in non-capital cases. The court found that defendant's sentence was not "final" and "irrevocable." The court held that "correcting or modifying mechanisms," such as the trial court's power to dismiss the special circumstances under Cal. Penal Code § 1385 or an appeal to the governor for commutation under Cal. Const. art. V, § 8, were available to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of burglary, rape, and first degree murder with special circumstances, and defendant's sentence of life imprisonment without parole because the Eighth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution did not mandate individualized sentence processes in non-capital cases and "correcting or modifying mechanisms" were available to defendant.

Santa Monica Pines, Ltd. v. Rent Control Board


L.A. Nos. 31615, 31616


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 858; 679 P.2d 27; 201 Cal. Rptr. 593; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 169



April 20, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 21, 1984. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. WEC 062153 and C 322806, Laurence J. Rittenband and Delbert E. Wong, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners of an apartment building sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which upheld respondent city rent control board's denial of appellants' claim of a vested right to remove apartments from the rental housing market by converting them to condominiums without obtaining a permit under the city's rent control law.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, owners of an apartment building, challenged the denial by respondent, city rent control board, of appellants' claim of a vested right to convert apartments to condominiums without obtaining a prior permit under the city's rent control law. Appellants contended that they acquired a vested right to complete the condominium conversion project, free of rent control, by allegedly expending over $ 40,000 following a tentative subdivision map approval of the project. The court affirmed, finding that the amount of money actually spent by appellants in reliance on the tentative map approval was inadequate to predicate a vested right to complete the conversion free of rent control. Appellants also contented that the removal permit requirement preempted the California Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66427.2. The court held that the provisions disclaimed any intention on the part of the legislature to limit by implication local power to regulate condominium conversion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against appellant owners of an apartment building and in favor of respondents, rent control board and others, denying appellants' claim to convert apartments to condominiums without a permit. The court held that the amount of money actually spent by appellants in reliance on the tentative map approval was inadequate to predicate a vested right to complete the conversion free of rent control.

People v. Harris


Crim. No. 21633

Appeal after remand at People v. Harris, 191 Cal. App. 3d 819, 236 Cal. Rptr. 680, 1987 Cal. App. LEXIS 1683 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., 1987)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of murder and imposed the death penalty. Defendant argued that the use of the voter registration list as the sole source for the jury pool failed to provide an accurate representation of a cross-section of the community and had deprived him of a fair trial.

190 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Hartzell v. Connell


L.A. No. 31701


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 899; 679 P.2d 35; 201 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 171



April 20, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 133394, L. Donald Boden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which rejected plaintiffs' claims that fees established by defendant public school authorities for the students' participation in extracurricular high school activities violated Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5, and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 5, § 350.


OVERVIEW: Defendant public school authorities established the charging of fees to high school students for participation in certain extracurricular activities. Plaintiff taxpayers filed for injunctive relief from the fee program on the ground that it violated the "free school" guarantee of Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5, and that it was barred by Cal. Code Regs. tit. 5, § 350. The trial court rejected plaintiffs' claims. Plaintiffs sought review, and the court reversed. The court held that all educational activities, curricular or extracurricular, offered to students fell within the free school guarantee, so that the imposition of the fees violated the guarantee. The court stated that a fee-waiver policy for needy students did not satisfy the guarantee's requirements, and that financial hardship of a school district was no defense to violation of the guarantee. The court held that Cal. Code Regs. tit. 5, § 350 also barred the fees and that in barring them it fell well within the California Board of Education's range of discretion.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying plaintiff taxpayers injunctive relief against defendant public school authorities' program of student fees for extracurricular high school activities was reversed because the activities fell within the California Constitution's "free school" guarantee.

 

191 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Smith


Crim. No. 22872


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 798; 678 P.2d 886; 201 Cal. Rptr. 311; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 166



April 16, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied In re Smith, 2009 Cal. App. LEXIS 325 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., Feb. 18, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 11181, Wickson R. Woolpert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it convicts defendant of second degree murder; in all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), in which she was convicted of second-degree murder, under Cal. Penal Code § 187, on a felony-murder theory in which the underlying felony was felony child abuse under Cal. Penal Code § 273a(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of felony child abuse under Cal. Penal Code § 273a(1), and of second-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187. The murder conviction was based on a felony-murder theory in which the underlying felony was the felony child abuse. Defendant appealed, and the court reversed the judgment insofar as it convicted defendant of second-degree murder. The court held that because the homicide was the result of assaultive child abuse, the underlying felony in the felony-murder theory was unquestionably an integral part of the homicide. Thus, the court held that the offense merged into the homicide and it was error to give the jury a felony-murder instruction. The court held that because the people could not show that no juror relied on the erroneous instruction as the sole basis for finding defendant guilty of murder, the error was prejudicial. The court held also that the felony child abuse statute, § 273a(1), was not void as unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for second-degree murder was reversed because defendant's commission of felony child abuse merged into the homicide for which defendant was convicted, and thus, could not be the underlying felony in a felony-murder theory.

 

192 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Slaughter


Crim. No. 22896


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 629; 677 P.2d 854; 200 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 158



March 22, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 71546, Martin N. Pulich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the motion to reinstate that portion of the complaint charging defendant with murder is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied its motion under Cal. Penal Code § 871.5 to reinstate a charge accusing defendant of murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder when his accomplice shot and killed a man as defendant waited in a car, acting as lookout. A magistrate dismissed the murder charge pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 871. The prosecution filed a motion in the trial court to compel reinstatement of the complaint pursuant to § 871.5, but the trial court denied the motion. The court reversed. The court noted that the role of the magistrate was not to decide whether the defendant had committed the crime, but only whether there was some rational ground for assuming the possibility that an offense had been committed and that the accused was guilty of it. The court adopted the standard of review that applied to a related statute, Cal. Penal Code § 739. Under that standard, when the magistrate made express findings of fact, the reviewing court was bound by such findings if supported by substantial evidence. But if the magistrate rendered no findings, the reviewing court could find the dismissal erroneous as a matter of law if the evidence provided a rational basis for believing that the defendant was guilty of the offense. The court held that, in the case at bar, the undisputed facts supported the charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision denying the prosecution's motion to reinstate the murder charge against defendant because, based upon the undisputed facts, a person of ordinary prudence would be led to entertain a strong suspicion of defendant's guilt.

 

193 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Warsaw v. Chicago Metallic Ceilings, Inc.


L. A. No. 31740


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 564; 676 P.2d 584; 199 Cal. Rptr. 773; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 154



March 5, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C303574, Carlos E. Velarde, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded respondent neighboring property owner a prescriptive easement over appellant's property and ordered appellant to dismantle and relocate a structure erected on appellant's property, but which interfered with respondent's use of his prescriptive easement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner sought review of a decision which awarded respondent neighboring property owner a prescriptive easement and ordered that appellant relocate a building that interfered with respondent's use of the easement. The lower court affirmed the trial court's decision. On appeal the court addressed whether respondent should be required to compensate appellant for the easement and the cost of relocating the structure. The court answered no and held that respondent had acquired a valid prescriptive easement by using a portion of appellant's property for the time required by statute and that to have respondent compensate appellant for the easement would contradict the long-standing intent of the statutes that encouraged the use of land rather than nonuse. The court also held that it would be within the discretion of a court of equity to order a party to compensate another for the relocation of a structure that encroached on an easement but not in this case because appellant willfully erected the structure with the knowledge that respondent had filed an action for a prescriptive easement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment which awarded respondent neighboring property owner a prescriptive easement over appellant property owner's land and ordered appellant to dismantle and relocate a structure that interfered with respondent's use of the prescriptive easement, because respondent had acquired a valid prescriptive easement and appellant's building, erected after litigation began, encroached on the easement.

 

194 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



County of L.A. v. Soto


L.A. No. 31678


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 483; 674 P.2d 750; 198 Cal. Rptr. 779; 1984 Cal. LEXIS 149



January 30, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petitions of Both Parties for a Rehearing were Denied March 29, 1984. Mosk, J., and Kaus, J. were of the Opinion that the Petitions should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D-971168, Philip M. Saeta, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed insofar as it holds that the 1978 judgment of paternity is invalid and on that ground vacates it. The matter is remanded to the superior court with directions to afford defendant a new hearing at which he may present evidence of his lack of knowledge of his rights at the time the agreement for entry of judgment was executed, of the effect of that knowledge on his decision to execute the agreement, and of his diligence in seeking to set aside the judgment entered pursuant to the agreement. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county challenged a judgment of the lower court (California). The trial court granted defendant putative father's motion to set aside a judgment entered pursuant to an agreement acknowledging paternity that had been executed in conformity with Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11476.1.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, putative father, executed an agreement for entry of judgment in conformity with Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11476.1 acknowledging he was the father of a child. The trial court set aside the judgment. The court concluded that the agreement was not void but could be set aside if defendant promptly challenged the judgment and established (1) that he was not advised before executing the agreement that he had a right to a trial on the question of paternity and ability to support, and (2) that he was unaware of his rights in this regard and would not have executed the agreement otherwise. The court found that at the hearing no evidence was offered of defendant's actual knowledge of his rights, of whether he would have executed the agreement had he had such knowledge, or of his diligence in attempting to set aside the judgment once he learned of his rights. The court reversed and found that the trial court ordered the judgment set aside on the basis of a record that established the first requirement, but not the second.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order vacating the judgment of paternity. The court remanded for a new hearing to determine defendant putative father's knowledge of his rights at the time the agreement for entry of judgment was executed, of the effect of that knowledge on his decision to execute the agreement, and of his diligence in seeking to set aside the judgment entered pursuant to the agreement.

 

195 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Ebersol v. Cowan


L.A. No. 31644


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 427; 673 P.2d 271; 197 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 276



December 30, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. SP50361, Jerome H. Berenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the trial court is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bus driver appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied her relief from the claim presentation requirement of Cal. Gov't Code § 945.4. Plaintiff argued that the trial court abused its discretion in concluding that her failure to timely file a claim against defendant superintendent was not due to mistake, inadvertence, surprise, or excusable neglect.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff bus driver was employed to drive mentally challenged persons to and from a work training facility. During one trip, plaintiff sought to calm down a rider who bit her wrist. Pain and swelling continued through numerous hospital visits until she was diagnosed with Volmann's contracture. During this time, plaintiff sought legal counsel through nine different attorneys who declined to represent her. Unknown to plaintiff, the 100-day limitation for filing a claim against defendant superintendent had expired. An attorney finally agreed to represent her, and through diligence found that defendant county ran the work training program. Because her claim was barred, she sought leave to file late, which was denied. The trial court similarly denied her claim. Plaintiff sought review arguing that the trial court abused its discretion. The court agreed, stating that the circumstances involved were excusable neglect. Plaintiff sought diligently to be represented by legal counsel. Once retained, the attorney diligently and vigorously represented her. Moreover, plaintiff had no reason to know the county's involvement or had any experience with claims for injuries.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment holding that it was an abuse of discretion to refuse plaintiff leave to file her claim late. The court found that plaintiff's failure to timely file was not occasioned by a lack of diligence on her part, but instead was due to excusable neglect because she sought to diligently pursue her claim under the circumstances.

Olson v. Cory


L.A. No. 31780


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 390; 673 P.2d 720; 197 Cal. Rptr. 843; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 274



December 30, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA000437, John A. Loomis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the trial court to vacate its order denying plaintiffs' motion for an order specifying that plaintiffs' right to interest is an issue without substantial controversy and to make a different order consistent with this opinion. The parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, judges and judicial pensioners, sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied plaintiffs' motion for a determination that their right to interest on retroactive salary increases and pension payments, which were previously awarded, was without substantial controversy.


OVERVIEW: In a previous California Supreme Court ruling plaintiffs, judges and judicial pensioners, were found entitled to retroactive salary increases in accordance with Cal. Const. art. VI, § 1, and retroactive pension increases in accordance with Cal. Gov't Code § 75000 et seq. Plaintiffs filed a motion under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 437(c) and requested an order to determine that there was no substantial controversy that plaintiffs were entitled to recover interest on the retroactive payments due. The trial court denied plaintiffs' motion, a non-appealable order. On appeal, the court treated the appeal as a petition for a writ of mandate. The court granted the writ of mandate and directed the trial court to vacate its previous order and to render a new order granting plaintiffs' the requested interest payments. The court found that plaintiffs were entitled to interest on the retroactive salary and pension payments pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 3287. The court also held that defendant governmental controllers were not prevented by § 3287 from paying interest on damages.


OUTCOME: The court issued a writ of mandate directing the trial court to vacate its previous order and to enter an order granting plaintiffs, judges and judicial pensioners, interest on retroactive salary increases and pension payments due.

 

197 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Fields


Crim. No. 21126


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 329; 673 P.2d 680; 197 Cal. Rptr. 803; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 273



December 29, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 27, 1984, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Richardson, J., * Participated therein. Bird, C. J., Mosk, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A346249, Bonnie Lee Martin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of robbery murder with the special circumstance of willful, deliberate, and premeditated murder during the commission of a robbery; robbery; kidnapping for robbery and forced copulation; kidnapping for robbery; rape; forcible oral copulation; and assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant was sentenced to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery murder, robbery, kidnapping, rape, forcible oral copulation, and assault with a deadly weapon. The jury found that the murder occurred during the commission of a robbery, found defendant was sane, and assessed the death penalty. On appeal, the court held that the exclusion of potential jurors based on their views on capital punishment did not deny defendant a representative jury. The prospective jurors were properly excluded because they would have voted against the death penalty regardless of the evidence. Defendant also questioned the sufficiency of the evidence as to the special circumstance of felony murder. The court held that there was sufficient evidence to show that the murder occurred during the commission of the robbery where defendant killed the victim to punish her for resisting the robbery and to prevent her from reporting that crime as part of a continuous transaction. The court rejected defendant's argument that he had an "antisocial personality" that constituted a form of insanity under the American Law Institute test and held that the test of insanity excluded abnormalities manifested only by repeated criminal acts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of first-degree murder with special circumstances and 13 other offenses and his death sentence. Jurors were not improperly excluded based upon their views on the death penalty. Defendant was not insane because his antisocial personality was not manifested in any manner other than by his repeated acts of criminal conduct.

 

198 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Burg v. Municipal Court


S.F. No. 24622


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 257; 673 P.2d 732; 198 Cal. Rptr. 145; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 269



December 22, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 19, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 507133, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), that denied his petition for a writ of prohibition that sought to compel respondent, Municipal Court for the Santa Clara County Judicial District of Santa Clara County, to grant a demurrer on the ground that Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b) gave constitutionally inadequate notice of the conduct proscribed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with driving while under the influence of alcohol under Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b). A chemical test revealed that he had a blood-alcohol content of 0.23 percent. The legal limit was 0.10 percent. Defendant demurred on the ground that § 23152(b) provided constitutionally inadequate notice of the conduct proscribed. Respondent, Municipal Court for the Santa Clara County Judicial District of Santa Clara County, overruled the demurrer. Defendant then sought writ of prohibition which was denied by the lower court. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that denied writ of prohibition because Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(b) provided adequate notice of the conduct proscribed and was not void for vagueness. The court found that the statute gave no discretion whatever to the police, and thus was not susceptible of arbitrary enforcement, and the very fact that a person had consumed a quantity of alcohol should have notified a person of ordinary intelligence that he was in jeopardy of violating the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that denied defendant's petition for a writ of prohibition to compel respondent, Municipal Court for the Santa Clara County Judicial District of Santa Clara County, to grant a demurrer on the ground that a drunk driving statute did not give constitutionally adequate notice of the conduct proscribed because the statute did give adequate notice.

 

199 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Carson Mobilehome Park Owners' Ass'n v. City of Carson


L.A. No. 31705


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 184; 672 P.2d 1297; 197 Cal. Rptr. 284; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 262



December 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 18, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-354785, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: This court concludes that the Carson ordinance is constitutional on its face. It is well settled that courts lack jurisdiction to enjoin the enforcement of a validly adopted constitutional ordinance. ( Conover v. Hall (1974) 11 Cal.3d 842, 849-850 [114 Cal.Rptr. 642, 523 P.2d 682]; Code Civ. Proc., § 526.) Accordingly, the trial court's order granting plaintiff's motion for a preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent association's motion for preliminary injunction enjoining application or enforcement of the ordinance adopted by city council that regulated rent increases and evictions in mobile home parks.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court erred when it determined that the ordinance adopted by respondent city that regulated rent increases and evictions in mobile home parks lacked sufficient standards to govern the Mobilehome Park Rental Review Board's (board) administration and a constitutionally adequate rent adjustment mechanism. The court held that the imposition of mobile home rent controls was a rational curative measure designed to counteract the ill effects of a shortage of mobile home spaces which had resulted in a low vacancy rate and rapidly rising space rents. The court held that just because the ordinance did not articulate a formula for determining just what constituted a just and reasonable return did not make it unconstitutional. The court concluded that that the ordinance's provision for adjusting maximum rents was constitutional because the board's time allowed for review and determination of rent increase applications was not excessive, and it was reasonably related to the legitimate governmental purpose of eliminating excessive rents.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of preliminary junction in favor of respondent association concluding that the ordinance was constitutional on its face because the imposition of mobile home rent controls was a rational curative measure designed to counteract the ill effects of a shortage of mobile home spaces.

 

200 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Carlos v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 31487


Supreme Court of California


35 Cal. 3d 131; 672 P.2d 862; 197 Cal. Rptr. 79; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 259



December 12, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Real Party in Interest for a Rehearing was Denied January 19, 1984.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to bar his trial on a felony murder special circumstance allegation filed pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2.


OVERVIEW: The court issued a writ of prohibition instructing the trial court not to conduct further proceedings as to a felony murder count. Defendant robbed a grocery store, after which a gunfight between defendant, defendant's co-perpetrator, and an off-duty deputy sheriff occurred. The co-perpetrator and the sheriff exchanged gunfire, resulting in the death of the sheriff's three-year old daughter. After the robbery, defendant left the scene in the getaway vehicle and returned to pick up the co-perpetrator. Defendant was charged with five counts, including felony murder with special circumstances pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2. On appeal, the court held that the count was not applicable to defendant as he did not have an intent to kill or aid in the commission of a killing. The court decided that, though the statute did not state an intent element, the word "intentionally" was to be construed to require an intent to kill element. The court held that legislative intent necessitated such an interpretation. The court reasoned that ambiguities in statutes were to be interpreted in favor of defendants and to avoid substantial questions of the statutes' constitutional validity.


OUTCOME: The court barred the trial court from further proceedings on the felony murder count with special circumstances on the grounds that an intent element was to be construed in the applicable statute.
